When I had completed this book for you, my sons, which is a part of medical science in its entirety and which I have made clear and explicit, I saw fit to complete it with this chapter which is a part of manual work.
For manual work is perfected in our country and our time, it has completely ceased to exist, so that its science is almost forgotten and its practice discontinued. And there only remains a little of it in the books of our predecessors, which has been corrupted by hands and mistaken by error and confusion until its meanings became obscure and its benefits distant.
So I saw fit to revive it, compose this chapter in the style of explanation and clarification and concision, and bring the designs of cautery irons and all other surgical instruments as it is part of further clarification and among what is most needed.
The reason why there is no skilled artisan in our time is because the art of medicine is long, and its practitioner ought to be trained beforehand in anatomy as described by Galen. So that he may be aware of the benefits, structures and temperaments of the organs, their connections and disconnections, as well as knowledge of bones, nerves, muscles; their number, exits, arteries - both pulsating and still, and where they emerge.
Hence said Hippocrates that doctors by name are many but in reality few, especially in the art of surgery; we have mentioned some of this at the beginning of this book.
Because whoever is not knowledgeable about what we have mentioned of anatomy, it will be inevitable that he falls into errors and kills people with them, as I have often witnessed among those who merely imagine themselves to possess this science without knowledge or understanding.
For I have seen an ignorant physician who made an incision on a cystic tumor in a woman's neck, thereby severing some of the carotid arteries, causing such profuse bleeding that the woman died before his very eyes.
I have also seen another physician who was overly hasty in removing a large stone from the bladder of an elderly man, and he did so by cutting away a piece of the bladder wall. The man died within three days, and although I was called upon to remove the stone, upon seeing its size and the patient's condition, I deemed it too risky.
I have also seen another physician who made a living from practicing medicine under the patronage of one of the leaders in our town.
There was a case of a young black boy with a fracture near his heel and an accompanying wound, and this physician, in his ignorance, hastily bound the fracture to the wound with bandages and splints too tightly, without allowing any ventilation for the wound.
He then left the boy to his own devices for several days, instructing him not to loosen the bindings until the boy's leg and foot had become severely swollen and he was on the brink of death.
I was called to attend him, and upon quickly loosening the bindings, he found relief from his pains, but damage had already been done to the limb, and I could do nothing to reverse it.
The decay continued to spread through the limb until he perished.
I have also seen another physician who incised a cancerous tumor, which later became ulcerated after several days, greatly worsening the patient's condition; this is because when cancer is purely of black bile, it should never be treated with the knife.
Except if it is in a member that can entirely be excised.
And so, my son, you should know that work done by hand is divided into two types: action accompanied by safety and action which in most cases results in damage.
I have cautioned you at every point where this book mentions procedures that are hazardous and risky, so you must be wary of them and refuse to perform them.
Lest the ignorant find a way to speak and criticize, take for yourself caution and vigilance, and for your patients, take gentleness and thoroughness.
Use the best approach that leads to safety and a commendable outcome, and avoid dangerous diseases that are difficult to heal.
Purify yourselves from anything that you fear will cast doubt upon your religion and your worldly affairs, for it is more enduring for your reputation and will raise your status in this world and the hereafter.
As Galen said in some of his advice, 'Do not treat the sick with evil, lest you become known as evil physicians.'
This chapter has been divided into three sections.
Section one.
The first section, on cauterization with fire and sharp medication, is categorized and organized from the head to the foot, including illustrations of instruments and cautery irons, as well as all that is required for work done by hand.
Section two.
Section two, on incisions, punctures, bloodletting, cupping, surgery, and arrow removal, is categorized and organized with illustrations of the instruments.
The third book.
In reduction, dislocation, treatment of stiffness and the like; arranged in an orderly manner from head to foot along with diagrams of instruments.
The first book.
On cauterization.
Before we describe the procedure, it is necessary to mention its benefits and drawbacks, as well as in which condition it should be employed.
Discussing the benefits and drawbacks of cauterization is a lengthy discourse, an intricate science and a subtle secret; several sages have spoken on this matter.
There is divergence of opinion among them; I have abridged their discussion for fear of prolixity.
Cauterization is generally beneficial for any unhealthy condition that involves an excess of humour, be it material or immaterial; this includes all but two conditions: the hot condition without material humour and the dry condition without material humour.
As for the dry condition with material humour, there is disagreement; some say that cauterization is beneficial in this case, while others argue to the contrary that it is not suitable for illnesses stemming from heat and dryness, because fire has a hot and dry nature.
It is impossible to treat an illness that is hot and dry with a remedy that has a hot, dry nature; those who argue against cauterization say that treating such illnesses with fire might be beneficial. When you add the human body with its moisture to the nature of fire, you render the human body cold.
And I say as he does, because experience has revealed this to me on numerous occasions; however, one should not presume such matters unless they are well-versed and practiced in the art of cauterization. He understood the variations in people's temperaments and the states of illnesses, their causes, symptoms, and duration.
As for the other temperaments, you need not fear them, especially in cases of cold, damp illnesses; all physicians agree upon this and have no disagreement regarding the benefit of cauterization in such cases.
Know, my sons, that one of the secrets to healing is cauterization with fire, and its superiority over caustic medicines; for fire is a single entity whose action does not extend beyond the part cauterized, nor does it harm any other connected organ, except in a minor way.
Whereas cauterization with caustic medicines may extend its action to other organs and could potentially cause illness that is difficult to treat, or even be fatal; fire, due to its nobility and the purity of its essence, will not do so unless there is an excess [of heat].
This has become clear to us through experience, due to our long service, attention to practice, and understanding of the facts.
For this reason, I have refrained from a lengthy discourse; were it not inappropriate for this book of mine, I would have presented to you a profound secret regarding fire and its action on the body, and how it eliminates illness - in philosophical, logical terms that are beyond your comprehension.
Know, my sons, that they [the physicians] have disagreed on the best time for cauterization; some say it is during the spring season.
I say that cauterization is beneficial at all times because the harm caused by time itself is outweighed by the benefits gained through cauterization. Especially if cauterization is for strong and urgent pains that cannot bear delay, due to the fear of a subsequent affliction which may be greater than the minor harm caused by time.
And do not assume, my sons, what the general public and ignorant physicians may believe, that cauterization which cures a certain disease will never recur and thus should be mandatory.
The matter is not as they have presumed, for cauterization is akin to a medication that alters the temperaments and dries up the humors which are the cause of pains.
However, cauterization is preferred over medication due to its swift success, potent efficacy, and powerful influence.
And it is possible for the disease to return at some point in time, depending on the temperament of the patient, the severity of their illness, and any underlying conditions present in their body due to dietary habits or other factors.
Unless the disease being treated with cauterization is a mild one, affecting a part of the body that has fewer humors and moistures. For instance, cauterizing a tooth to relieve pain may prevent the recurrence of that pain, especially in less severe cases.
And as for the common belief that cauterization is a last resort in medicine, this statement is correct but not in the way they intend it. They assume that no treatment can be effective after cauterization with medication or otherwise, which is not always true.
The meaning of the statement 'burning is the last resort in medicine' is that we, when we have used all forms of treatment for a disease and they are not effective, then as a final recourse we use cauterization, which proves successful. It is from this point that the expression 'burning is the last resort in medicine' has arisen. No, it does not mean what the public and many ignorant physicians believe.
The ancients have mentioned that cauterization with gold is better than iron, and they said this because of the moderation of gold and the nobility of its essence. They claim that the site of cauterization does not become ulcerated; however, this is not entirely true.
For I have tested this, and found that it only acts in such a manner on some bodies and not others; cauterization with gold is better than iron as they say, but when you heat the gold instrument over fire, it is difficult to determine how hot it has become due to the redness of the gold.
And because it quickly becomes cold, and if you increase the heat it will melt in the fire, which causes difficulty for the practitioner; therefore cauterization with iron is faster and more suitable to use, God willing.
I have organized this chapter on cauterization into sections, from the head to the foot, so that it is easier for the student to find what they need; God willing.
Chapter 1.
For the head, there is one cauterization site.
This cauterization is beneficial for an excess of moisture and coldness in the brain, which are causes of headache, frequent sneezing towards the eyes and ears, excessive sleeping, toothache, and sore throat. In summary, for every disease caused by coldness such as paralysis, epilepsy, stroke and their likes.
This cauterization is to first order the patient to purge and take purgative medication that purifies the head for three or four nights according to the strength of the patient, their age, and their habits.
Then you should order the patient to shave their head with a razor, then have them sit in front of you cross-legged with their hands on their chest.
Then place the base of your palm on their nose between their eyes, where your middle finger ends, mark that spot with ink.
Then heat the olive-shaped cautery tool, which is illustrated here:" [Illustration of an olive-shaped cautery iron]
Scrape the bone until a hole is made as big as a cumin seed or an oat grain, then lift your hand away, unless you need to repeat this with the same iron or another one if it has cooled down. Do this until you see what I've described to you in the bone, then take some salt, dissolve it in water and soak a piece of cotton in it, place it on the spot and leave it there for three days.
Then apply to it a piece of cotton soaked in butter, leave it there until the sizzling sound stops, then treat with the four-ingredient ointment until they are healed, God willing.
It has been said that the longer a wound remains open, expelling pus, the better and more beneficial it is.
Some have suggested cauterizing the skin down to the bone, holding the iron there until some of the bone's thickness burns away; then you strip off what has been burnt from the bone and treat it afterwards.
Others said one should cauterize extensively until it affects the bone strongly, so that something like a kernel or small bead falls from the bone. They claimed that this releases vapors from that spot and leaves the wound open for a long time.
Then it should be treated until it heals, and I do not see these two types of cauterization at all, except in some people on the path of risk. In my opinion, avoiding it is better. And farewell if the head becomes weak when its natural connection is broken, as we have observed in other body parts.
Especially if the patient's head is naturally weak, the first type of cauterization is safer and better in my view. I use this method and have been successful with it, God willing.
Chapter Two.
On cauterizing the head, as well.
If a patient has chronic pain in the head, and this persists for some time despite using various treatments like eyrarges, qogais, snuffs, ointments, and dressings—especially if they have already undergone one type of cauterization as described above without any benefit—then consider the following. If the patient's head is naturally strong in constitution, not weak, and they experience severe coldness, cauterize another spot just above that one. Then cauterize each corner of their head until the skin thins out, exposing the bone as described. Cauterize another spot at the back of their head, in an area known as 'the axe.' Be gentle with this one and do not expose the bone, for the patient will experience severe pain unlike any other cauterization on the head.
The iron used to cauterize the horns and the back of the head should be more delicate than that used for the middle of the head. This is its image.
Chapter 3.
In which acute (non-chronic) headache.
If a pain occurs in the half of the head with headache and this pain extends to the eye, purge the patient by using medicines that clarify the brain and use other treatments mentioned before for various illnesses. If these are not effective, cauterization is performed on two sides (of the head). It can be either by using a very strong medicine that burns or an iron (tool).
As for cauterization with a burning medicine, you should take one clove of garlic, peel it and cut its ends from both sides, then make an incision in the place of pain on the temple with a wide scalpel until there is enough room under the skin to accommodate the clove.
Insert it under the skin until it is no longer visible, then tightly pack it with a bandage and leave for about fifteen hours, after which you remove it (the garlic) along with the gauze and let the wound rest for two or three days before proceeding to the next steps.
Apply a piece of cotton soaked in butter until it becomes inflamed and treat it with ointment until it is healed, if God wills. Alternatively, you may use some of the burning medicines mentioned in chapter eighteen on burning medicines.
As for cauterization with an iron, it should be done as follows: heat the iron that looks like this (image).
And it is called the spiked cautery because its head is shaped like a nail, with some curvature and a small projection in the middle.
Then you place it on the painful spot, holding your hand and turning the rod a little at a time; the amount of skin burned should be half its thickness. You lift your hand so as not to burn the artery below and cause bleeding; then you soak a cotton pad in salt water and place it on the spot, leaving it for three days. Then you treat the cotton with lard, and then you treat it with ointment until it heals, God willing.
And if you wish, cauterize this headache with the blunt end of the rod, taking care not to cut the artery in this acute headache especially.
Chapter four.
Treatment of chronic headache.
If you treat the headache with what we have mentioned in the previous treatments and in the chapters on diseases, and it does not improve, and you see that the condition requires something beyond what we have mentioned of cauterization with medication or fire. You should heat the blunt end of the cauterizing rod until it is white hot; you mark a line on the painful spot that is half an inch or thereabouts long, and you leave your hand resting once as you pull it to cut through the artery and expose the bone.
You should be careful not to cauterize near the joint where the jaw moves during chewing, as you may burn the muscle or nerve that controls it, causing spasms.
And take care and beware of the hemorrhage from the artery you have cut, for in it lies great risk. Especially if you do not know what to do, or have no prior experience in this area; it is better to leave such work.
The treatment of accidental arterial hemorrhage will be mentioned in its appropriate chapter, if God wills; should you see a condition which is not addressed by this cauterization method.
If the patient's condition is tolerable, apply a cauterization to the center of the head as described previously; treat the wound until it heals (if God wills).
You may use the cauterization method described in the chapter on 'Ligation of Artery with Hot Iron and Knife', which is superior to this technique.
Chapter Five.
On cauterization for earaches.
If an earache occurs due to cold and is treated with purgatives and other methods mentioned previously, but the pain persists, heat up the iron called 'An-Nuqta', which looks like this: [image]
Then, after heating it up, apply the iron all around one ear (or both ears if necessary) in a circular motion. Keep the cauterization away from the base of the ear, marking its position with ink; apply ten dots or so to each ear, then treat the wounds until they heal (if God wills).
Chapter Six.
Regarding the treatment of dislocation.
The dislocations that are treated by cauterization are those caused by phlegm, as I mentioned in the sections on diseases.
And cauterization should be avoided for dislocations caused by dryness and nerve contraction.
When you have treated this type of dislocation with earpips, snuff, and gargles, and your treatment has not been effective, the patient should be cauterized three times. One at the base of the ear, the second just below his temple, and the third at the corner of his mouth.
Make sure your cauterization is on the opposite side of the affected area, because relaxation occurs on the side that appears healthy.
The cauterization process involves burning the patient with a hot iron, once just below the top of the ear and another on the temple; the length should be equivalent to that of your thumb.
Lower your cauterizing hand until it burns approximately half the thickness of the skin; this is what the cautery looks like, and it is a type of small knife that has been described before but is slightly more delicate, as you can see. The knife should be slightly thicker than usual, and then you treat the wound as described previously until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Seven
Regarding the treatment of chronic stroke.
If the stroke becomes chronic, you treat it as we have described previously; and if this is not effective, and there is no fever present in the patient, cauterize him four times. On each horn of his head a cauterization, and a cauterization in the middle of the head as mentioned, and a cauterization at the back of the head as previously explained.
The cauterization recipe as previously explained, and one may also cauterize with a cauterization on the stomach's mouth, which would be more effective, then treat it as previously mentioned.
Chapter eight.
In cauterization of forgetfulness.
For that which is caused by phlegm, the patient should first be given large doses of ayurvedic medicines and brain-cleansing grains.
Then his entire head should be shaved, and a poultice of mustard, as written in the article on poultices, is applied to the back of his head for several applications; this is a form of cauterization.
Do this on the same rank as mentioned there, and if he is cured thus, then it is well; otherwise cauterize him three times at the back of his head in a straight line from the top of the head to the nape of the neck.
Let there be the breadth of a finger between each cauterization, then treat the cauterizations as previously explained; if you wish to increase it and the patient is able to bear it, then cauterize him on the middle spot.
If you wish to increase it further, then cauterize him on the horns, and treat him until he is cured; the cauterizing iron should be olive-shaped as previously described.
Chapter nine.
In cauterization for paralysis and relaxation of the entire body.
You should first proceed with the purification of the head using auras, as mentioned previously. Then shave the patient's head and cauterize it in the center, on each side towards the back, and at the nape of the neck.
Cauterize three times on the vertebrae of the neck; if you need more for a case of body relaxation, and if the patient can withstand it and the disease is strong, then cauterize four times over the vertebrae in their back. Go to the extreme with cauterization, burning most of the skin; then lift your hand and treat it as previously described until it is healed, God willing. The cauterizing instrument should be olive-shaped.
Chapter Ten.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
The person with epilepsy should be cauterized if their seizures are caused by phlegm; first, the brain must be purified using large auras and other treatments mentioned in the previous section, provided the patient is an adult and can tolerate medication.
For a child who cannot tolerate medication, gargles and chewed medicines that purify the brain should be used many days in advance while improving their diet.
Then shave their head, then cauterize once in the center of the head as previously described and another at the nape. If the patient is strong and can tolerate it, cauterize as mentioned for cases of facial paralysis and body relaxation on the vertebrae in the neck and back; use an olive-shaped instrument as previously described.
If the patient is a child, make the cautery instrument fine and delicate like this.
Chapter Eleven.
In cauterizing melancholy (i.e., treating melancholia by cautery).
If the cause of melancholia is corrupt humours and thick phlegm, apply the same cautery techniques we mentioned for the patient with hemiplegia.
If the cause of melancholia is an excess leaning towards black bile, and the patient's body is moist, administer a purgative to cleanse the brain as mentioned previously in the classification (i.e., the section on melancholy in the textbook).
Then shave the patient's head, create a tight cake-like pad from linen, shaped like a circle, and place it on the top of their head while they sit cross-legged holding [the pad] from each side. Then take one pound of old sheep's butter (i.e., ghee).
Heat it over a fire to a moderate temperature, as hot as the finger can bear when inserted into it, then empty [the ghee] in the circle on top of their head and leave it until cooled. Do this for the patient once per week along with all other good management until they recover, God willing.
And if you wish, you may cauterize with many small punctate marks without holding the cautery instrument in your hand but instead keeping it close (to the patient's body); this type of cautery moistens the brain moderately.
Then place upon it a piece of cotton wool soaked in ghee or chicken fat (i.e., apply this topically to the cauterized area).
Chapter Twelve.
On the cauterization of a water cyst in the eye.
If it becomes apparent to you that there is the onset of a water cyst in the eye, with the signs mentioned previously, then treat the patient promptly by cleaning his head and protecting him from all moisture. Make him sweat in the bathhouse before breakfast for several days.
Then ask him to shave his head and cauterize one spot in the middle of the skull. Then cauterize two spots on the temples, whether there is a water cyst in both eyes or only one eye.
Then cauterize all the veins and arteries under the skin with the iron, making the cauterizations long and broad across both temples. Be careful to avoid excessive bleeding.
If you see any bleeding, stop it immediately with whatever treatment is available to you. We will discuss the details of extracting and ligating arteries while avoiding hemorrhage in a later chapter.
Two intense cauterizations may be applied to the nape of the neck, below the two bones.
Chapter 13.
On the cauterization of chronic tearing.
If the tearing from the eye is chronic and continuous, and it originates from the veins and arteries on the outside of the head, and you ascertain that this is due to an excess of cold, thick, phlegmatic humor, then cauterize exactly as I described in the previous chapter on the onset of a water cyst. Cauterize one spot in the middle of the skull, two spots on the temples, and two spots at the nape of the neck below the two bones. If further treatment is required, cauterize one spot on each side near the tail of the eye, at the edge of the eyebrow, with a small iron.
Chapter Fourteen.
In cauterizing the nose.
If you treat it as mentioned in the division and the treatment is not effective, then take the patient to an isolated place for three nights, then shave his head and cauterize the middle spot with an oily iron. Then cauterize with the screwed iron two spots above the eyebrows, slightly under the hair, and be careful not to cut into the artery. This is an image of the screwed iron.
Chapter Fifteen.
In relaxing the eyelid through cauterization.
If the eyelid relaxes due to illness or moisture, cauterize the eyelid with one spot using this crescent iron.
If you wish, cauterize slightly above the eyebrows with two spots on each side, away from the temples, and make each spot as long as the eyebrow.
Do not exaggerate with your hand in cauterizing, just enough to burn a third of the skin, and this is an image of how the iron should look like.
Chapter Sixteen.
In cauterizing the eyelid.
If the eyelashes turn inwards and irritate the eye, cauterization is of two types. One method is cauterizing with fire, and for this, the patient should be instructed to stop plucking their eyelashes beforehand so they can grow long and even.
If you pluck it at its root, bind his eyes with a bandage so that they do not move until it grows back. Once it has regrown and healed, place the patient's head in your lap, then mark on the eyelid with ink a sign shaped like a bay leaf.
The mark should start near the lashes, then place under the eyelid cotton soaked in egg white or in seed of psyllium juice, then heat this iron as shown [in the illustration].
Then cauterize according to the shape marked, little by little in many sessions, until the entire surface of the skin is burned, which will be like a bay leaf, especially outwardly. The sign of success is seeing that the eyelid has been retracted and the hair raised from the eye muscle.
Then raise your hand and leave it for three days, then apply cotton soaked in lard until the scab comes off. Then treat with ointment until it heals, God willing. If any hair returns after some time and the eyelid becomes slack, repeat this process.
Repeat the cauterization on that spot as you did first. If the hair is in the lower eyelid, remove it until it regrows to its natural position and does not irritate the eye. As for chemical cauterization, this is when you instruct the patient to let their lashes grow long and even.
Then make a paper pattern of a bay leaf and take one dram or so each of common soap and quicklime, then grind both together well. Do this quickly to prevent drying. Then spread it over the paper you made in a bay leaf shape, placing this on one or both eyelids and putting under the eye cotton soaked in egg white, with the patient's head in your lap.
Place your index finger over the medicine and squeeze it slightly as you move it whenever the patient feels a stinging sensation from the medicine, for he will feel a sting like fire. As long as he feels the sting, leave the medicine in place and continue moving it with your finger. Once the stinging stops, remove the medicine and rinse the eye with water.
Look, if you see the eyelid has risen as it does when exposed to fire or cut open, otherwise, apply more medicine to the area that was not affected by the medicine and did not turn black until your work is complete and the eye has healed. Then place cotton with butter on it until the scab from the burn falls off.
Then treat it with palm oil ointment or another similar one until it heals. When working, you should be extremely careful to ensure that none of the medicine falls into the eye. If after a few days the eyelid becomes relaxed and there is a chance that something might have gotten in the eye...
Reapply the medicine to that relaxed area of the eyelid, as you did initially, then treat it until it heals. This is an image of a bay leaf.
Be aware that people's eyes can vary in size, some being smaller and others larger. Base your treatment on this variation accordingly. For those with experience in this field, the correct approach will be obvious.
Chapter Seventeen.
On cauterizing the fistula which occurs in the corners of the eye.
If you treat the fistula as mentioned in the previous chapters on diseases, and your treatment is not effective, it should be cauterized in this manner.
Instruct the patient to place their head in your lap, and have a servant hold their head between your hands firmly enough that it does not move or shake.
Then place a cotton pad soaked in egg white or the saliva of berberis on their eyes, then heat the iron depicted below.
It should be hollow like the quill of an eagle's feather on one end, where the cauterizing is done. If you wish, the other end can be perforated, and if you prefer, it could also be solid like a pencil, though this hollow one is better for your work, God willing.
Then squeeze the fistula if it is open, and drain any pus from it; dry it thoroughly or open it up if it is closed, extracting all its pus. Then place the red-hot iron on it, holding it with your hand until you reach bone.
Move your hand slightly away from the eye towards the nose when cauterizing, to avoid any mistakes or disturbing the patient, lest the iron fall into the fleshy part of the eye and damage it. If you reach bone in the first pass, well and good; otherwise, reapply the hot iron a second time if needed.
Leave it for three days, then apply a cotton pad with butter and treat it with the drying ointment until it heals. If after forty days there is still no healing, apply caustic medicine to expose bone; denude it according to what will be mentioned later in its section, God willing.
An alternative method for cauterizing fistulas, as mentioned by some early authorities: Incise the fistula site and place within this incision a thin funnel like this one.
Pour in it the weight of a dirham of melted lead, and hold your hand over the funnel tightly bound; do not allow the sick person to move at all lest the molten lead flow into his eye; you should place on the patient's eye some cotton moistened with egg white or water.
For the lead will burn and heal the fistula in a wonderful way; if the fistula is healed by the cauterization and treatment mentioned, then no other course must be taken except to use perforation of the nose, and return the fistula to the course of the nose as shall come in its more specific place.
Chapter 18.
On the cauterization of cleft lip.
A cleft called 'al-sha'rah' frequently occurs in the lips, especially those of boys; if you treat this with what we have mentioned under its section and it does not work, then heat a small lancet-shaped cautery like this:
Its hollow should be as thin as a knife, then put it on the wound while hot with quick action so that the cauterization reaches the depth of the cleft; then treat it with qirouti until it heals God willing.
Chapter 19.
On the cauterization of fistula occurring in the mouth.
If a swelling occurs at the root of the gums, or on the palate, or at the roots of the molars, and it discharges pus and then bursts, leaving as its result a fistula; if you treat this and the treatment is not effective, then heat a cautery of such size as will fill the fistula.
Then insert it, hot, into the fistula opening and hold your hand until the iron is heated to its depths and extremity. Do this once or twice; then treat it as we have mentioned above, until it is healed, God willing.
If the medicine stops flowing and it heals, then that is fine; but if not, you must examine the place and remove the decayed bone as will be explained later, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
In extraction of loose molars and gums.
If the gums become loose due to moisture, and the molars move, treat them with medicine; if it is not effective, then place the patient's head in your lap, then heat the cautery iron - as shown in the following image - after placing the tube on the molar.
Insert the hot iron with the wheel, and hold your hand for a little while until the patient feels the heat of the fire reaching the root of the molar; then lift your hand and reinsert the iron as many times as you wish; afterwards, have the patient rinse their mouth with salt water.
Let the patient hold it in their mouth for an hour, then spit it out; the loose molars will become fixed and the relaxed gums tightened, drying up the harmful moisture.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In cauterization for toothache.
If the toothache is due to cold or worms, and treatment with medicine is not effective, cauterization can be performed in two ways: either with butter or with fire.
To cauterize with butter, you should take cow's butter and boil it in a metal ladle or shell, then take cotton, wrap it around the end of a rod, dip it into the boiling butter, place it on the aching tooth swiftly, and hold it there until it cools down.
Then repeat the process several times until the heat reaches the root of the tooth; or, if you wish, dip a piece of wool or cotton in cold butter and place it on the aching tooth, then put a heated iron above it until the heat reaches the bottom of the tooth.
Alternatively, cauterization with fire can be done using a copper or iron pipe, which should have some thickness to prevent the heat from reaching the patient's mouth; then, heat up the cautery tool as shown in the illustration and place it on the same tooth, holding your hand there until the tool cools down. Repeat this process several times.
The pain should subside, either on the same day or the next; afterward, the patient should fill their mouth with good-quality butter and hold it for an hour before spitting it out. This is a depiction of the cautery tool.
Cauterize using whichever end you prefer, as appropriate; this is an illustration of the pipe.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On the cauterization of warts.
If warts are caused by phlegm and cold moisture, and they do not respond to medication but require quick maturation, then heat up the hollow cautery tool with openings at both ends, as shown in this illustration.
To draw out the smoke during cauterization from the other end, place it over the same swelling once and again if necessary until you reach its depth; should the swelling be small then make the iron to fit that swelling.
Then leave it for three days and apply a lint soaked in suet until the part burned by fire is cured, then treat it with ointment and pledgets so that it may heal God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
On cauterization for hoarseness of voice and shortness of breath.
If phlegm predominates in the pulmonary artery, especially if this is accompanied by a cold constitution, the patient ought first to be evacuated by cathartics and then cauterized at the nape of the neck where the gullet ends.
Take care not to reach the gullet with your cauterization nor burn more than half of the skin, then cauterize it again at the articulation of the neck just below its lowest point most thoroughly; and let the iron be pyramidal in shape as previously described.
Then treat him with what has been mentioned until he heals God willing.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On cauterization for pulmonary disease and cough.
If cough and pulmonary disease are owing to cold phlegm, if the patient has no fever nor consumption, and his complaint is chronic in character; then cauterize him twice above the clavicle at its lowest, softest, and least prominent part, and once more over the sternum between the breasts.
The cautery should be nail-shaped as previously described, and if you wish your cauterization to be dotted, use the cautery known as 'the dot', whose shape has already been given for dotting earache. The dots may range from thirty to about that number.
Then treat it as previously mentioned until it heals. You may make a cautery with three prongs in this shape, which will hasten the cauterization because you can cauterize three spots at once. This is its shape.
Chapter Twenty-Five
On cauterizing the armpit.
If the head of the humerus dislocates due to slippery moisture, or if it is not fixed when reduced so that it becomes habitual and then dislocates again at the slightest movement, as we have seen it happen, the jaw should first be opened. Then have the patient lie on his back or on his right side, and lift up the skin inside the armpit with your left hand if the joint dislocates inward.
Then heat up the cautery with two prongs, which is shaped like this.
Then cauterize the skin with it until it penetrates to the other side, and the shape of the cauterization will be four dots; you may also cauterize with a three-pronged instrument, in which case the shape of the cauterization will be six dots. The prongs should be as thin as veins.
This is its shape.
One may add one to this number, making the cauterizations eight; then apply ground turnips with salt on top of the cauterization site. The patient must remain still and not move the affected limb for a period until it heals. This is particularly important when the dislocation is upward, which is rare.
Apply a single, good, nail-like cauterization over the shoulder, or multiple small dot-like cauterizations. This will strengthen the joint, dry out any moisture, and heal the patient.
Chapter 26.
On the cauterization of the stomach.
If the stomach becomes cold and moist, deviating from its normal state, with frequent vomiting, and treatment is ineffective, then the patient should lie on their back, stretching out their legs and arms. Then, three cauterizations should be applied. Cauterize below the manubrium of the sternum, approximately one finger's width, using a nail-like cautery instrument. Apply two additional cauterizations below and to either side of the first, forming a triangle shape with the three cauterizations. Leave some distance between them so that if they become ulcerated they do not join together. The depth of the cauterization should be approximately two thirds of the thickness of the skin, and the shape should be uniform across the three cauterizations.
In this manner, to this degree, with no more (than what has been described).
If you wish, cauterize with one large cauterization in the middle of the stomach, following this same method.
The cautery instrument used for this single large cauterization should be circular in shape, as illustrated here.
The stomach may be cauterized with dots for those who are afraid of this type of cautery: mark the stomach with dots in the quantity you desire using ink, then cauterize it with a dotted cauterizer, and treat it with the treatment described previously until it heals.
Chapter 27.
In the cautery of the cold liver.
If there is pain in the liver due to cold, moisture, or excessive flatulence, and the patient has been treated with remedies mentioned previously without success... The patient should lie on their back, and three cauterization marks should be made with ink in this specific shape and size on the liver below the ribs where a person's elbow ends.
Each mark should be spaced apart by the width of a finger, and the cauterization should follow the length of the body in a straight line. Do not press down hard with the cautery iron.
The depth of the burn should be half the thickness of the skin, no more. The patient should stand on their feet; if unable to do so, they can lie down with legs extended and arms raised. This is an image of the cauterizer.
You may use a knife-like cauterizer to perform these cauterizations if you are skilled and careful in your technique, being mindful not to burn too deeply as the skin here is thin and you could damage the belly and intestines.
Chapter 28.
On the softening and reduction of liver swellings through cautery.
If there is an abscess in the liver and you want to know whether this swelling is in the liver flesh or its membrane, then if it is in the liver flesh, the patient will feel a heavy and painful sensation without sharpness.
And if it is in the liver's membrane, there will be severe pain with sharpness. And you see that doctors are unable to treat it, then the patient should lie on their back and the swollen area should be marked with ink.
Then heat the iron in the fire, this is an iron which is shaped like a millstone, here is its picture. And cauterize it with a single cautery using that iron until all the skin is burned and you end by cauterizing the membrane so that all the pus is drained, then treat it with abscess medication until it heals.
This type of cauterization should only be performed by those who have extensive experience in the medical field and have treated such diseases numerous times through practical experience. Then one can attempt this procedure, though abstaining from it is preferable.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
On cauterizing the navel.
The ancient ones mentioned cauterization using the root of the thorn bush for a cold navel using this method: take one long, dry root of the thorn bush that is as thick as your finger and longer than any others you may find. Then dip it in oil and light it on fire. Then cauterize with a single cautery between the collarbone and the neck, two small cauteries below the jugular veins slightly towards the area under the chin, and two large cauteries above the breasts between the third and fourth ribs.
Two cauterizations between the fifth and sixth ribs, slightly leaning backwards; another cauterization in the middle of the chest, and one above the stomach. Three more cauterizations from behind: one between the shoulders, and two on either side of the spine below the cauterization that is made between the palms.
The hand should not be inserted too deeply for cauterization; it should only reach the surface of the skin. Some ancient practitioners have mentioned that certain people would use an iron implement, like a needle or probe, which they would heat up and insert between the ribs until it reached the tumor.
Then the pus is drained as previously mentioned in liver tumors. And so, cauterization for this condition is a risky procedure; either the patient dies immediately or develops a fistula at the site that cannot be healed.
Chapter Thirty
Cauterization of the spleen.
If you treat an illness of the spleen with the aforementioned treatment methods, and it does not improve, then cauterization can be performed in three different ways, all of which are correct: one is to perform three or four cauterizations in a row along the length of the spleen.
They should be done in the same pattern as the cauterizations for liver conditions, with each one spaced apart by a finger's width or slightly more. The cauterization iron should have the same characteristics as mentioned earlier for liver cauterization, and again the hand should not be inserted too deeply. The patient should lie on their back during the procedure.
An alternative approach is at the elbow: you lift the skin opposite the spleen where the left elbow of the patient ends, and your lifting should be across the width of the body so that the cauterizations can be made along its length.
You then insert the needles very carefully until you pierce the skin on the other side, and then remove the cautery iron. This will result in four cauterizations. If you wish to use the other cautery iron with three needles instead, feel free to do so.
After that, you treat the cauterized area after leaving it to discharge pus for many days; this is more effective than all other treatments mentioned previously.
Chapter 31.
Regarding the cauterization of ascites.
Cauterization is particularly effective for ascites in the belly, especially if other treatments mentioned previously have not been successful. In this case, you should cauterize the patient four times around the navel and once on the stomach.
Another cauterization should be performed on the liver, one on the spleen and two behind their back between the scapulae, one opposite the chest and another opposite the stomach. The depth of the cauterizations should be close to the thickness of the skin. After this, you leave the cautery wounds open for a long time so they can discharge pus.
Do not leave the patient without any treatment after cauterization, as they should also receive other necessary treatments to promote healing. An illustration of the iron used for cauterizing the back resembles a nail, as described previously, while that used for the front is olive-shaped.
Chapter 32.
Regarding the cauterization of feet and legs.
If the feet and legs become swollen with yellow fluid in a patient suffering from ascites, you should cauterize on the back of the foot at the hollow between the little toe and the one next to it.
Hold your hand with the cautery iron and do not bend it, then lift your hand and never return it for there is a rush of yellow fluid, and this should be how the cauterization looks.
Then cauterize the legs twice on each leg with two burns, using the pointed end of the iron for this, with one burn below the knee and another lower down towards mid-calf; and to every thigh, two burns.
Leave the cauterization open without treatment for a long time, letting it discharge fluid, then treat with all that we have mentioned.
Chapter 33.
On cauterization for diarrhea.
If the diarrhea is due to cold and dampness, weakening the retentive and digestive powers in the stomach and intestines; despite treatment with all remedies it has not improved, but you see the patient can tolerate cauterization and is strong, then apply a large cautery burn on their abdomen. Following what has been said about cauterizing the abdomen, do this with a circular iron and four small burns around the navel using the small studded iron; one large burn over the coccyx or two if required.
If you see an excess of dampness and the patient can tolerate it, then cauterize with one burn over the pubic bone, one on each hip; perhaps add two small burns to the abdomen near the large one - this treatment is highly successful and will surely help God willing.
Chapter 34.
On the cauterization of hemorrhoids in the rectum.
If there are many chronic or single hemorrhoids in the rectum, and they consist of thick, cold, or corrupt humors, and treatment with what we mentioned in the classification is not effective, then let the patient undergo three cauterizations on the lowest part of the back, just below the waistline, triangularly.
A cauterization under the navel, about two fingers' width in size; and if you estimate that their stomach has cooled down and their food is not being digested properly, and you see their face becoming swollen, then cauterize them over the stomach with a large cauterization at what was previously mentioned, and another one on their liver. Another one on their spleen, with a cauterization iron shaped like a nail; leave the cauterized area open for some time before treating it to heal, God willing.
Chapter 35.
On the cauterization of warts.
After cutting the wart, heat up the cauterization iron that resembles a needle, then insert it while hot into the cut wart; press down with your hand until the iron reaches the mouth of the vein from which blood is flowing; do this once or twice.
If there are many warts, cauterize each one with a single cauterization as described above; then treat them with the appropriate ointment until they heal. If you use a larger cauterization on the cotton [poultice], it will be more beneficial.
Chapter 36.
On the suturing of fistulas that occur in the anal region and its vicinity.
If the patient does not respond to the incision and procedure described in its place, and fears it, perhaps cauterization might cure him/her. If there is a fistula that has persisted for some time with pus and foul fluids coming from it, you must first measure it with a fine probe.
Then, determine its depth by using the probe and then heat a cauterizing iron that resembles a pen. Insert it, while hot, into the same fistula in line with the fistula's depth and to the extent the probe entered. Repeat the cauterization until all of those foul bodies are burnt once or twice or thrice as required.
Take care not to burn a nerve if there is one, or a large vein, and if the fistula leads into the body of the bladder or that of the intestine.
So, be cautious of these areas; and only do this if the fistula is in a fleshy area, and you think it is not penetrable. Then treat that place until it heals completely. If the wound closes up, and the discharge stops for some time then it has healed perfectly.
And if the discharge does not stop, know that it is penetrable or there is a foul bone in its depth or something similar, which will be described later on.
Chapter 37.
On the cauterization of kidney stones.
If there is pain in the kidney due to cold or gas and intercourse decreases this, then you must cauterize it on both sides above the kidneys with a spiked iron described before; perhaps we cauterized it thrice over the same bone, so three cauterizations were performed in one line, which would be more beneficial.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
The opening of the bladder.
If the bladder becomes weak and relaxed due to cold and moisture, such that the patient is unable to urinate, cauterize it with a cautery in the lower region of the navel over the bladder where pubic hair begins; further, cauterize [the area] to the right of the navel as well as to its left.
The cauterization on each side should be about the size of a thumb's breadth, and you may cauterize [the area] in the lower back, one or two times if needed; and the cautery instrument should be pointed as mentioned previously.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
The opening of the womb.
If there is any material in the womb due to cold and moisture, preventing conception by corrupting its seed and hindering its flow, or if it causes pain upon arrival, then three cauteries should be applied around the navel as mentioned previously for bladder cauterization. A cautery on the coccyx below the back or two cauteries, and the instrument should be pointed.
Chapter Forty.
The opening for dislocation of the hip.
Mucus-like moisture may collect in the region of the hip, causing it to dislocate from its proper position; this is marked by one leg becoming longer than the other when measured against each other and you find an empty space at that spot; thus, the patient should be cauterized on the hip itself with a cautery in the shape of a semi-circle.
After learning to draw a circle with ink, position the right angle as it rotates to place the same right angle in the middle of the circle and create an image similar to that which you use for cauterizing the stomach; if this device is not available, perform three cauterizations using the large olive-shaped cautery iron.
Make the cauterization deep enough to match the thickness of all the skin, then treat it until it heals, God willing.
Chapter 41.
On cauterizing the sciatic nerve.
If there is pain in the right hip caused by cold and moisture, and you have treated it as mentioned earlier without any improvement, then it has become chronic; in this case, the patient should be eased of thick humours with colocynth or cactus seeds, etc.
Then cauterize, and the direction of the cautery can be of two types: either with caustic medications or with fire. The latter is performed in many ways; one way is to create three triangular cauterizations on the right hip as shown below.
The depth should be suitable, and the distance between each cauterization should be as thick as a finger. The iron used should be olive-shaped. You can also perform one cauterization in the middle of the head of the right hip; this would make four cauterizations total. If you wish, you can perform just one single cauterization using the circle mentioned earlier for dislocated hips.
The circle should surround the entire hip and burn through all layers of skin; this is how you cauterize for sciatica when there is constant pain in the hip itself but no extension to the thigh or leg.
If it extends to the thigh and leg, cauterize with two cauteries on the thigh at the spot where the patient indicates pain. Also, cauterize above the ankle four fingers away towards the outer side slightly. The cautery iron should be spatula-shaped; the depth of the cauterization should be equal to the thickness of the skin only.
If the patient indicates that the pain extends toward the toes, cauterize at the spot they indicate with a point cautery three or four times or more if needed. If the pain is indicated below the knee towards the leg, then cauterize there with one spatula-shaped cautery.
Be cautious in all your cauterizations to avoid reaching and burning any major nerve or artery, as this may cause severe harm or disability to the patient.
I have seen two cases where cauterization was performed above the ankle, and it was excessive, resulting in discolouration of the leg up to the toes and subsequent gangrene. This led to diarrhea and death afterward. So, if pain is felt on both sides, cauterize them following this very method.
Some renowned physicians have mentioned cauterizing the hip with a method like this: Create an iron cup-like tool, its diameter half a span, and at its mouth let it be as thick as a date stone or slightly less. Inside that cup, place another smaller one, and inside that, yet another.
Let the distance between each cup be as wide as the gap between your thumb and index finger, with both ends of the cups being open. Their height should be around one or two spans. A handle is made from iron, firmly attached to the cups; this is its diagram.
Then it is heated in the fire until it turns red and sparks fly, then it is placed on the patient's hip while they are leaning on their healthy side. It is cauterized with three round brands all at once; after which, they should be left alone for three days. After that period, one ought to apply some butter as a dressing, but the wound must remain open for several days.
Repeatedly treat it with ointment until healing occurs. The author of this book says that this type of cauterization is rarely used due to its ugliness and the horror of its appearance, as well as the few people who can bear it. However, for those who are able to endure it, it is an effective method of cauterization when applied to the appropriate location.
As for cauterization with caustic medications, you should make two small cups resembling the part of a door where the latch descends; these should be made from copper or iron.
Their edges should be raised by about two knots or so, and the distance between them should be equivalent to the thickness of a finger. They ought to be open at the bottom but closed at the top, with their handles joining each other in this fashion.
Then lower them onto the patient's hip while they lie on their healthy side, and squeeze your hand tightly. Pour some sharp water - which has been slightly warmed by fire - between the two circles, and hold it there for an hourglass' worth of time. The patient must endure its sting and burning sensation, as it will feel like fire.
Until the stinging sensation subsides, then lift your hand with the instrument and wipe all of the sharp water off the hip using fresh water. Leave it alone for three days, applying butter to it until the burn mark fades; leave it a few more days after that while pus flows from it - this is most beneficial.
Then treat it with ointments until it heals, but if the pain extends to the thigh or leg, make a cast for him that holds water, just as you did for the hip, alike. This is the description of sharp water, and I have proven it in an article on remedy reform.
Take a part of both soda ash and quicklime, grind them together, place them in a new pot with only one small hole at the bottom that fits a gimlet, placing another glazed pot under the first pot's base.
Pour fresh water over the soda ash and quicklime, covering them by a finger's width after firmly packing them with your hand. Let the pot sit until the caustic liquid drains into the glazed pot below, then gather all of that liquid and pour it over new batches of quicklime and soda ash.
By this point, it becomes very intensely caustic and is useful for many medical treatments as well as cauterizing all manner of body parts because it acts just like fire itself. Some medicines that can also be used to cauterize the hip are thorn apple, barberry honey, quicklime mixed with soap, and ointment.
Galen relates the story of an ancient man's treatment for hip pain, sciatica, and osteoporosis, which he greatly exaggerated as being a cure-all requiring only one application.
Even to the point of entering the bathhouse being carried in and leaving cured, it is said that one should take fresh turpentine resin, or failing that, newly dried sap, grind them finely with a little lard and apply this to the hip where the pain is, or on the leg or thigh.
The dressing is applied tightly and left on for about three hours, or until the patient feels that the burning sensation has subsided. Then they should enter a bath; once their body is sufficiently moistened, they should immerse themselves in a tub of water. The pain will go away and healing will occur, God willing. If no healing takes place, repeat the dressing after ten days.
It will heal, God willing. Know that no one should use cauterization with these medicines without first purging the body. Dioscorides has stated that goat dung, when used for cauterization on sciatica, is beneficial.
Cauterization is performed as follows: wool is soaked in old oil and placed on the deep area between the thumb of the hand and the wrist, closer to the latter. Then take a dry goat dung pellet.
Set it alight until it becomes a glowing ember, then place it on the wool and leave it there until it cools down. Repeat this process with another pellet, continuing to do so until the sensation reaches halfway up the arm towards the hip; the pain will subside, God willing. This type of cauterization is called 'bapri cautery'.
Chapter forty-two.
Regarding cauterization for back pain.
Back pain can arise from many causes, such as a fall, a blow, excessive purging, and so forth; it is caused by the pooling of cold, moist material in the back, for which cauterization is the sole treatment.
For pain caused by the pooling of cold material, after purging the patient with stinking sowbane or similar, cauterize their back where it hurts in three rows as wide as a table; mark the area first with ink. In each row, make five or more burns according to the tolerance and strength you perceive in the patient.
The cauterization is done with a pointy iron. If you wish, you can do three or four cauterizations with a medium-sized nail iron like this.
Chapter thirty-three.
On cauterization at the beginning of the hunch.
This illness often affects young children, and the first sign in a child is shortness of breath when they move around. You can also see that the last vertebra of their spine has risen higher than the rest.
If you see that, and want to stop it, cauterize them with an iron that is circular like this. The cauterization should be done on both sides of the vertebra, evenly. If you wish, you can do it around the vertebra with a pointy iron in two or three rows, with each dot close to the next one. Then treat the area until it heals as we mentioned.
Take care not to use cauterization for hunches that come from nerve tension.
Chapter forty-four.
On cauterization for gout and joint pain.
If joint pain comes from cold moisture that has gathered in any part of the body, and if it happens in the legs, doctors usually call this 'gout' specifically.
If cold gout is treated with the methods we mentioned in this section and the pain doesn't go away, then cauterization will remove it. You should cauterize around the leg joints many times after an emetic has been administered, using a medium-sized iron like an olive.
And if you need to cauterize a spot on the face, do so with an iron cautery tool. If pain rises up to the knees or other joints, and this often occurs, then brand each knee three or four times from either side with this very same oil-filled cautery iron.
If you require more cauterization, brand the area and do not go too deep with your iron; only as thick as the skin itself. If pain rises up to the hips or back, then use what we have mentioned before about cautery.
If the pain is only in the hands, cauterize around the forearms like you would draw two circles. If the pain remains in the fingers, brand each one and the palm of the hand. If the pain rises after a few days to the elbows or shoulders...
Brand them on either side, and the patient should continue good management and take medicines. If they improve their diet and remove phlegm, then with this branding, God willing, they will recover.
Chapter 45.
On the cauterization of hernias.
If a hernia appears in the groin, and some of the intestines and omentum descend into the scrotum while it is still at an early stage, then you should advise the patient to abstain from food that day and use laxatives to evacuate their bowels.
Then have them lie down in front of you on their back and tell them to bear down so that the omentum or intestines are extruded; then return it with your finger, after which mark below the hernia with ink onto the pubic bone a shape like half a circle whose edges point towards the upper body.
Then heat the iron, which has this form.
Until it becomes white, emitting sparks. Then the intestine or omentum is returned to its place inside the body. The assistant should put his hand over the spot to prevent the intestines from protruding, and I spread the patient's legs apart, placing a cushion underneath them while another assistant ties up their legs.
Another assistant is positioned on the patient's chest, holding their hands. Then you lower the iron to the same spot and press it down while standing upright with the iron held vertically in your hand until you reach the bone or return the iron once more if you do not reach the bone the first time.
Be careful to prevent the intestines from protruding while you are ironing, lest they be burned, which may cause death or great harm to the patient. Know that if you do not reach the bone with your ironing, your procedure will fail. The irons used for children should be suitable for their size, as should those for adults.
Then, after three days, treat the ironed spot with butter until the effect of the fire's crackling subsides. Afterwards, treat it with any other ointments until it is healed. The patient should remain lying on their back for forty days until the wound closes.
Their diet during treatment should include foods that are gentle on the stomach to prevent the intestines from protruding when straining for a bowel movement. If they wish to get up after the forty days, you should use a tight bandage and keep it on for another forty days. They should also reduce exertion, avoid becoming full from food or drink, and refrain from loud shouting.
If this treatment is administered in this manner, they will heal completely, God willing. I shall mention the treatment of ruptures by incision in its respective section, God willing. As for a rupture that occurs elsewhere in the abdomen and is just beginning, if you wish to prevent it from worsening, cauterize the rupture with a round iron suitable to its size. And if two-thirds of the skin is burned, treat it with what we have mentioned, and it will not increase, God willing. Some ancient authorities state that a hernia should be cauterized with a triangular cautery iron of this shape.
After having learned about hernia treatment with cautery, mark a line across the upper side of the hernia and another below it; then lower your hand in between them for one single cauterization, which is easier and better than the first, God willing.
Chapter 46.
Cauterization of fistulas.
If there is pain in some of the limbs following a fall or blow, and this pain persists for an extended period without responding to treatment with medication, then it should be cauterized with a point-cautery iron; each limb should be treated according to its size, smallness, weakness, and strength.
If the pain and fistula become entrenched, and it heals from the first cauterization, then repeat this process if needed; for such pains tend to spread from one limb to those close by, so you must follow them with cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Cauterization of leprosy.
As for the lepers, they may benefit greatly from cautery; especially those whose leprosy stems from putrefaction of phlegm and melancholic humor.
When cauterizing a leper, if the disease is in its early stages and you have treated it as mentioned previously without seeing deterioration or stagnation of the symptoms, and you fear that the corruption may spread throughout his constitution—then cauterize his head with five applications. The first is in the middle of the head, the usual one; the second below it towards the forehead at the end of the hairline; two on the temples; and another from behind at the occipital bone. Press your hand slightly with the cauterization iron until there is a slight effect on the bone.
And scales will come off, making it easier for the thick vapors to breathe; cauterize it also in the same way as the spleen, as mentioned before. However, if leprosy persists and becomes apparent, you should apply these cauterizations that we have mentioned on the head. Cauterize at the tip of the nose, two cauterizations on each cheek, two on the vertebrae of the neck, six on the vertebrae of the back, one large one on the coccyx near the base of the tail, another above it on the same plate, and two on each hip. Two on the knees, one on each knee; two on the shoulders; two on the elbows; two on the clavicles; and cauterize each joint of the fingers of his hands and feet once, as well as each heel of his feet and the forearms.
Be careful not to burn the nerves at the back of the heels; you may cauterize once on the pubic bone, another time on the stomach opening, and again on the liver. Know that the more you increase the number of cauterizations, the more beneficial and effective they will be.
Know that the patient will not feel pain from cauterization as much as a healthy person would, due to their body being numb. The size of the cauterization irons should be appropriate for the different parts and joints, as previously explained regarding the characteristics of the instruments.
Then treat the cauterization with fine caraway flour and honey along with other treatments until it heals, God willing.
Chapter forty-eight.
When cauterizing an anesthetized part, if a limb has been anesthetized and treated with medications, ointments, and dressings but fails to heal, burn the same anesthetized limb with multiple cauterizations according to the size or smallness of the limb. Let your cauterization be slightly more intense than the thickness of the skin.
You then treat it with ointment until it heals. Some cases of anesthesia that occur in the hand or foot, due to a spinal vertebra where the nerve exits and moves that limb, can be treated by cauterization, which eliminates the anesthesia.
No one should undertake such [procedures] without being well-versed in the anatomy of limbs and the exits of nerves that move the body.
Chapter forty-nine.
On the cauterization of leukoderma (white leprosy).
If leukoderma becomes chronic and no medical trick works on it, burn it with a cauterization that is slightly deep according to the thickness of the skin until the whiteness disappears and its color changes.
Then treat it with lentil flour, rose oil, asparagus leaves and dove blood or flycatcher blood, one part of each, mix all together, paint on a cloth and keep in place until it heals.
Chapter fifty.
On the cauterization of cancer.
If the cancer is in its early stage and you wish to stop it, cauterize it with a circular iron tool all around as if tracing a circle. Some sages have mentioned that one should use a powerful cautery technique at its center; however, I do not see this appropriate as I fear ulceration may occur, which has happened several times in my experience.
The correct approach is to cauterize it with a circle around its perimeter, as we mentioned earlier, or with multiple cautery applications.
Chapter 51.
On the cauterization of abscesses.
If an abscess occurs and its maturation is delayed, either due to the inherent strength of the infection or due to the patient's age if they are elderly with poor circulation, or even due to seasonal factors, and you wish to hasten its maturation, then cauterize the surrounding area with multiple small cautery applications.
Then leave it be, for it will accelerate its maturation. If you wish to slow down the process with cautery, heat up the iron tool, which is illustrated here: [image of an iron tool].
Place it in the middle of the abscess until it penetrates through the skin, and let the cautery be on the side closer to the ground so that the pus can easily drain out. Then treat it as necessary until it heals.
Chapter 52.
On the cauterization of gangrene.
Gangrene is a disease that spreads in the member, devouring it as fire devours dry wood. If you see gangrene in a place suitable for cauterization with fire, heat up many nail-like irons of various sizes according to what fits the location of the gangrene.
Then cut it from every side until all the corruption is removed and nothing remains of it at all, then leave it for three days and apply powdered sulfur with oil to the cauterized areas until all the scabbing and decay are gone.
Then treat it with salves that promote flesh growth, and if after three days you see the flesh growing healthily without corruption, leave it be; otherwise, repeat the cauterization of the remaining decayed areas. Gangrene can also be treated with caustic medication.
It acts as a substitute for fire, being more effective and faster-acting. I have already mentioned the treatment of gangrene with caustic medication in an earlier section; you can refer to that when needed.
Chapter 53.
On the cauterization of inverted and non-inverted nails.
This condition often occurs on the soles of the feet and is a rough, painful growth. Cauterization can be done in two ways: with fire or with caustic water.
Regarding cauterization with fire, you should use a hollow iron tool resembling an eagle's feather, made specifically to fit around the nail from all sides and with a thin edge. Heat this tool and place it on the nail, then rotate your hand holding the tool around the nail until it reaches its base.
Leave it for three days until pus forms, then apply a poultice of wild marjoram pounded with salt, leaving the poultice in place overnight. This will cause the nail to detach from its root. Treat the wound site with a flesh-growing salve until it heals; if the nails are not inverted.
These often occur on the surface of the body, especially on hands and legs. You should take a tube made of copper or iron or eagle's feathers, put it over the nail or wart, then pour into the tube some strong water in small amounts.
Hold your hand and rotate it slightly, while squeezing your hand a little to allow the edge of the tube to affect the base of the nail. The strong water will then find its way to penetrate down to the root of the nail, and the patient should bear with the stinging sensation caused by the strong water for an hour.
Then leave it, and the nail will come out along with its root. You can do this one by one until you have treated all those on your body. Then treat the affected areas afterward using ointments that help flesh grow.
Chapter 54.
On cauterizing shingles.
If a patient has shingles due to exposure to cold, or from quartan fever or other causes, you should cauterize it four or five times on the back, between each vertebra and once in their chest. And on the patient's stomach, cauterize once using an oily cautery instrument, as this will soothe shingles and accelerate healing from the cold disease.
Chapter 55.
On cauterizing boils occurring in the body.
There are certain ugly boils that erupt in the body due to cold, thick, spoiled matter. When they first appear, each boil should be cauterized gently on its head using a cinnamon stick whose end has been lit by fire or by long-stemmed asarabacca root, or by a lens-shaped cautery instrument.
Abscesses may be cauterized in their first eruption in this manner; the action of the agent that promotes them increases, and the patient recovers from them, although it is necessary that this should be done after the patient has been bled.
Chapter 66.
On the cauterization of an artery when it is severed.
Often, when an artery is severed by a wound from without or upon the incision of a tumour or cauterization of a member and such like, it is difficult to tie.
So, if this happens to anyone, quickly place your hand over the mouth of the artery with your index finger and squeeze it well until the blood is contained beneath your finger without any escaping; then take several small and large olive oil cauteries and blow upon them until they become very hot.
Then take one of these, whether small or large according to the wound and the place in which the artery is exposed; lower the cautery upon it after quickly removing your finger, holding the cautery until the blood ceases to flow.
If, upon lifting your finger from the mouth of the artery, it gushes forth and extinguishes the cautery, then take another one quickly from those prepared in the fire; continue doing so with one after the other until the bleeding ceases.
Take care not to burn any nerves that may be present, lest you inflict another injury upon the patient; know also that an artery from which blood flows cannot be tied off unless one of four methods is used: cauterization as we have said, or by amputation if it has not already been severed. When it is amputated, its ends contract and the blood flow ceases. It can be either tightly bound with threads or treated with medications that stop the bleeding and secured firmly with bandages.
And as for those who attempt to stop the bleeding with a bandage, by applying pressure with cloth, or using burning substances and the like, it will not cease at all, except very rarely. If this happens to someone and neither a doctor nor medication is present, they should quickly place their index finger on the wound itself, as we have described.
They should apply pressure firmly until the blood is restricted and flows away from the wound, towards the artery. The finger must not be removed by cold water, very cold indeed, always until the blood coagulates, thickens, and stops flowing. During this time, one should consider what it needs in terms of cauterization or medication.
Chapter Two.
On incisions, punctures, bloodletting, and other surgical procedures.
Khalf has said: 'We have mentioned in the first chapter every illness that can be treated with cauterization by fire and caustic medication, its causes and reasons, its instruments, and the shapes of cautery irons, arranging these in chapters from the head to the foot.'
And I will follow that same path in this chapter to make it easier for the student to find what they seek.
Before I begin, you should know, my sons, that this chapter contains even more uncertainty than the first one regarding cauterization.
For this reason, caution should be exercised to a greater degree because in this chapter there is often the loss of blood, which is the very foundation of life when an artery is opened or incised on a tumor, when puncturing and draining an abscess, treating wounds, removing arrows, incising for the extraction of stones, and similar procedures that are all accompanied by uncertainty and fear, and in most cases, death.
I advise you to avoid any situations that might cause suspicion, for in this profession of medicine and surgery you will encounter all kinds of people with various ailments; some may be weary of their illness and wish for death.
Due to the severity of his illness, and having endured a long period of suffering, some patients may have resigned themselves to death, indicated by certain signs in their condition. There are also those who will offer you money, hoping that your treatment can save them from an otherwise fatal disease; it is not appropriate for you to assist such patients at all.
Your caution and vigilance should be greater than your desire, and you should only proceed with treatment after absolute certainty of a favorable outcome. In all cases, ensure the patient is fully aware and warned about potential risks to their safety.
This will help you gain praise, glory, and reputation; may God guide you, my sons, and grant you wisdom and success, for it is in His hands alone.
I have organized this chapter with sections covering the entire range of cauterization, from the head to the foot, so that it is easier for you to find what you need. God willing.
Chapter One.
Treatment of the buildup of fluid in the heads of infants.
This illness often presents in infants at birth, due to excessive force being applied by the midwife during delivery; it may also occur from an unknown and hidden cause.
I have only seen this illness in infants, and in all cases, death was swift; for this reason, I advise against any treatment. In one case, the child's head filled with fluid and continued to grow larger each day. The boy could not bear to sit up by himself because of his large head and the increasing moisture until he perished.
And this moisture may gather either between the skin and bone or beneath the bone on the peritoneum. The procedure for this is that if the moisture is between the skin and bone, and the swelling is small, you should make an incision in the middle of the head across it, about two finger breadths long, so that the moisture can drain out. This is a picture of the scalpel.
If there is more moisture and the swelling is greater, make two intersecting incisions as shown in this picture:
