If the moisture is beneath the bone, indicated by open scalp sutures on all sides and water seeping inwards when you squeeze it with your hand, then this should not be difficult for you to identify. In such a case, you must make three incisions in the middle of the head as shown in this picture.
After the incisions are made, all the moisture will be released. Then you must close the incisions with cloth and bandages, then cover them from above with ointment and oil until the fifth day. Next, remove the bandage and treat the wound with astringents and ointments, making sure not to bind it too tightly. Finally, feed the patient dry food that contains little moisture.
Until the body part is strengthened and healed, here is another method of incision: you must look at where the bone of the swelling appears and where the water gathers because it may be more towards the back or front of the head, or on either side.
You must aim your incision at the point of swelling and where you see moisture accumulating, making it as precise as possible. Be careful not to cut an artery, causing a hemorrhage that may kill the patient due to blood loss along with the release of moisture.
Chapter two.
Ligate the two arteries behind the ears known as al-hisasan.
If anyone experiences an acute attack in the eyes or chest, and this becomes chronic and does not improve with medication, then the best treatment for it is to ligate these two arteries.
When you wish to ligate them, you should first shave the patient's head with a razor and then rub the area with a coarse cloth to make the artery visible. Next, you should tighten the patient's neck with their own garment and look for where the pulse beats and its two locations, which are the lower areas behind both ears; it is rarely hidden except in some people.
Then you should mark them with ink and cut them with a sharp scalpel all the way to the bone, doing so across the width of the head. If you wish, you may also insert the scalpel from underneath the artery and sever it by cutting upwards; the length of the cut should be about two fingers' breadth when they are held together.
When the artery is severed, blood will gush out in a pulsating manner, leaping forward and jumping repeatedly. If the artery is not palpable to the touch, you should estimate its location three fingers' breadth from the ear, mark it with ink, and then make an incision all the way to the bone. You must...
You will release about six ounces of blood, possibly more or less, depending on the patient's strength and the fullness of their arteries; you may judge this for yourself. Then examine the wound: if there is any peritoneum left over the bone, cut it away.
To prevent a hot swelling, you should bind the wound with a thread of linen cloth and then treat it with ointments until it heals.
Chapter 3.
Open the two arteries in the temples.
For a person who has chronic migraines or acute attacks of severe heat and moisture in the temples, or muscles, or severe headaches that do not respond to conventional medical treatments, we have found relief by opening the arteries in the temples or cauterizing them as described above.
The process of opening the arteries involves shaving the hair from the temples and identifying the visible artery, which is usually noticeable due to its pulsation; however, in rare cases, it may be hidden, except in very few individuals.
Alternatively, in extremely cold weather, if the artery is difficult to locate, the patient should tighten their scarf around their neck and then you can rub the area with a cloth or apply a hot compress until the artery becomes clearly visible. Then, take the scalpel which looks like this: [image]. Using the scalpel, carefully make an incision in the skin until you reach the artery; then, insert a hook and gently pull it upwards to extract it from beneath the skin; remove any membranes attached to either side of the artery, taking care not to damage it if it is thin by twisting the hook around its end. Cut a segment from the artery, ensuring its ends are separated and contracted to prevent bleeding; if it is not properly severed, there will be no blood loss; in this procedure, up to three or six ounces of blood may be drawn.
If the artery is large, it should be tied in two places with a strong double thread made of either silk or lute strings to prevent putrefaction before the wound heals and causes bleeding.
Then you cut off the excess between the two ligaments, doing this in that hour or after some time has passed; and if you wish to cauterize it down to the bone with a bidentate cautery until its ends are severed, it will serve the same purpose as this procedure or even better, as we said.
Unless the patient has a fever or is in a choleric condition, because cauterization helps to eliminate moisture and thus be even more beneficial; after ligating the arteries, the cavity should be packed with old cotton and secured with tight dressings, then treated with drying medications that stimulate flesh growth as well as bandaging until it heals, God willing.
If bleeding from the artery occurs during your operation, act quickly to ligate it either by cauterization or filling the cavity with vitriol and applying pressure until the blood flow stops; if neither of these are available, place your finger on the wound until the bleeding ceases.
Irrigate the area with very cold water to calm the inflammation and apply pressure as needed; a less invasive option than ligating the artery is to cauterize the vein with this bidentate instrument after marking the two points with ink.
Insert it very hot until you reach the bone and sever the vein in two places so that its ends are separated; this ensures that it never reconnects, for cauterization prevents reunion. This is an illustration of the instrument.
It has two sharp blades resembling small scissors, although much less sharp than a knife because if they were as keen, they would cool too quickly and fail to sever the flesh promptly; some bluntness is therefore desirable to retain heat.
Cut the flesh quickly; this action is better than any other and quicker and easier, let it be after the distance between the two knives the thickness of a finger.
Chapter 4.
On the treatment of hot, constant tears running into the eyes.
If the tears are constant and their flow originates from the veins on top of the skull externally, and no treatment with medicine provides any relief, and you observe that the patient's face has turned red. And they feel a tingling sensation in their forehead, like the crawling of ants, and their eyes are withered and moist; their eyelashes have deteriorated and their eyelids have become rough from the intensity of tears. The ancient physicians were compelled to perform this treatment, which they call 'treatment by sword'; it involves instructing the patient to shave the hair on their forehead.
Then you make three parallel incisions on the forehead, lengthwise; each incision should be about two finger-lengths long and should run parallel to the bridge of the nose in the middle of the forehead, while the second runs slightly beyond the movement of the muscle in the temple and the third from the other side.
Take care not to cut the arteries on both sides, and keep your hand away from the connection of the jaws; let there be a distance between each incision equal to three closed fingers. Have with you prepared sponges or many dry rags to soak up the blood.
Then insert the double-edged sharp knife, as shown in this picture: Starting from the incision next to the temple, you should use it to strip all the skin between that and the middle incisions along with the membrane on top of the bone; then do the same from the middle incision to the third one. Finally, remove this knife. Another instrument also enters through the first incision, called a sharp scalpel on one side and smooth, non-sharp on the other. This is its image: .
Its sharp side is directed upwards towards the meat attached to the skin and its smooth side towards the bone, and you push it until it reaches the middle incision and cut all vessels that descend from the head to the eyes without reaching the outer skin.
Then you do the same in the middle incision to the other incision, and after a moderate amount of blood has flowed out, you squeeze the locations where the blood is clotted, then go over each incision with a cotton swab and place on it a dressing that has been soaked in wine or vinegar mixed with oil.
To avoid the occurrence of a hot swelling, and on the third day you remove the bandage and use copious washing with warm water, then treat it with basilicon ointment, rose oil, and whatever else you usually treat wounds with until it heals, God willing.
Chapter Five.
Treatment of tears and discharges into the eyes from inside the head.
If a person has had many severe, burning discharges which are constant and the eyes appear emaciated and small, with their vision weakened, and the eyelids are inflamed with loss of eyelashes. And there is severe, painful pain in the depths of the head along with continuous sneezing then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep vessels; the best treatment for this is as follows: have the patient shave their forehead then make one incision in the middle of the forehead.
And there is severe, painful pain in the depths of the head along with continuous sneezing then know from these symptoms that those materials and discharges come from many deep vessels; the best treatment for this is as follows: have the patient shave their forehead then make one incision in the middle of the forehead. Or raise it slightly horizontally and begin the incision from the left temple to the right, extending down to the bone. Keep your hand away from the moving muscles of the temples during chewing until all blood has been dried up with sponges.
Separate the edges of the incision with old cotton or a piece of linen, then apply tension from above with bandages. Moisten the bandages with vinegar and oil to prevent hot swelling. Once you release it and see that hot swelling has recurred, you must scrape the bone until new flesh begins to grow.
Then treat it with a drying agent that promotes flesh growth, such as a salve made from two parts wheat flour and four parts galena, which is useful for promoting flesh growth in wounds like these.
Chapter Six.
On the treatment of objects that fall into the ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four types: either a metallic stone or something similar, like iron or glass; alternatively, it could be plant matter such as chickpeas or seeds; or finally, a liquid substance like water or vinegar.
And finally, it could be an animal. If a pebble or similar object falls into the ear and you know that it cannot grow there, expose the ear to sunlight. Then drip some violet oil or lamp oil onto the pebble, and try to remove it by moving your head or sneezing with musk. Close your nostrils when you sneeze after placing a cloth or woollen ring around the ear, and lift up the ear. This often aids in removal. If it still does not come out, try using gentle forceps like these to remove it.
If it comes out with forceps, otherwise try to remove it with a fine, flexible blind hook. If that does not work, make a copper tube and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole properly.
Plug the area around the tube with softened pitch mixed with oil so that there is no way for air to pass except through the tube, then blow hard and strongly into it; often it will come out this way. If it does not come out as described above, take a small amount of nabatean resin or trapping bird resin and place it on the end of the stylet.
After wrapping a tight piece of cotton around it, gently insert the stylet into the ear hole after drying the ear from moisture; if it does not come out with all that has been described above then proceed to incision before severe swelling or spasm occurs.
The incision treatment involves first bloodletting the patient at the cifal and extracting an amount of blood proportional to their strength, then sit the patient between your hands and turn their ear upwards, making a small incision in the root of the ear at its cartilaginous base where it is lowest.
Make the incision in a lunar shape until you reach the stone, then remove it with any instruments available to you; stitch the incision quickly and treat it until it heals.
If the object in the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, try to remove it with what has been mentioned; if it does not come out then take a fine, thin scalpel like this one.
Attempt to cut that type of grain fallen in the ear with it, and only do so when you are certain that the grain has absorbed moisture from the ear until it becomes many small pieces; then remove them with a blind hook or fine forceps or by suction.
As we mentioned, it is easy to remove them, and as for the water that enters the ear, the patient should use a sneezing kundas first after filling their ear with some old cotton while lying on the side with the affected ear; if it comes out like this.
Or, take many pebbles the size of fingers, thin and smooth; heat them slightly over a fire and let the patient insert one into the hole in their ear. They should hop on their other leg from that side while striking another pebble against the stone in their ear.
Continue doing this with one pebble after another until all the water comes out; or, the water may be extracted by taking a piece of papyrus or feather and inserting one end into the ear while lighting the other end on fire so that most of it burns up before reinserting another feather.
Do this several times until all the water is removed, or draw it out with a tube as described for pebbles; and if there's an animal inside, examine its size - if small like a flea or similar, treat it as mentioned in the classification; but if not...
If its body is large enough to be felt, try extracting it with forceps and hooks; this will prove easier than removing anything else that becomes stuck in the ear; however, if worms have developed inside and your treatment using the methods described for inflammation under "Irrigation" has been ineffective...
You should inspect the ear in sunlight; if you can see any worms, remove them with forceps or fine hooks; but if not, take a tube of this shape...[image follows]
Narrow at the bottom and wide at the top, insert the thin end into the ear as much as the patient can tolerate, then suck strongly. Do this several times until all the worms come out. If they do not comply with coming out, seal around the tube with wax as I mentioned to you in the case of stone.
If they do not come out with what we mentioned, use the drops I described that have been tried by previous doctors to kill worms. You will find this in the article on drops. Pour the oils and medicines into the ear using this instrument; here is its image:.
As you can see, make it from silver or copper, narrow at the bottom with a small hole and wide at the top. If you wish, the nozzle inside the tube can be made of tightly fitted copper; if you prefer, take a pencil and wrap some cotton tightly around one end.
Then put the oil or juice or whatever medicine you want into the tube while it is in the ear, then insert the pencil with cotton on top and squeeze it gently with your hand until the oil flows into the ear canal.
It goes into the patient; whatever you pour into the ear should be slightly warmed over a fire, and take care that what is dropped in it is not too cold or too hot, for the ear cannot tolerate this.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of temporary blockage in the ear.
Some children may be born with their ear canals unperforated.
In some people, a blockage may also occur in the ear canal due to an injury or tissue growth there. This obstruction might be deep within the ear canal and not visible.
If it is exposed just outside the hole and can be seen, then if what is exposed deep in the ear cannot be seen, in most cases healing will be difficult. If it can be seen, you should place the patient's ear in the sun. Look into it, if you see the blockage visible, then open it with a delicate scalpel like this one:
The end of it should be somewhat wider and slightly rounded, with the rest of the scalpel smooth on both sides to avoid harming the ear. If the blockage is due to flesh that has grown in the ear canal, grasp it gently with a fine hook and cut it off very carefully until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep inside the ear, take a fine and smooth probe, warm it slightly over a fire, then insert it into the ear canal. If you feel the blockage and the patient experiences a sensation of reduced hearing, gently rotate the probe.
Be careful not to injure the ear membrane, then place in the ear a wick of a size matching the hole, having first soaked it in Egyptian ointment; do this for several days until you are confident that the wound has healed or take a wick and soak it in water before sprinkling it with powdered zinc oxide.
Use this treatment, but if you see the ear becoming swollen with a hot sensation, it should be necessary to remove the wick and replace it with another that has been soaked in some alitrus made from rose oil until the hot swelling subsides; then treat it until healing occurs.
If there is any bleeding of blood, immerse a sponge or cloth in cold water and place it on the ear; use other treatments described in the section on cataplasms that stop such bleeding.
Chapter Eight.
In the treatment of warts that appear on the eyelids.
These warts that appear on the eyelids may be moist or dry. You should grasp them with forceps or a hook, and then cut them off at their roots with a scalpel, after which you should apply powdered zinc ore to the affected area.
If blood flows from the wound, cauterize it with a mild lentil-shaped iron. Cauterization after cutting is preferable. They often return if some of their roots remain; if you cauterize them with fire, you will burn those roots and they will not return.
Chapter Nine.
In the treatment of colds that appear in the eyelids.
It is common for something resembling a cold in intensity and hardness to appear on the eyelids.
This condition, known as a cold, is caused by the accumulation of thick moisture in both the upper and lower eyelids. If the lesion is raised on the surface of the eyelid and moves in all directions, it is relatively easy to treat; simply make an incision across it.
Gently peel it away from each side until it is released, then hook and cut it with a hook. If you cannot cut it without piercing the eyelid, this will not harm the patient; if the incision is large, simply stitch it together and treat it until it heals.
If the lesion is small, you need not worry; ointment will encourage it to heal and seal itself. If the lesion leans towards the interior surface of the eyelid, simply turn the eyelid inside out, hook the lesion without cutting it (as this is unnecessary), then cut it away from each side.
If you cut through the eyelid, it will not cause harm, then wash the eye after cutting the membrane with salty water and treat the area with something that will heal it until the patient is cured.
Chapter 10.
The treatment of the tumor that appears on the eyelid.
A tumor is a fatty tissue found in the layers of the upper eyelid, and it often affects boys, weighing down their eyes and making them susceptible to illnesses during the dawn hours. They are unable to look directly at sunlight. Because tears reach them quickly.
And for that reason, you see them constantly leaning on their faces and sleeping on them; their eyelids are under the eyebrows, moist with puffiness, and the tumor is visible to all. If you press the area between your fingers, you can feel the tumor between them. The procedure requires that the patient place their head in your lap.
You then take a piece of linen cloth and prepare from it a wick; you make the wick into a circle that covers the tumor on all sides, placing it over the area and pressing with your fingers from every side to gather the tumor in the center of the circle.
Then you make an incision in the middle of the moisture with a sharp scalpel, making it across and not larger than the typical venesection cut. However, the depth should be enough to cut through the entire layer of skin until you reach the tumor.
In most cases, the tumor will protrude from the incision in the form of a white fatty lump; you then extract it with a cloth wrapped around your fingers and pull it out while rotating your hand right and left until the patient is cured. Be cautious not to make the cut too deep so as to avoid damaging the scalpel and harming the eye.
If the swelling does not appear at the beginning of the incision, you should gently extend it a little until the swelling is exposed and then pull it out as we have mentioned before; after this, immerse some cloth in vinegar and water and place it on the area while applying pressure with a bandage. Some people prefer to crush salt instead.
Place it in the depth of the incision so that any remaining moisture dissolves, then treat it until it heals. If there is a hot swelling in the area, treat it with soothing dressings until it heals.
Chapter eleven.
On the types of squints.
If extra eyelashes grow on the eyelid in an unnatural direction, beneath the natural ones and persist, they can harm the eye and cause various ailments such as constant watering of the eyes, drooping eyelids, opacity and thickening, to the point that this may lead to blindness.
Squints are treated in four ways: either by cauterization with fire, or by using strong medicines as previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization, or by cutting and stitching, or by using canes, which I will now describe.
You should place the patient's head in your lap and then turn the eyelid over with your left hand. If it turns, all is well; otherwise, insert a needle with thread from beneath the eyelid, pierce through with the needle above, close to the hair itself, and pull the thread upwards along with the eyelid.
Turn it over with a smooth stick, then make an incision inside the eyelid, away from the excess hair, using a sharp knife starting at the larger margin and moving towards the smaller one. Then remove the thread and place beneath the eyelid a small dressing made of cotton or cloth; finally, mark the eyelid with ink.
It should be like the shape of an ace leaf, but it is necessary for the size to match your desired eyelid lift, because this may vary among people; some might need a considerable amount cut from their eyelids due to the level of relaxation.
Some others may need a smaller cut, depending on the level of eyelid relaxation; then you should make an incision with a scalpel along the two lines that you have marked, starting from the larger canthus to the smaller one, and ensure the incision is as thick as a makeup brush near the natural hair.
Next, insert the hook into one corner of the skin and then peel off all the layers; afterwards, bring together both lips by sewing them with a thin wool thread and needle, wipe away any remaining blood and stick down any loose threads on the eyebrows using some adhesive.
If you wish to do so, or otherwise it doesn't matter; the stitches and threads remain for around three or four days before you treat them, although if preferred you can leave the wound without any stitching and just treat it with a drying agent that causes constriction in the area, as this will lift the eyelid. However, stitching is preferable.
An alternative method of blepharoplasty that was mentioned by previous generations, although it is somewhat arduous for the patient; this is nevertheless an excellent approach with no associated risks. Another approach to consider is marking out a shape on the eyelid similar to that of an ace leaf, as previously described. Next you should lift the eyelid using three hooks, which may be either separate or grouped together in this fashion:
Then you should cut away any excess eyelid with small scissors, following this pattern but doing so cautiously:
If you cannot contain the tendons and they are not stable for you, take a needle with thread and insert it into the middle of the shape. Then, insert another thread near the larger angle and a third one near the smaller angle. Gather the threads between your fingers moderately, then lift them gently with your hand.
Lift the marked skin as described, then bring together the edges of the wound with sutures and treat it until it heals. If a hot swelling appears upon cutting or incising, calm that swelling with cirrus or similar soothing ointments.
The lower eyelid may also experience inversion of its lashes, in which case you should employ what we've mentioned regarding cutting, suturing, and cauterizing with fire or sharp medicine. Cauterization is more effective and less burdensome for the patient than cutting and stitching.
Cauterization with reeds is performed as follows: turn the eyelid outward and make an inner incision as described previously. Then, prepare two thin reeds or wooden sticks whose length matches that of the eyelid and whose width is slightly less than a lancet.
Notches have been made at both ends of the reeds, on either side, where the threads will catch. Then, you gently bring them together over what remains of the eyelid and pull the reeds taut from both sides for several days. The stretched skin eventually dies off, turns black, and decays.
If it takes too long for the skin to fall off on its own, then trim it with scissors. Treat the wound until it heals, and once healed, the eyelid will have risen without the lashes irritating the eye. This is an illustration of the two reeds, which you should craft in this shape and those exact dimensions regarding length and width.
Chapter twelve.
On the removal of ingrown hairs from the eye using a needle.
If it is a single hair or two, you should take a thin needle and thread a fine, smooth silk through it; then gather the ends together and tie them in a very delicate knot. The length of the silk should be about a span.
Then, in the loop, thread another silk that is shorter and finer than the first one; tie its ends if you wish. The patient should place their head on your lap, close to the sunlight so they can be seen clearly, as this is very delicate work. Then insert the needle with the loop over the root of the ingrown hair.
You must pass the needle through the eyelid quickly until you see it emerge on top of the natural hairs; then pull the silk upwards, over the loop and insert that hair or those hairs - there should be no more than three - into the loop.
Then you must pull your hand back with it until the hair comes out of the eyelid along with the loop and the natural hairs. If you pull on the loop but do not see the hair coming out, then you should tug downwards on the loop using the silk that was first threaded through the needle so that the loop emerges from the upper hole.
Then you must put the hair back into the loop and pull them both until they are visible through the upper hole; at that point, you should slip the loop off of the needle and remove it along with the silk, then massage the eyelid and leave it tense for two or three days so that the hair stays in place while flesh grows over it.
If the hair is too short, you can either add a longer natural hair to it so that they rise together or leave it for a time until its length allows you to extract it.
Chapter Thirteen.
In treating the ptosis that occurs in the upper eyelid of the eye.
The cause of this ptosis is named lagophthalmos and it can be either congenital or acquired. The latter is caused by the healing of a wound, laceration, cauterization, or something similar.
The treatment involves incising that healing, separating its lips and placing between them a wick of linen, which is then tied until it heals. It should be noted that substances which dry or contract [the wound] ought not to be used in treating this condition.
If you do that, the ptosis will return more severely than before. Instead, use substances which relax [the wound], such as fenugreek and a poultice made with water in which mallow and flax seeds have been boiled. You may also use an ointment of *dyakilion* dissolved with some oil, with which you anoint the wick and treat the condition.
The crux of the treatment is to use every trick in order to prevent it from healing in its original form. The method we have described is but one part of treating ptosis, not a way to restore the tissue to its prior condition entirely.
Chapter Fourteen.
In treating the ptosis that occurs in the lower eyelid.
This ptosis, which is inferior in nature, is truly called *ptosis*. It can be congenital or acquired. The latter arises from a wound, laceration, cauterization, or something similar.
You should take a needle with double-threaded string and suture it into the flesh, passing through from left to right of the orbit until the thread is on both sides of the flesh. Then you stretch the flesh upwards using the needle, and sever it with a broad scalpel.
If the eyelid returns to its proper shape, but if not, take a stick and place it on the incision site; turn the lid with it and make two incisions on the inside of the lid, with both ends meeting at the corners that were cut to form an angle. When they meet, their shape becomes similar to this: [image of an angle].
Which is the Greek letter lambda; then you remove that flesh to the extent that its sharp edge ends up below what follows the eyelid, and then you gather together the separated parts with two sutures by stitching it with a wool thread before treating it as we mentioned previously using relaxing medications and twisting until it heals.
If the squint occurred due to an incision, a suture or cauterization, then it is necessary to make a simple incision without removing any hair from above the eyelashes as previously mentioned; you should also separate the lips by twisting them according to what was mentioned earlier.
In summary, treating the squint, whether it is from above or below, involves following a procedure according to what seems appropriate given its specific nature; note that this condition may present with many different appearances and thus the surgeon must adapt his approach as necessary.
So that the shape is returned to its natural form or something close, and in most cases the condition itself will guide you towards what procedure and tools are required.
Chapter Fifteen.
On the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva or cornea.
This adhesion may occur in many people when the upper eyelid adheres to the white part of the eye, hindering its easy movement and preventing it from performing its natural functions; therefore, one should insert the end of a stick underneath the lid.
Lift it upward or extend it with a hook, then cut the adhesion gently with a small scalpel that is not too sharp like the regular scalpels but rather slightly dull so as not to disturb the patient during the procedure and cause the scalpel to slip and damage the eye with its sharpness. Your cutting should be done as if you were peeling off this adhesion.
And wait until the eyelid returns to its natural position, and all adhesions heal. Then rinse your eyes with salty water or drip into them a solution of zinc sulfate dissolved in water. Afterward separate the eyelid from the eye with a piece of cotton.
Place above the eye a piece of wool soaked in egg white, and after three days use soothing douches until it heals.
Chapter 16.
On the excision of pterygium and meat of the eyelids.
Pterygium is of two types: either it is a nervous one, which resembles a hard and thin membrane, or it is non-nervous, resembling a solid white moisture that if touched by iron or grabbed with the hook breaks up.
And if a hook cannot hold it, both types nonetheless start from the upper eyelid until they gradually cover the cornea and block light as well as eye movement. The procedure for cutting them is that the patient should lay their head on your lap.
Then the patient should open their eyes and you must lift the eyelid with your hand, then grab the pterygium with a slightly bent hook and extend it upwards. Next take a needle, insert into its eye an individual horse or cow hair along with a strong thread, bend one end of the needle somewhat, and pierce it through the middle of the pterygium.
You implement it with a needle and tie the sty to the thread, then stretch it upwards and peel away the side of the sty closest to the pupil as if you were shaving it with a hair all the way through its length. Then cut off what's left at the root of the conjunctiva with a delicate scalpel or small scissors and leave the natural meat of the conjunctiva.
So as not to allow the cutting of it [the sty] to cause permanent weeping, and you may stretch out the sty with a hook alone or with thread as we have said then peel away its side with a smooth-edged scalpel which is depicted thus:.
Be cautious not to touch the corneal membrane with the scalpel and thus create a rupture, opening up the pupil; after cutting [the sty], throw into the eye some powdered salt or rusted copper filings, leaving it there until the next day before treating appropriately so that healing may begin.
Should the sty be insensitive, and you are unable to insert a needle or fasten it with a hook [as in previous instructions], this type of sty is often treated by medicine as described in the classification chapter, and should you wish to cut it then open the patient's eye.
Take up a delicate scalpel, smooth-edged with a thin blade as illustrated here:. With a scalpel of this kind, gently peel away the sty from above and should you find it to become loose or crumble under pressure then drip into the eye immediately some rusted copper filings, red oxide, or powdered salt; hold the eye shut until the following day.
Then repeat this procedure until it has all gone away, except if you encounter a warm swelling in the eye then leave the sty and treat the warm swelling until healed; after which return to treating the sty by peeling until healing begins; as for an eyelid fold...
If the stye causes severe discomfort to the eye, suspend it with a hook and cut off some of it, but do not go too deep in the cutting process so as to avoid excessive tearing. Then apply red alum or zinc oxide drops into the eyes until it heals.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the excision of varices and superfluous flesh that grows in the eye.
In the eyes of some people, red flesh may grow and overlay or almost cover the cornea, or even spill over onto the eyelids; possibly causing the eyelids to turn outwards, resembling wild roses.
The procedure involves having the patient place their head in your lap, then opening their eyes while you extract all of that red flesh using appropriate hooks.
Alternatively, you can grasp it with forceps or tweezers and then cut the flesh bit by bit until all of it is removed; be careful not to injure the eye while working on it. Your cutting instrument could either be the lancet we described for excising calluses or a pair of small scissors. Similar to those used for extracting splinters, as illustrated in the chapter that follows this one.
Once you have completed the excision and all varices are gone, fill the eye with ground salt or apply red stibnite along with other caustic medications; place cotton wool soaked in egg white on top of the eye to prevent hot swelling.
If any varices remain and blood becomes an issue, causing you to fear hot swelling, then leave the eye for now and treat it with medications that will ease the inflammation; return to perform the procedure once more until it heals. Do the same when dealing with superfluous flesh that may appear in this type of situation.
However, you should avoid treating every weak eye, whether through a natural tendency or due to an ailment, by operating with iron instruments. Do not embark on any such procedures until you see that the organ can withstand it.
Chapter 18.
The extraction of arrows from the eye.
Arrows are red veins that form on the eye, obstructing vision and weakening it over time. First, you should examine whether the eye with arrows is strong and free of other ailments.
Then proceed with the extraction, which is as follows: Have the patient rest their head on your lap. Attach one or two hooks to those veins, depending on your skill, and ensure they are supple. The hooks should look like this [image]: .
Alternatively, you may use two double-pronged hooks attached to a single shaft, as pictured here [image]: .
Then, with a fine pair of scissors, gently remove those veins and wipe away the blood periodically until you see that the eye is free of them and the bleeding has stopped. Take care not to harm the eye with the scissors' tips, and ensure your work is done in the morning or evening when the sun is low in the sky.
Pay close attention to your work, ensuring you only cut those specific veins and nothing more. Once finished, drop green or red eye medicine into the eye to eliminate any remaining arrows with its sharp action. If it is not possible to complete the entire procedure at once, then apply a dressing to soothe the eye's pain.
Leave the dressing in place for a few days until the pain subsides and any inflammation has gone down, then repeat the procedure precisely as described above until the eye is healed. [image of scissors]
Leave the patient [with their head in the student's lap, and another should dry the tears and carefully examine the front and back of the eye while you] originally operate by opening the eye with fixed or triangular forceps held by a pupil.
Accurate, and the patient's head should be in the student's lap; another should dry the tears and examine both the front and back of the eye, while you may choose to start the extraction from above or below the eye; it is preferable to use two forceps on the upper side, one at the end of the eyebrow and the other at its beginning.
Hold the forceps between your middle and index fingers, then attach hooks underneath them; hold these with your ring finger and little finger, and lift your hand gently until you see all the flaps have been raised; cut from the smaller corner either with scissors or a sharp knife as preferred.
Insert the needle under the flaps, making sure all of them are lifted over the blade; do not cut until you have dried it well, for tears and blood may become slippery otherwise.
Then cut with the scissors any excess over and under the eyebrow, leaving no residue stuck to the cornea; if you can manage to peel it off the cornea with your finger, that is best, although in some people this may not be possible.
If you have cut it, then the pain will last for days; alleviate this with soothing agents to avoid a hot swelling, and repeat the procedure as needed.
Chapter Nineteen.
On returning the cartilage to the nose.
Physicians call the cartilage a nāsūra; if you have treated it by cauterization or with sharp medicine as described earlier and it has not healed, there is no other option but to cut into the swelling when it ripens and extract all moisture or pus within.
Until the bone is exposed; when the bone is exposed and you see corruption or blackness in it, scrape it with this tool:
It is called the 'rough-headed' and it's made of Indian iron; its head is round like a button, either grooved like a file or smooth like a rasp. Place it on the affected area of bone then rotate it between your fingers as you squeeze your hand slightly until you feel that the corruption has been scraped away.
Do this several times then treat the area with astringent and drying medications; if the wound closes, flesh grows back in it, discharge ceases and it remains like that for forty days without anything new happening to it.
Then you may be sure that it has healed; otherwise, there is no other course of action but to reconnect the fistula with the nostril in this manner: uncover the bone again either by using iron or an acrid medication. Once the bone is exposed take a bifurcated tool like this:
Its iron tip is triangular and its shaft tapers to a point, as you can see; place it on the bone close to the corner of the eye and keep your hand slightly away from the patient's eye then rotate the tool with your hand until it penetrates the bone and the patient feels it.
By feeling air escaping from it when he covers his hand over his nose; at that point treat the area with astringent and drying medications as I told you earlier. If flesh grows back in it and the place hardens then whatever used to discharge outward will now return into the nose which should be less bothersome for the patient, God willing.
Chapter Twenty.
Inversion of the eyelid.
If the eye itself has come out without any damage or loss of sight, you should ease the patient and then cauterize it at the root. Then place a syringe in the back of the neck, but without making an incision, and suck gently.
Then you should place on the eye a plaster made of acacia, aloe vera, frankincense and solomon's seal. Then bandage the eye securely with numerous swabs; these are prepared by taking very soft lint and folding each piece into four layers to make thick pads.
Then place the swabs one on top of the other, as many as necessary for the eye. Then bandage them securely with a firm cloth, pulling it tight. Reapply the syringe and suck without making an incision. Finally, remove the syringe and leave the eye bandaged like this for a day and a night.
If the bandage loosens during this time, it should be tightened again. Then remove the bandage after a day and a night as previously stated. If you see that the eye has not returned to its normal position, reapply the dressings, swabs, tightening and syringe until it does.
Chapter 21.
On the excision of the iris.
If a tear occurs in the iris layer, and it comes out and protrudes outside the eyelids, like an iris, disfiguring the person's appearance, you may wish to excise it. Proceed as follows.
This involves inserting a needle at the base of the iris, from underneath upwards; then insert another needle with doubled thread through it and pierce this. Leave the first needle in place.
First, cut the location where the doubled thread bends and tie some grapes to it around the needle and pull it tight. Then remove the needle and place wool soaked in egg white on the eye; leave it until the threads fall out along with the grapes, then treat the eye with a strengthening medicine.
until the wound heals; you may pull on the grapes from each side with a circle of cloth until the eye protrudes sufficiently, then apply a thin lancet that reaches deep into the eye so as to drain out the vitreous humour and the eye will drop and wither where it lies; then pull on it until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
Treating cataracts.
This condition known as cataract is a sort of veil that gathers over the eye, resembling descending water but without any fluid; to address this one must seat the patient upright on a chair and take hold of their head with both hands on either side.
And shake their head until you see the veil move downward in your eye, then hold still and it will dissipate; light will be restored and the patient will perceive objects as they did before; if this veil does not descend, we know that this is due to fluid, for if the above method fails to bring down the veil.
Then seat the patient between your hands and take a thin lancet, making an incision above the corneal membrane slightly where the conjunctiva meets with the cornea at the limbus, so that the material is discharged.
When this material is discharged, one must then drip into the eye hot water with honey mixed in or water that has been boiled along with fenugreek and honey; afterwards treat it with other remedies until healed.
Chapter twenty-three.
As we have mentioned the types of water in the division and which are suitable for use in the goblet with an explanation, you can take it from there as being correct; at that point, you should have the patient sit facing you in a cross-legged position opposite the light near the sun.
You tie up the patient's healthy eye and pull it tight, then lift the eyelid of their eye with your left hand if it is the left eye that has water in it or with your right hand if it is the right eye.
Then you place the tip of the goblet near the cornea with as much pressure as a pencil, in the white part of the eye from towards the smaller corner; then push the goblet forcefully while turning it with your hand until it penetrates the white part of the eye and you feel that the goblet has reached an empty space.
The depth to which the goblet should penetrate into the eye should be as deep as from a child to the edge of the iris, which is the outer part of the eye; you will see the copper in the same way that you would look at an eye through a lens due to the clarity of the cornea; then move the goblet upwards towards the location where the water is.
Then push it downwards repeatedly; if water comes out immediately, the patient will be able to see what their vision was opened up to immediately while the goblet is still in their eye and then wait a moment. If the water rises again, lower it once more without removing the goblet.
If it becomes stable and does not rise, remove the goblet gently while turning your hand a little at a time; then dissolve some pure Andrian salt in the water and wash out the eye from within, then apply an ointment or piece of damp cotton wool to the outside of the eye.
Rose oil and egg white are mixed together and the eye is bound with it; however, we and those of our time place upon it crushed cumin with egg white. If this does not allow the sty to open up in the eye due to its hardness, then know that there are people whose eyes may be very hard. It is necessary for you to take the scalpel called 'Al-Barid', which has this image:
Then you perforate with it only the conjunctiva and do not delve into the perforation; rather, gently tap a sty-lance at an appropriate spot and then insert the lance as we mentioned. When your work is complete, prepare for the patient a firm bed in which they can sleep on their back inside a dark room.
He should abstain from all movement and coughing, and his diet must consist of foods that soften his constitution. He is not to move his head right or left at all, and the bandage remains in place until the third day, when it is removed inside this dark room, after which you test his vision.
You show him objects and then replace the bandage until the seventh day, without doing so during or after the treatment, for lancing it may cause water to rise rapidly due to severe straining of vision.
If a hot swelling occurs, then you should remove the bandage before the seventh day and treat it to ease this swelling, until such time as it subsides; at which point you can release his vision and place over his face a veil which he can get accustomed to seeing through from beneath. He remains in that dark room for several days.
He should then leave the house in stages, adapting to normal life once more. Be aware that a novice sty-lancer cannot do without observation on multiple occasions before proceeding with such work; I have been informed by certain people of Iraq that they use a perforated lance which can suck up water.
I have not seen anyone in our country make such a thing, nor have I read about it in any of the books of the ancients. It is possible that this may be something new and innovative. Here are depicted various types of syringes for those who are unfamiliar with them.
It is made especially of copper, and its tip is extremely thin and triangular in shape with a sharp edge.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
On treating flesh growths in the nose.
Various types of excess flesh may grow in the nose, resembling something like a many-legged scorpion, or it may be hard and cancerous with a dark color, or soft and not discolored.
If the flesh growth is soft, not malignant, and not cancerous, then the patient should sit facing the sun with their nostril open. You should insert a hook into this flesh, draw it out, and cut off whatever you can reach with a sharp, delicate scalpel on one side.
Keep doing this until you are certain all the flesh has gone; if there is any remaining that cannot be cut, then gently scrape it away with a delicate instrument so as to leave nothing behind. If blood loss becomes an issue or inflammation and heat occur.
Then treat it accordingly or, if the growth is malignant, immediately cauterize until blood flow ceases and all flesh has gone; afterward, pour vinegar water into the nostril after cutting, or an alcoholic drink, so that moisture passes through the nose to the throat.
You may assume the patient has healed if moisture passes as it should; but, if not, know that there is still flesh growth higher up at the fragile bones which your instrument could not reach to cut. At this point you must take a somewhat thick thread of linen.
You tie many knots in it and leave between each knot a space of about one finger or less. Then the patient carefully pushes one end of the thread into his nostril with an applicator, or whatever else is available, after having knotted it like a button. He then pulls on the thread until he feels the other end reach his nasal cavity.
It comes out at his throat, and boys in school often do the same thing; this is easy for anyone who wants to try it. Then you take both ends of the thread: one that came out at the mouth, the other which stayed inside the nose.
Next, you use the thread's knots to separate the flesh, doing so until you are sure that the tissues have been cut by the string's knots; then you remove the thread and clean any blood from inside the nose using a piece of cotton which has been soaked in Egyptian balm ointment. Do this for three days or more.
This process continues until the ointment has devoured all that is left of the flesh; lastly, you insert a lead tube inside the nose for several days until it heals completely. If further treatment to dry out the wound is required, then this technique can be employed.
This is an image caption of the instrument used to pour oil-based medicines into the nose: "A syringe for administering oils and drugs by dripping them into the nasal cavity.
It is made of silver or copper, resembling a small open-topped lamp with an open spout as well.
If you wish, the tube may be constructed with an enclosed end like a pipe, and the spout of the syringe can be flat instead.
There is also a handle at the other end, as you can see, which you grip when heating up oil or whatever fluid or liquid substance you want to use inside it.
Chapter twenty-five.
In warts that grow at the tip of the nose.
A wart often grows at the tip of the nose, it becomes bigger over time until its appearance is ugly.
Therefore, one should cut it off at its first appearance and remove all of it; then apply either cauterization or the burning medication that acts as a substitute for cauterization to the affected area. If you miss cutting it before it becomes big, examine whether it is hard, stony, dark in color and has little sensation.
Do not expose it to iron, as it is a cancerous growth and I have often seen those who cut this tumor suffer great harm from it; even if the tumor feels soft, is not dark in color and you think that cutting could be an option for treating all of it. Make the patient vomit, cut it without hesitation or waiting; treat the area with something that dries and tightens until it is healed.
Chapter twenty-six.
On stitching the nose, lip and ear. If the connection becomes separated due to injury or something similar, know that when there is a separation of the connection in one of these cartilages, rarely does treatment work well on it except for some people.
Whenever something like that happens to one of them, examine whether the wound is fresh with its blood; you should bring together the lips of the wound by stitching and then treat it until it heals. If the separated connection has already divided both sides and each side became a proper incision on its own, you must excise from every incision as thick as its outer skin until they bleed.
Then the lips are united by sewing, tightened and sprinkled with powdered frankincense and myrrh. Above this sprinkle a plaster of mastic ointment or other healing ointments; leave it bound for two to three days before unbinding.
The dressing is changed, and left until the stitches fall out by themselves; then it is treated with ointment until healed. The procedure for stitching is to reunite separated parts: either with needles as described in sewing the abdomen, or with thread as explained there.
Chapter 27.
On the removal of swellings that appear in the lips.
Many people get small, hard swellings inside their lips that resemble goose bumps; some are smaller and larger than others. The lip should be turned over and cut open at each swelling then hooked with the needle.
Cut it from all sides, then pack the wound with powdered alum to stem the bleeding; rinse out your mouth with vinegar and salt water. Treat the wounds with an astringent until they are healed.
Chapter 28.
On the excision of excess flesh from the gums.
Often, excess flesh grows on the gums that the ancients call apolys. You should hook it with a needle or grasp it with forceps and cut it off at its base; leave the pus or blood to drain before applying powdered alum to the site.
Or one of the astringent, drying plasters; if the flesh returns after treatment then it often does so. Cut it off again and cauterize it for it will not return after cautery.
Chapter twenty-nine.
Extracting teeth with an iron instrument.
Hard and unsightly tartar may form on the inner and outer surfaces of teeth and between the gums, sometimes becoming black or yellow or green; this can lead to gum disease and discolouration of the teeth.
You should have the patient sit in front of you with their head resting in your lap, and then extract the tartar, along with any sand-like particles, from the tooth or molar where they are visible; likewise for black, yellow or green discolouration or anything else until it is all removed.
If it is removed entirely in the first instance, so much the better; otherwise you should repeat this process day after day until your objective has been achieved, and remember that a molar requires different tools of varying shapes and sizes depending on what you are trying to achieve.
Because the instrument used to clean a molar from within is different from that used for external cleaning, and another still for those between molars; these are various designs of instruments you should have ready.
Chapter thirty.
Extracting teeth.
You should try to treat the molar for pain by any means necessary, and be hesitant about extracting it because nothing can replace such a precious entity if you do; even so, there may come a time when extraction is unavoidable.
If the patient is insistent upon extraction, you should ensure that you are absolutely certain which molar is causing them pain. Often the patient is deceived by the pain and thinks it's in the healthy tooth, so he extracts it, but the pain doesn't go away until he removes the diseased one. We have seen this repeatedly with barbers.
So if you are sure that the painful tooth is correct, then you should incise around the tooth with a sharp scalpel until you loosen the gums on all sides; then move it with your fingers or with gentle forceps at first little by little until you dislodge it.
Then take a firm hold of it with the large forceps, with the patient's head between your knees so he cannot move; then pull the tooth straight out lest you break it. If it does not come free, take one of those tools and insert them under the tooth from each side gently, rocking it as before.
If the tooth has a hole or a cavity, you should fill that hole with a cloth and pack it well with the end of a slender spatula so that it does not crumble when you grasp it with the forceps; and you must be thorough in incising all around the gums.
Take care not to break it, for if any part remains it will cause the patient more harm than his original pain; and avoid doing what ignorant barbers do with their bridges, acting without using the methods we have described.
They often cause great harm to people, the least of which is that a tooth breaks leaving its roots whole or in part. Or it may be that some of the jawbone is extracted along with it, as we have seen many times; after extraction he should rinse his mouth with wine or vinegar and salt. If there is bleeding from the site, which often occurs, then pound some zinc and fill the socket.
Otherwise, extract it if the vitriol is of no use to you. Figure of fine forceps which are used first to move the tooth: they have long branches and short handles, rather thick so that they do not bend when seizing the tooth with them. This is a figure of large forceps:
They are, as you can see, thick handled so that when you grasp them they do not give way or bend; the branches should be short and made of Indian iron or steel, well tempered at their extremities, and at their ends have teeth which fit into each other to enable a firm, close grip.
The branches may also be made with a file-like end so that they are likewise strong and secure when closing.
Chapter thirty one.
On the extraction of tooth roots and removal of broken jaw bones.
If, when extracting a tooth, the root remains and is broken off then you should place over the area some cotton with butter for a day or two until it softens, after which introduce a pelican or forceps. which at their ends have branches resembling the beak of the bird called 'pelican', this is an image of these forceps:
