Chapter 32.
On the extraction of teeth that have grown over others.
If teeth grow in an unnatural manner, it ruins the appearance, especially if this happens to women and slaves; therefore, you should examine whether a tooth has grown behind another one.
If it cannot be removed by forceps or a lever, and if it is attached to another tooth, cut this tooth with the instrument shown in this illustration.
It is shaped like a small beak and made of very sharp Indian steel. You should cut this tooth over several days due to the hardness of the molar, and so as not to shake other molars. If it is protruding, use a rasp like this one from India.
It should be entirely made of Indian steel, with a very finely engraved handle like the rasp used to make needles. Use it to gently rasp away at the molar little by little over many days so as not to shake other molars loose. Then smooth and clean it with some rasps.
If a molar has broken and is causing pain to the tongue when speaking, it should also be rasped until any roughness from the break disappears so that it becomes smooth again and stops hurting the tongue or ruining speech.
Chapter 33.
On securing loose molars with silver thread or gold wire.
If the front molars become loose and wobbly due to a blow or fall, and the patient cannot chew anything without them falling out - despite having applied astringent medicine - then it is advisable...
The solution is to secure them with gold or silver thread, although the former is preferable as it does not oxidize and become foul-smelling like silver after some time has passed. The thread should be medium in thickness relative to how much space there is between each molar.
The image of ligation is as follows: take the thread and pass it through the gap between two sound molars, then weave with both ends of the thread around the mobile teeth, be they one or more, until you reach the other side with your weaving to the sound molar.
You then weave back to the side you began on, pulling gently and judiciously so that it moves not at all; your pull on the thread should be near the roots of the teeth so as not to slip, after which you cut the remaining ends with scissors, gather them, twist them together with a pair of tweezers, then hide them between the sound molar and the mobile one.
So that the tongue is not hurt, you should leave it tight as long as necessary. If it loosens or breaks, tighten it with another thread and enjoy this way for your whole life. This is an image of the molars and how ligation appears in two sound molars and two mobile ones as you see here:
Sometimes you may return a single tooth or two after they have fallen out to their place, and ligate as we described above; this is done by the skilled dental surgeon, who might also carve bone from the cow's skull to make it like a molar tooth, placing it in the position of the missing tooth. It is then tightened as we said before so that you may enjoy its use for an extended period of time.
Chapter 34.
On cutting the ligament which appears under the tongue, preventing speech.
This ligament that appears under the tongue may be either natural, with which a person is born; or acquired due to an injury which has healed.
The procedure is as follows: open the patient's mouth and place their head on your lap; lift up their tongue, then cut that nerve-cord across so that the tongue is released from its grip. If there is some stiffness or knotting due to a healed wound, incise it with a hook and make an incision across until the cord is divided and the knots are loosened.
Be careful not to make the incision too deep into the flesh, lest you sever an artery and cause bleeding; then have them rinse their mouth after the cut with rosewater or vinegar and cold water. Then place a linen wick under the tongue, which the patient should hold every night.
So that it does not heal again; if blood is shed, apply powdered white vitriol to the spot, and if the bleeding is strong, cauterize the place with a biconvex iron suitable for this purpose. Then treat them with all other therapies until they recover.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of the frog-like cyst under the tongue.
A small frog-like cyst may occur under the tongue, which prevents the tongue from functioning properly; it might even grow into a bone and fill the mouth. The procedure is as follows: open the patient's mouth towards the sun and observe where the swelling originates.
If you see it, dark-coloured or black and hard without feeling for the patient, do not touch it as it is a cancerous growth; but if it tends towards whiteness with some moisture present, incise it with a hook and make an incision with a delicate scalpel, freeing it from every side.
If the blood overwhelms you during your work, place powdered lead acetate on it until the bleeding stops. Then return to your work and remove it completely, after which he should rinse with vinegar and salt.
Then treat it with the appropriate remaining treatment until it heals.
Chapter Thirty-Six
On the treatment of swollen tonsils.
And other tumours that grow in the throat may be glands similar to those that appear outside, called tonsils. If you treat them as mentioned in the previous section and they do not heal...
If the tumour is hard, dark in colour and has little sensation, do not expose it to iron. And if it is red in colour with a thick base, also do not expose it to iron for fear of bleeding. Instead, leave it until it ripens; you may then either remove it or allow it to burst on its own.
And if it is white in colour, round and has a thin base, this is the one that should be cut out. Before operating on it, check whether its hot swelling has completely subsided or diminished to some extent.
Then have the patient sit beside the sun with their head in your lap. Open his mouth and take a servant's tool between your hands to press down on his tongue using an instrument like this: [image of an instrument]
It is made of silver or copper, thin like a knife. When you press the tongue down with it and spot the tumour, take a hook and insert it into the tonsil, drawing it outwards as much as possible without pulling any membranes along with it.
Then cut it with an instrument that looks like this: resembling a pair of scissors, but its two ends are curved, and the edges of each end are very sharp. It is made from Indian iron or Damascene steel.
If this instrument is not available, then cut it with a scalpel that looks like this:
It is sharp on one side and not sharp on the other. After cutting out one tonsil, cut out the other in the same way. Then, after cutting, gargle the patient with cold water or a mixture of vinegar and water.
If there is any bleeding, gargle with water that has been boiled with pomegranate rind, myrtle leaves, or similar astringents, until the bleeding stops. Then treat it until it heals. Other tumors may grow in the throat apart from the tonsils; cut them out as I mentioned for cutting out the tonsils.
I treated a woman for a tumor that had grown inside her throat, leaning towards becoming a cyamosis, and was slightly insensitive. It almost completely blocked the throat, and the woman could only breathe through a narrow passage. It had prevented her from eating or drinking water.
She was close to death, and if she remained like that for another day or two, the tumor would have killed her. Two branches had sprouted from it, growing out of both her nostrils. So I quickly inserted a hook into one branch and pulled on it; this caused a substantial piece to be extracted.
I then cut the piece I had extracted at the nostril hole, and did likewise with what was protruding from her other nostril. Next, I opened her mouth, pressed down on her tongue, and inserted the hook into the tumor once more. Then I severed a portion of it; only a small amount of blood flowed out.
The woman's tumor burst and she immediately drank water, then had some food. We continued to cut away at the tumor for a long time; as we removed parts of it, new tissue formed in their place. This went on for quite a while.
I devised a plan and cauterized the tumor inside her throat, hoping it would stop growing. I then left that region and did not know what became of her after my departure.
Chapter Thirty-Seven.
On the excision of tonsillar tumors.
If a tonsillar tumor, known as a grape-like growth, descends and swells up, appearing white and elongated, it is called a column. If the base is thick and round, then it is referred to as a grape. Should treatment be applied as outlined in this section but proves ineffective...
When I saw that the hot tumor had subsided and her throat was thin, it became apparent that an excision should be performed. However, if the growth is clustered and round with no length, appearing reddish-blue or dark in color, and lacking sensation, then such an intervention ought to be avoided.
But this carries risk for the patient, and so if you encounter a growth of the type described, with its whiteness and length, it is best to have the patient sit facing the sun and depress their tongue using the instrument that will be described shortly. Then, insert the hook into the grape-like tumor.
Use this method to pull the growth downwards and cut it with one of the two instruments mentioned earlier for tonsillectomy. It is important not to remove more than what has grown beyond the natural state, as excessive excision could damage their voice and speech.
Then, after the incision, you should employ what we have described for cutting the tonsils and treat them until they heal. If the patient is reluctant to have them cut, then you should use cunning to cauterize them without fear or hesitation. The method of cauterization involves using a sharp medicinal tool while holding the patient's head in your lap.
Then, depress their tongue with the tool we mentioned earlier. Next, take some of the caustic water I described in the section on cauterization and mix it with unslaked lime to make a paste that is neither thick nor thin. Fill this paste into the depression of this tool; here is an illustration.
Its end, where you place the medicine, has a depression like that of an enema spoon. You position the tool with the medicine on their uvula while they lie on their side so that saliva can drain from their mouth and prevent any from falling into their throat which could harm them.
Then, hold your hand with the medicine and squeeze it onto their uvula for around half an hour until you see it has turned black and the stinging of the medicine has subsided. If you wish, you can take some cotton wool, wrap it around a tongue depressor, dip the cotton in the medicine...
...and insert the tongue depressor with the cotton into a tube from above so that the cotton adheres to their uvula. Repeat this process as many times as needed until you have cauterized the tonsils sufficiently. Leave it be, and after three or four days they will wither and fall out. If necessary, reapply the medicine.
After cauterization, wipe around the tonsils with cotton wool soaked in butter and dry off any medicine from the surrounding area. Then have them rinse their mouth with cold water. Treat the outside with compresses and the inside with gargles until they heal. This form of treatment is safer than incision and lessens fear.
The uvula can also be treated with a milder method than cauterization and excision. It is treated by fumigation in the following manner: take foutheng, hyssop, sage, rue, wormwood, chamomile, southernwood, and similar herbs; collect all or some of them in a pot and cover them with vinegar.
Boil [the herbs] and cover the pot with a layer of clay from a potter's wheel, ensuring it is well-sealed. Leave an opening in the middle of the lid; attach to this opening the hollow instrument made either from silver or copper: ...
Insert the end with the pomegranate-shaped knob into the patient's mouth so that the steam rises to reach the uvula via the tube until the uvula is sufficiently steamed; then repeat this process several times until it withers. Be sure not to perform this treatment in the early stages of swelling.
Should you not have this apparatus readily available, take a hollow reed and attach to one end an eggshell so that the patient's mouth is not burnt.
For the eggshell prevents the heat of the steam from scorching the mouth, making it a good and safe treatment.
Chapter 38.
On the removal of thorns and other objects that may lodge in the throat.
Frequently, a fishbone or other object may lodge in the throat. To remove visible objects, depress the tongue with an instrument and then extract the item.
To locate any unseen objects, induce vomiting in the patient before their food is fully digested; this may dislodge the object or allow you to see it as it passes through the throat.
Or they may swallow a mouthful of dry bread, or take a piece of soft sea sponge and attach it to a thread before swallowing it; when the sponge reaches the spot where the thorn is lodged, pull on the thread quickly. Do this several times.
Frequently, the thorn or bone becomes stuck in it and is dislodged. If this method does not succeed then use an instrument made of lead shaped as follows:
It is slightly thicker than a stylus and has a hook on one end which the patient inserts gently into their throat, with their head raised upwards and taking care not to touch the larynx so as not to cause coughing. The physician may also insert it by hand.
It is better for the patient to insert it as they know where the object is embedded and can push downwards, or pull their hand upwards using the instrument, doing so according to what seems appropriate until the object is removed.
Chapter 39
On the removal of leeches embedded in the throat.
If you treat the leech using the methods described in section on medicinal treatment and it is not effective, then examine the patient's throat when there is sunlight after pressing down their tongue with the instrument that I have described to you.
If you see the leech then pull it out with a small hook or a fine, sharp pair of forceps; if this is not possible, take a hollow tube and insert it into the patient's throat close to the leech, then pass a red-hot needle inside the tube.
You do this several times, and the patient refrains from water all day, then he takes a bowl full of cold water, opens his mouth in it, rinses his mouth with it without swallowing even one drop, and moves the water around occasionally with his hand.
For the leech will drop off if it feels water, so if it does not come out as we have described, then vaporize the throat with urine and gallnut by means of the instrument that I have described for fumigating the uvula; do this several times until it falls off.
The way to use the fumigation is as follows: take a container with glowing coals heated by fire, and cover it with a lid that has a hole in its center; insert the end of the instrument into this hole, then place the incense inside. The patient puts his mouth on the end of the tube and closes it so that no smoke escapes until he knows that the incense has reached his throat.
Then the leech will drop off, and if it does not, then the fumigation is repeated several times; meanwhile, the patient abstains from water because of thirst, eats salty food and garlic but drinks no water – this treatment must succeed. Here is a drawing of an instrument with which you can extract a leech from the throat when it comes into view.
It resembles grappling hooks as you can see, except that it has these curved prongs which are inserted into the throat; its two ends resemble a bird’s beak and have the roughness of a file – if they grab something then they do not let go.
There is another instrument which presses down on the tongue; it consists of a wide iron ring, as large as the mouth, placed above the tongue. It is inserted into the mouth vertically on its side, and keeps the mouth open despite the patient’s lack of will to do so – he cannot remove it by any means until you take it out.
Description of the instrument that opens the mouth on this very measurement.
Chapter Forty.
In it he speaks about the treatment of tumours by incision.
Tumours are of many types and varied, as will be mentioned individually in this book; they differ with regard to incision and splitting on two counts: first, the type of tumour itself and what it contains in terms of humours.
The second type of difference is due to the locations in which they occur in the body, for a tumour that occurs in the head is not like one occurring in the buttocks; nor is a tumour arising in a fleshy part like one occurring at a joint - each has its own rules regarding treatment.
There are tumours that should not be lanced until the pus in them has matured and reached its full state; there are others which should be lanced while still fresh, before they have fully matured - such as those occurring near joints.
For if a tumour occurs near a joint and is left until its surroundings become gangrenous, it may destroy the ligaments or nerves of that joint, causing permanent damage to the limb; or perhaps the tumour is close to an important organ.
If you delay lancing it until it is fully matured, you may damage the important organ; or perhaps the tumour is close to the buttocks and must be lanced while fresh, even though it is not yet fully mature - this is necessary so that the incision does not become gangrenous and penetrate into the interior of the buttocks, forming a fistula.
And it becomes a wound that will not heal; you should know the time for lancing tumours which have fully matured, which is when the pain of the tumour has subsided, the fever has gone away, redness and swelling have decreased, the head of the tumour has become soft, and all other signs.
The duck's beak should be inserted at the lowest point of the tumor if possible, to facilitate the flow of pus downwards or at the thinnest part of the tumor and where it is most prominent. The duck's beak should be positioned in line with the body if the tumors are located towards the hands and feet.
The positions of muscles, tendons, nerves, and arteries should be considered in general for all flat areas without bends or folds. For those with bends or folds, adjust the duck's beak accordingly. If the tumor is located in fleshy areas, it is best to leave the duck's beak out.
Until it is fully ripe, as we have said before, if you lance it prematurely, the discharge of pus will be prolonged, and there will be much suppuration and dirt. The lips may become hardened, and the cavity contract. Some tumors require lancing across the body when necessary or according to the needs of the affected part.
For small tumors, use a single duck's beak, and for large ones, use a wide beak or make multiple incisions according to the size of the tumor. In some cases, it may be necessary to scoop out the skin and cut it if it has become like a rag.
It has reached a certain limit, and what is dead must be removed, as often happens with many abscesses and cysts. Some are incised with an incision having three angles, while others have a piece cut out of them shaped like a bay leaf or the groin tumor.
For some, a circular or crescent-shaped incision may be used, along with other similar incisions. For those without a head, such as smooth, flat tumors, only a simple duck's beak should be employed. If the tumor is large and contains a great deal of pus, having been lanced already, you should not proceed hastily to extract all the pus at once.
Remove some of it [the pus] and then tighten the swelling for another day, then remove some pus as well. Repeat this process several times gradually until all the pus is out.
Especially if the patient is weak or a pregnant woman, child or elderly person; often the animal spirit dissipates with the rapid removal of pus, and the patient may die without you realizing it. So be very cautious about this procedure.
After you have lanced these swellings, it is necessary to clean the wound and assess if the incision is small or simple, then use a bandage of old linen or cotton; but if the swelling is large and there are multiple incisions,
You should pack each incision with a pledget, ensuring they are all connected; if the swelling requires skin excision or debridement, it must be packed with old cotton or linen threads without any moisture and tightly bandaged until the third day.
Then remove the packing and treat it with appropriate ointments until healing occurs; if bleeding persists, use cold water and vinegar after soaking a linen cloth in them, applying it to the site of bleeding repeatedly.
If bleeding continues, you must employ management and treatment methods described in various sections of this book as well as the compendium; if it's winter and the site is highly innervated, then moisten dressings with warm oil and spirits.
Apply these dressings to the site; if it's summer and the area is fleshy, moisten dressings with cold water or spirits/oil; by day three as previously mentioned, remove the bandages and clean the wound.
And it is treated with something similar to itself until it heals, and this is all you need to know about the treatment of tumors in general. As for the detailed method, I have mentioned each type of tumor individually, categorizing and summarizing its specific treatment.
Chapter Forty-One.
On incising tumors that occur on the scalp.
Tumors occur on the scalp that are small and belong to the "sala'a" category, contained within thin membranes resembling chicken egg sacs; their types are numerous: some are fatty while others contain a viscous liquid resembling mud.
Some contain a moisture resembling bran or soup-like substance, while others are hardened and solid. All of them can be safely incised and removed unless you encounter an artery during the incision process.
The procedure for incising them is to first probe them with the instrument described below, called a "madis", in order to understand their contents. If they contain moisture, then make an incision along its length with a simple cut as illustrated: [image of incision].
Begin the incision from line ~B~ to line ~C~. Once the moisture has been drained, peel and cut away the entire sac that contained it; leave nothing behind for it often returns if any part is left remaining.
Then dip a piece of cotton in galbanum ointment, or salt water if it is not available, and fill the wound with it. Leave it for another day as it will dissolve any remaining sac; repeat this process with fresh galbanum-soaked cotton a second and third time if necessary.
Until you are certain that none of the cyst remains, then treat the wound with ointments until it heals. If the swelling contains fatty tissue, make an incision in this manner:.
Make a cross-shaped incision and hook the forceps into each lip of the wound, then peel it away from every side, striving to extract the membrane that encloses it. If you encounter an artery, do as I described for you previously. Likewise, proceed with the swelling if it is hardened by the incision.
The treatment is as mentioned, and the incision into a hardened swelling is easier because it has less blood and moisture. Some of these swellings in the head may occur in certain people with absolutely no moisture at all; I have come across such a case.
In the head of an elderly woman, I found a swelling like hard stone, rough and white; it could not be broken even if someone were to throw it at you. The other swellings that occur in the heads of children are different from these.
Make an incision at the base of the ears, a single one; arrange its margins from below to facilitate drainage downwards and then treat it with whatever treatment is appropriate.
Chapter Forty-Two
On Incision into Cysts that Occur in the Neck, These cysts frequently occur in the neck, under the armpits and in the groins; they may also be found elsewhere on the body. Sometimes there is just one of them in the neck, while at other times there are many, some generated by others.
Each cyst is enclosed in a membrane of its own, just as with tumors and swellings on the head, as we described earlier. There are many types of these cysts: some harden, others contain moisture, while yet others are malignant and do not respond to treatment.
If you touch it and find that the mass is soft, its surface color matches closely to that of the skin, and it moves freely in all directions without being adherent to any nerve or vein of the neck, nor does it appear sunken. Therefore, you should make an incision on it simply from above to below the body in this manner, along the line from point 'J' to point 'B':.
You should then strip it away from all sides, stretching the lips of the skin with a hook or two hooks or many as needed, just as we said for head tumors, and extract it little by little while being careful to keep it on the neck so that you do not cut any veins or nerves; let your scalpel be only slightly sharp.
So that you do not cut more than necessary or cause the patient distress and thus end up cutting something that should be left untouched, if ever you do cut a vein or artery and this prevents you from continuing your work, place powdered zinc oxide in the wound or some other hemostatic agent.
Then compress the wound and leave it until the inflammation subsides, the wound relaxes, and suppuration begins to form; at that point, the bleeding will stop so you can resume your work and complete it. Then check with your index finger if there are any remaining small cysts, which you should excise and remove.
If the cyst has a large nerve at its origin, you should not excise it entirely but instead tie it with a strong double thread and leave it so that it can drop on its own without causing any harm; then pack the wound with old lint dipped in Egyptian ointment and treat it.
And if the entire hogshead is cut, it should be gathered that the edges of the wound are brought together and sutured immediately after confirming there remains no excess flesh whatsoever. If you observe any remaining excess due to the bone structure of the hog.
You should aim to cut at its base and apply the suturing method as mentioned previously. For cuts made on hogs, which tend to be more moist, you may simply pad it with a simple dressing where necessary to promote healing, ensuring the padding is applied to the lower side as instructed earlier.
After the padding, you may use Egyptian ointment and similar substances to promote healing of any remaining corruption until you observe the wound is clean. Then treat it with ointments that stimulate flesh growth until it fully heals.
Chapter 43
On the incision of the larynx to treat swellings that occur within the throat.
Early sources have mentioned this laryngeal incision for throat swellings, but I have not witnessed anyone in our region perform it. This is a common thread in their discourse. As for those suffering from dysphasia, they should avoid the laryngeal incision as it would be of no benefit to them.
Due to the fact that all of the internal organs and the lungs are affected, and for those suffering from hot swellings in the mouth, throat or tonsils if there is no problem with the windpipe, it is imperative to employ the laryngeal incision to prevent deterioration caused by asphyxiation.
When performing this, one should make a small incision across the larynx below three or four of the tracheal rings, between two rings and to an extent similar to an incision in the peritoneum but not through cartilage. This position is suitable for the incision.
Since it is devoid of flesh and the blood vessels are distant, if the physician is timid they should stretch out the skin of the throat with a hook, then make an incision in the skin until you reach the trachea; turn away from any blood vessels that may be visible.
Then the peritoneum, which we have described, is cut through, and you can find out where to make an incision in the trachea from the phlegm that comes out of it along with what perforates; also from the cessation of the voice, and leave the wound open for some time. When this period, during which suffocation was feared, has passed, bring together the lips of the wound from the skin.
Its outline, and the cartilage alone is removed without any of its lining. Then you should use medicines that promote flesh growth until it heals. The author of this book says: 'The meaning of all these words which we have related is only when they see a patient whose throat has been blocked by one of those tumors that were mentioned'.
And the patient is near to death, and their life is in peril; they proceed to make an incision into the larynx so that the patient can breathe somewhat through the wound site and thus be saved from death. They were instructed to leave the wound open until the severity of the illness has passed, which takes about three days or so.
Then they were instructed to suture the wound and treat it so that it may heal. What I personally witnessed was a servant who took a knife and thrust it into her throat, cutting part of the trachea; when summoned to treat her, I found she was gasping like something slaughtered. So, I uncovered the wound.
I found that the blood which had come out of the wound was minimal, so I knew she hadn't cut any arteries or veins; breath came from the wound. So, I quickly sutured the wound and treated it until it healed. Apart from a hoarseness in her voice, nothing happened to the servant.
And she returned after some days to her best condition. Thus we say that laryngotomy carries no risk.
Chapter 44.
On the incision of a swelling that appears in the pharynx from without, which is called elephantiasis of the pharynx.
The swelling called elephantiasis of the pharynx is a large tumour coloured like the body; it occurs frequently in women and has two varieties.
It may be either natural or symptomatic; there is no remedy for the former, but as to the latter, it has two forms: one resembles a fatty tumour and the other resembles an arterial aneurysm. To perform an incision on this kind carries some risk. You should never dare to use the knife on it, except for a small one which you have tested and probed with the probe, finding that it resembles fatty tissue without being attached to any of the vessels; then cut into it as you would an ordinary fatty tumour. Remove it with its capsule if there is one, otherwise excise the whole thing and treat the wound as required.
Chapter 45.
On incising different kinds of fatty tumours.
There are numerous varieties of fatty tumours, all of which have been mentioned in the classification; we should now clarify the distinction between them and ulcers since this can be confusing. An ulcer is accompanied by fever, heat and severe pain until the inflammation subsides. And the abscess completes its process; then the fever and inflammation subside. There is no heat or fever with this treatment, nor any pains. It is contained within a serous membrane which forms a special covering for it. Its color resembles that of the body, and initially it is like a chickpea but becomes larger, sometimes like an egg, and at other times smaller.
There are two types of it, either fatty or containing fluid, and the colors of the fluid are many as I have already mentioned in the classification. Therefore, when you proceed to treat an abscess, you should first examine it thoroughly using the instrument known as 'al-madis,' which will be illustrated in the following chapter.
Examine the size of the swelling, then use the 'madis' and observe what it brings out. If fluid is discharged, regardless of its color, make a simple incision as described for other tumors. But if no fluid is released after using the 'madis,' know that it is fatty.
Make a cross-shaped incision on it as I have instructed you, then suspend it with hooks and carefully remove the skin from each side. Be cautious to preserve the sac, if possible, so that you can extract it whole together with the abscess.
If the sac is ruptured during the procedure and you are unable to remove it whole, which often occurs, then extract it in pieces so that nothing remains. For if any part of it is left behind, whether small or large, the abscess will recur with greater severity.
If you are unable to remove all of it and a small amount remains, then irritate the wound upon finishing by applying some sharp corrosive substances. Draw the edges of the wound together tightly and apply something that will reduce the hot swelling. Treat it with other remedies until it heals completely. If the abscess is large, draw its lips together and treat it with a substance that promotes healing.
If you encounter a prominent vein, whether beating or not, and the bleeding is excessive, quickly apply crushed zinc oxide to the area and leave it tightly bound for two or three days until the wound becomes gangrenous and the blood flow subsides. Then return to remove any remaining pieces.

Chapter 47.
On the treatment of male breast conditions.
Some people's breasts may swell during puberty, resembling those of women and remaining unsightly as the breast enlarges unattractively. Anyone who dislikes this should make a crescent-shaped incision in the breast like so:
From line B to line C. Then remove all the fat and fill the wound with healing medication; bring together the lips of the wound by sewing them up, and treat it until it heals. If the breast sags downwards and becomes slack due to its bone structure, as happens in women,

Chapter Forty-Nine.
In incising an aneurysm, which is a swelling occurring over an artery or vein.
When an artery is wounded, and the skin above it has healed over, then a swelling often occurs there; likewise, when a vein is injured, a bulge and swelling may develop upon it.

A swelling caused by a vein will be rounded, and appear upon the surface of the body; making an incision in these swellings is dangerous, especially when they occur in the armpit, groin, neck or other parts of the body which are very large - it should be avoided to treat them with metal instruments.

Chapter Fifty.

Swellings caused by nerve injury occur as a result of trauma, excessive fatigue, or similar causes and are most often found in the wrists and heels.

Chapter fifty-one.

Chapter Fifty-Two.
In the treatment of an umbilical hernia.
An umbilical hernia can have many causes: either from a rupture of the peritoneum on the abdomen, causing the omentum or intestine to protrude as in other types of hernias; or due to bleeding from a vein or artery, as previously mentioned; or because of trapped wind.
If it is due to a rupture of the peritoneum and protrusion of the omentum, then the tumor appears flesh-colored, soft, painless, and visibly abnormal. If it is due to protrusion of the intestine, then its appearance differs greatly from what we have described: when pressed with fingers, it recedes and returns; there may be borborygmi, and it increases considerably in size upon defecation and during intense physical activity. If it is due to moisture, then it remains soft.
If it is due to bleeding, then with these signs the tumor appears dark in color; if it is caused by overgrown flesh, then the tumor feels hard and firm, remaining at a consistent size.
If it is due to trapped wind, then its touch feels soft. In this case, you should refrain from treating an umbilical hernia caused by arterial or venous bleeding, or by wind, as there are risks involved, as I informed you in the chapter where I discussed tumors arising from arteries and veins.
If the navel bulge is due to the intestines or omentum, you should instruct the patient to hold themselves in and stand erect with their body stretched out; then mark all around the navel with ink. Chapter Sixty-One.
The Extraction of Stones in Women.
Stones rarely form in women, and if one should suffer from a stone, it is difficult to treat for many reasons. One reason is that the woman might be a virgin, and another is that you will not find a woman who allows herself to be examined by a doctor.
If she is chaste or from a forbidden degree of kinship, it becomes even more challenging due to several factors: the lack of skilled female practitioners in this field, especially those proficient in manual procedures; and the anatomical differences between men and women, which necessitate a deeper incision when operating on a woman.
In such cases, there is a certain degree of risk involved. If absolutely necessary, one should seek out a skilled female doctor, although they are rarely available. As an alternative, you could request the services of a chaste and gentle male physician or engage the assistance of a knowledgeable midwife who specializes in women's health, or even consult with a woman who has some basic understanding of this procedure.
You would then bring this woman and instruct her to perform all tasks as directed, beginning with a thorough examination to determine if the patient is a virgin. This involves inserting a finger into the patient's anus to search for the stone; if found, she should apply pressure to it with her finger.
If the patient is not a virgin, but rather a woman who has given birth (a 'thayb'), instruct the midwife to insert her finger into the patient's vagina and search for the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and squeezing it gently.
If you find it, then you should insert the tube from the bladder's mouth downward to its full extent until you reach the root of the thigh. Then make an incision over it at the midpoint of the vulva, at the root of the thigh on whichever side is most suitable. Feel for the stone in that area.
Keep your finger pressing against the stone, and do not let it move from underneath. Let the incision be small at first; then introduce the sound into this little cut. When you feel the stone with the sound, make a bigger cut according to what you judge is necessary for the stone's exit. Know that stones come in many varieties.
Some stones are small and others large, some smooth and others rough; they may be long or round and have many different shapes. Know the different varieties so that you can judge what is needed. If blood becomes a problem, sprinkle powdered styptic into the wound and let it sit for an hour until bleeding stops. Then return to your work.
Continue until the stone is out, and remember to have ready all the instruments mentioned for dealing with stones in men so that you can make use of them as necessary. If bleeding becomes overwhelming and you find it is coming from a severed artery, sprinkle styptic powder onto the spot.
Bind it tightly with bandages and leave it, do not touch it again or try to remove the stone; the patient may die from this. Then treat the wound, but if bleeding continues for days and turns septic then return to your work of removing the stone.
Chapter Sixty-Two.
On incising the water cyst.
Hydrocele is the accumulation of moisture in the white membrane situated under the skin of the scrotum surrounding the testis, known as the scrotum. It may also occur in a particular membrane prepared by nature on one side of the testicle.
So that it is mistaken for another testis, and occurs between the scrotum's skin and the white membrane we mentioned previously. This does not happen often; this hydrocele develops due to a weakness in the spermatic cord, leading to the accumulation of this substance, which may also occur after a blow to the testes.
This moisture can have many colors; it may be yellowish, bloody red, dark green, or watery white, which is the most common type. These are the signs that help identify it.
If the accumulation of water is in the white membrane as mentioned, then the swelling will be round and slightly elongated like an egg shape, with the testis not visible as moisture surrounds it on all sides.
If the moisture is in its own particular membrane, then the swelling will be round on one side of the testis.
Due to this, a person may mistake it for another testis. If the moisture is between the scrotum's skin and white membrane, then the swelling will be under the testis. To determine the color of the moisture, use the square-shaped probe as illustrated previously; whatever color appears on the probe determines the nature of the moisture.
When treating with cauterization, the patient should be instructed to undergo venesection if possible and if their body allows it; they should then lie on their back on a slightly elevated surface with several cloths placed beneath them, while you sit on their left side.
Instruct a servant to sit on his right, stretching his penis towards one side of the scrotum and toward the lower abdomen. Then take up a wide knife and make an incision in the scrotal skin from the middle downward close to the pubic area.
The incision is made parallel to the line dividing the scrotal skin in two, extending all the way down to the white membrane covering it. You then peel back this membrane and take care not to cut into it; instead, focus on peeling away from the side that adheres more closely to the testicle, being as thorough as you can.
You then thoroughly separate the membrane filled with fluid, extracting all of its contents. Next, you use forceps to spread open the lips of the incision and pull back the membrane without disturbing the scrotal skin covering it. Finally, you proceed to cut through this membrane, doing so either in one clean motion or cutting it into several pieces.
Pay particular attention to the thinner side, as if you do not cut it thoroughly, there is a risk of fluid re-accumulating. Should the testicle emerge from its covering during your work, simply return it and then proceed to stitch together the scrotal skin.
Treat the wound as you would any other injury, allowing it to heal. However, if the testicle has become damaged due to some other illness, you must first tie off the vessels in the spermatic cord to prevent bleeding before cutting away the entire scrotum and extracting the testicle. Following this, treat the patient as previously described.
If the fluid has accumulated in both, indicating two hydroceles are present, repeat the process on the other side as you did with the first. Aim to perform the procedure identically if possible. Once complete, wipe away any residual blood.
You enter the incisions with cotton that has been soaked in oil or rosewater, and from the outside another piece of cotton which was soaked in wine and oil is placed on top. Then you lay out cloths folded over each other; these are the dressings, on top of the testicles and abdominal flanks. And you bind it from above with a bandage that has six ends, as shown in the figure: [Figure goes here].
Then two cloths are taken, sewn together and stuffed with fluffed cotton, like so. The ends that are tied are made of either damp cotton or wool, gathered tightly at the trousers' waistband, one end being placed on the buttocks towards the back.
The second end is directed towards the pubic area, with the other two ends being on the lower thighs and the remaining two ends are placed over the groins. All tension is gathered at the trousers' waistband. These dressings can also be made by cauterization instead of incisions using an iron.
This is done by taking a smooth, double-edged iron and making an incision with it while it's hot, as described before, until the white serosa containing the fluid is exposed. Then take another iron of this shape: [Iron shape goes here].
It resembles the Greek letter Epsilon, and with it you cauterize that serosa while still hot until all fluid has been drained out. Then you stretch the serosa with hooks and peel off the layer containing the sac using an iron; cut as much as possible to remove all of it, being careful not to let fire touch the testicles themselves.
Be aware that this method is safer and less likely to cause bleeding, and superior to the incision method. If you encounter a hot swelling or blood flow during either the incisions or cauterization, or any other issue, it should be treated with the appropriate treatment as described previously.
If the patient is a coward and cannot bear this procedure due to its hideousness, then let him use an instrument called the 'batt' as I shall describe it.
Since the abscess is watery, you should make the patient sit on a high chair and slice the base of the swelling with a broad lancet. Place the 'batt' along the body, making sure that the incision is wide enough for all the water to drain out. Then cover the area with cotton wool and bind it.
Allow it to drain the remaining water, then treat the wound until it heals. If the water does not flow out when you are performing this procedure and instead gathers at the mouth of the incision, this is due to the white membrane obstructing the opening and preventing the water from flowing.
In that case, you should insert a goose feather or the instrument used to extract water from melons (as previously illustrated) into the wound, or widen the incision slightly.
Be aware that water may return and accumulate again after around six months, in which case you should lance it in the same manner as before, and the patient's condition will improve over time.
Chapter sixty-three.
On incising fleshy abscesses and their treatment.
Be aware that incising these abscesses is a dangerous procedure that can lead to death in many cases, and so we advise against it for the patient's safety. Nonetheless, I shall describe this operation along with the various types of swelling.
There are many swellings that can occur in the testicles, arising from numerous causes such as excess heat or a blow to the area. The colour of these swellings will match that of the body, and they do not contain water.
The tumor is hard and perhaps stony, its color like soot, with no sensation. It may be due to the hardening of arteries and their swelling as mentioned before or to the dilation of veins; that which is due to the swelling of arteries becomes known by disappearance of swelling.
When pressed with your fingers, it should not yield at all; as for the one that results from the dilation of veins, nothing will disappear when you press it with your fingers. This type may prove more difficult to treat than other types of tissue.
This is done by incising the scrotal skin, then stretching the testicle upwards and removing it from under the white membrane, freeing the spermatic cord from vessels, tying them off, and cutting the cord after detaching it on all sides of the testicle; if the testicle has adhered to that overgrown tissue.
You must remove the testicle and cut it out, and if there is adhesion between any of the membranes or vessels, you should separate all such adhesions and cut them in a circular fashion.
If the overgrown tissue is in the place of adhesion behind, you must cut all of it and remove the testicle as we said before; then when your work is done, pack the wound with wool soaked in rose oil and wine, and treat it with other remedies until it heals.
Chapter 64.
On the treatment of hydrocele with inguinal hernia.
Inguinal hernia is a twisted tumor with some twisting, similar to a bunch of grapes, accompanied by relaxation of the scrotum and difficulty for the patient in movement, exercise, and walking; this affliction can be treated similarly to what was previously mentioned.
However, we should mention the procedure as performed by our predecessors. I say that you should make the patient sit on a high chair and then push the testicles downwards while holding the scrotum with your fingers together with the vessels close to the penis.
The assistant should grasp and stretch it firmly, then you make a slanting incision with a sharp wide knife next to the vessels until they are exposed, after which you remove them from each side as previously mentioned in the case of arteries found in gums; finally, suture them with a needle having doubled thread.
You tie it to the first place where you displayed the vine leaf and also at its end, then make a vertical incision in the middle along the body's length and remove any impure foul humours collected there.
Next, you treat the wound like any other surgery to prevent suppuration for a period until the vessels nourishing one testicle atrophy; this is acceptable. Should the vine leaf expose all vessels, it would be necessary to excise one testicle with its vessels lest nourishment to the semen be lost. Before cutting the vessels, it wilts so that it is useless.
Chapter 65.
Treatment of venous varicose veins.
These varicose veins occur due to an incision made in the peritoneum extending over the abdomen towards the groins at the sides of the abdomen, causing the intestine to herniate from that rupture onto one testicle.
This hernia may result from either an incision in the peritoneum or its extension, and these two types occur due to numerous reasons such as a blow, jump, shout, lifting heavy objects etc., and if caused by stretching of the peritoneum it appears gradually over an extended period.
It does not occur suddenly, and the swelling remains level with the depth before the peritoneum squeezes the intestine; its sign is that if it comes from a tear in the peritoneum, there will be great pain right away, and a variable lump will appear just under the skin nearby.
This is caused by the protrusion of the intestine and its position outside the peritoneum, sometimes with omentum as well; such a hernia is then both intestinal and omental. It may also occur with wind, or the feces can pass through the intestines and become trapped there, which would be fatal for the patient.
As it causes severe pain and rumbling, especially if compressed, treating this condition with iron is dangerous; you must be careful to avoid doing so. The treatment procedure is as follows: instruct the patient to push the intestines back inside their abdomen by hand if possible.
Then the patient should lie on their back, with you at their side; lift his legs up and pull down the skin that covers the groin until it is stretched out as far as possible, then cut along the entire length of the scrotum before using hooks to open the wound.
The wound should be opened up as far as is necessary for the testicle to pass through, then peel away any peritoneum that lies beneath the scrotal skin until you reach the white, tough membrane on either side; at this point insert your index finger behind the testicle.
Insert it between the white peritoneum that lies beneath the testicular skin and a second layer of peritoneum, releasing any adhesions behind the testicle; using your right hand push this inside the scrotal sac while stretching out the white membrane above with your left, then lift up both the testicle and its peritoneum towards the wound.
Instruct the servant to stretch the egg upwards, and you should release the adhesion from behind with a complete release. Then examine carefully with your finger to ensure there is no part of the twisted intestine in the tough white membrane; if you find any, push it downwards into the abdomen.
Take a needle with thick thread, which has been twisted from ten threads, and insert it at the end of the membrane that is under the scrotal skin next to the incision. Then cut the loop's ends so there are four threads. Overlap them in a rigid formation and tie the membrane, which we said was under the scrotal skin, tightly on both sides. Also wrap and tie the thread ends securely so that none of the vessels supplying it can be reached. To prevent any hot swelling from occurring, and to also create a second ligature outside the first one at a distance of less than two fingers away, leaving behind some of the membrane under the scrotal skin equivalent in length to a finger's width.
To prevent any hot swelling from occurring, and to also create a second ligature outside the first one at a distance of less than two fingers away, leaving behind some of the membrane under the scrotal skin equivalent in length to a finger's width. Cut away all that remains circumferentially, taking the egg with it; then make an incision below the scrotal skin to drain blood and pus as previously described. Use oil-soaked wool in the wound and apply the ligature we have described.
The tough white membrane we spoke of, after being cut carefully to avoid blood loss - which often occurs - can be cauterized. Leave the ligature until it falls off on its own; if this takes too long, moisten it with hot water and apply something that induces sweating until it comes off.
You then treat the wound with all the usual treatments for wounds until it heals.
Chapter 66.
In this treatment for rheumatism, I have not seen anyone daring enough to treat it with iron. The ancients mentioned that it should be done as described in the treatment for gangrene at the root of a grapevine: the vessels are tied off after being carefully opened below.
Then you open it up in the middle and treat the swelling with whatever opens it until the vessels fall out, then treating the wound as we mentioned until it heals.
Chapter 67.
On the hernia that is in the groin.
A hernia in the groin may occur, as we said before, with the protruding part remaining above the scrotum and nothing descending from the intestines, or if something does descend it is minimal and can be pushed back up at any time.
But if left untreated for too long, the hole in the peritoneum increases in size until part of the intestine and omentum descend into the scrotum. This happens due to the stretching of the peritoneum that is present in the groin as mentioned before. This is because the peritoneum stretches, then relaxes, causing a protrusion and swelling of the area. The treatment for this is cauterization as previously described.
It may also be treated with iron in the following manner: have the patient lie on their back between your hands, then cut across the swollen hernia three fingers wide and separate the peritoneum below the skin.
Until the white peritoneum that follows is exposed, you take a probe and place it on the protruding site of the peritoneum, pressing it into the depth of the abdomen. Then you suture the two protruding sites at the end of the probe with the peritoneum, adhering one to the other with the sutures.
Then you remove the end of the probe, but do not cut the peritoneum at all nor touch the testicle or anything else, as I have already instructed you in the treatment of intestinal hernia. Thereafter, you treat it with the treatment for surgical conditions.
If the sutures break, you clean them and dress the wound until it heals. The peritoneum contracts and does not increase the size of the hernia.
Cauterization is better in this location because it is closer to the safe area.
Chapter 68.
On the relaxation of the scrotal skin.
The skin of the scrotum often relaxes in some people without the flesh inside relaxing, and it is unsightly. Therefore, one who wishes to treat this should put the patient to sleep on their back.
All the skin that has relaxed is cut off, leaving only the thick skin behind. Then you bring together both lips of the scrotum; if you wish to suture first, do so with three or four sutures and ensure a tight seal before cutting between them.
Then you treat it with standard surgical procedures until it heals, at which point the sutures fall out.
Chapter 69.
On Castration.
Castration is forbidden in our religion.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book of mine; however, I did so for two reasons: first, to ensure that the doctor is aware of it if asked and knows how to treat such cases; second, we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Such as lambs, male goats and cats; thus I say castration can be performed in two ways: by crushing or by incision and cutting. In the first method of crushing, one should immerse the animal in hot water until its genitals relax, soften and droop.
Then crush its testicles by hand until they dissolve and cannot be felt anymore. As for castration by incision, one should hold the animal and squeeze the scrotum with their left hand, then tie up the spermatic cords and make a single cut over each testicle.
When the testicles protrude, cut them off after removing their coverings and leave only the thin tunica vaginalis over the vessels intact. This method of castration is better than the one involving crushing.
Because crushing may leave some of the testes behind, causing the animal to retain its sexual desire and want to mate; then one should treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On the Treatment of Hermaphrodites.
There are two types of hermaphrodites among men: the first is when a shape resembling a female genitalia appears under the scrotum or on the skin of the testicles, complete with hair; sometimes urine may flow from this area.
In women, there is one type [of hypospadias], located above the vulva on the pubic bone, similar to small male genitalia, protruding outside with one resembling a penis and both are like female genitals; treatment for the three types.
The two types in men and one type in women: those excess tissues should be cut off so that their effect is removed, then treat it with the treatment of other surgeries until they heal; as for the second type in men where urine exits from within the scrotal skin there is no action to take nor will it ever heal.
Chapter seventy-one.
On excision of the clitoris and labia from women's vulvas.
The clitoris may increase in size beyond what is normal until it is unsightly and its appearance becomes ugly; in some women, it can become enlarged like that of men's and be used for intercourse.
You should grasp the excess of the clitoris with your hand or a hook and cut it off, but do not cut too deeply, especially into its origin, lest you cause bleeding.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals; as for fleshy growth, this is flesh that grows in the mouth of the womb until it fills it.
And it may exit to the outside like a tail. For this reason, some of the ancients call it tail disease; you should cut it as you would cut a clitoris and treat it until it heals.
Chapter seventy-two.
On treating obstructions.
Obstructions can occur when a woman's vagina is not perforated or the perforation is small, either naturally at birth or as an acquired condition. This latter type results from a prior illness and may be due to an excessive growth of flesh.
A thin or thick membrane, situated either deep in the uterus, along its sides, at the top, or at the bottom can obstruct intercourse and pregnancy, as well as childbirth; it may even prevent menstruation. The midwife identifies this condition by visual inspection.
If the obstruction is visible and nearby, but not so if it's hidden, then examine it with your fingers or a probe. If the membrane is thin and close to the labia, you should proceed by making an incision; place protections over the labia and use both thumbs for this purpose.
With the woman lying on her back, legs spread apart, stretch open the labia with force to perforate the thin membrane and open up the obstruction; then take some wool soaked in oil and place it at the location of the incision. The woman should have intercourse daily to avoid a reunion of the tissue.
Should the membrane be thick and dense, you ought to incise it with a broad scalpel resembling an Asian fig leaf; if instead, it is caused by overgrown flesh, then suspend this tissue using hooks and cut it off. Ensure that you have medications on hand to stem bleeding without cauterization such as senna pods, gallnuts and frankincense combined in egg whites.
Afterwards, use a wide lead pipe to prevent the wound from healing too quickly; hold it in place for several days and employ a wick of dried linen thread. Treat with other medications that promote healing until the wound is fully closed. Other types of flesh growths may develop within the uterus which should be treated in an analogous manner.
Unless it is a cancerous tumor, one should never operate on a uterine carcinoma with an iron instrument.
Chapter Seventy-Three
On the treatment of hemorrhoids, warts, and red blisters that occur in women's genitalia.
Hemorrhoids are caused by the swelling of the veins, leading to continuous and excessive bleeding. When hemorrhoids progress, they become warts, which I have classified into types based on their characteristics.
Hemorrhoids and warts that are deep inside the uterus, beyond physical sensation, cannot be treated with metal instruments. However, those that occur at the opening of the uterus can be treated.
The woman should be placed in a cold room, and then the warts should be scraped with an iron instrument or rough cloth, cutting them off from their base. Then, to stop any bleeding, apply a styptic.
Such as alum, tartar, frankincense, and the like. Then her legs should be raised against a wall for an hour before being lowered into cold water. If bleeding persists, have her sit in a decoction of sumac, pomegranate rind, gallnut, and similar substances. Then take barley flour, knead it with honey and vinegar.
Apply this to her back. If the bleeding stops, you can cease treatment; if it continues, then apply cups to her spine and breasts without making incisions. Then take a woolen swab, dip it in juice of nettle or juice of plantain or juice of blackberry branches with a gallnut infusion, and apply it to the affected area.
If the swelling subsides, treat the area with ointment until it heals. As for the red blister, it resembles the heads of thorns and has a rough appearance; therefore, you should cut away what is visible from it as mentioned for warts, equally, and treat it until it heals.
Chapter 74.
Regarding suppuration that occurs in the womb.
There are many types of tumours that can occur in the womb, such as cancer, calcified tumour, ulcers, abscesses, gangrene, fistulas, haemorrhoids, fissures, warts and hot tumours. I have already mentioned all these diseases, their types and signs in the relevant section.
We should mention in this article the hot tumour that occurs in the womb, especially if it is one of those tumours that persists over time. How should its treatment with iron be carried out? You must examine whether the pain of the tumour at its onset is sharp, accompanied by throbbing, inflammation and fever.
If you see that it is red and sensitive to touch, do not rush its treatment with iron, but treat it with what will aid maturation. Once these symptoms subside, the woman should then sit on a chair with two arms and lean her back against them.
Bring her legs together towards the lower abdomen, with her thighs spread apart, and place her arms under her knees; then tie them with a suitable bandage.
The midwife should then sit at the woman's right side and use the instrument for opening the womb's mouth, which will be illustrated later. When using this instrument, you must estimate with a probe the depth of the woman's womb so as not to insert more of the instrument than the depth of the womb.
If the instrument causes pain to the patient, and if it is deeper than necessary, then some pads should be placed on the cervix to prevent the entire instrument from entering the uterus deeply. The spiral which runs along the instrument should be at its upper end.
While holding the instrument, your assistant should turn the spiral to open up the cervix of the uterus. If the abscess appears and it is soft when touched by hand then you should make an incision at its head with a wide lancet. When all the purulent discharge has come out.
You should introduce into the wound a soft wick soaked in rose oil or green liniment with some styptic properties. The end of the wick will be left outside of the incision on the cervix of the uterus and a piece of pure wool, also soaked in water that has been boiled with baker's yeast, is placed over the uterus and pubic region.
Then you will treat it after two days with the appropriate ointment until complete healing is achieved.
You may also wash the uterus and wound by injecting into it a mixture of water with honey, then another injection is made using water that has been boiled with liquorice root or long bugle. Afterwards, you will return to applying ointment; this procedure should be performed if the abscess is hidden within the uterus.
One should avoid treating it with iron, but rather one should treat it as previously mentioned in this section.
Chapter 75.
On the treatment of midwives regarding how they should care for live infants born with abnormal shapes.
The midwife should first be aware of the normal delivery process and its signs: if you see that the woman's bearing down, eager to take a deep breath, and finds some relief from what she is experiencing during childbirth.
It hastens the baby's exit, so know that this birth is in a natural manner and its exit is on its head with the placenta along or hanging from its navel, so when you see these signs then you should squeeze her belly to make the baby come out quickly.
If it comes down on its head, the placenta will come with it and cleanses from those impurities in a good manner, apart from this birth is unnatural and blameworthy because the baby can exit on its legs and bring out its hands before its head and legs.
Its one hand or leg comes out, or its head and a hand come out together, or it exits folded up, maybe even turned on its back and such shapes are blameworthy; the midwife should be skilled in all these things and should avoid any mistakes.
I have explained every shape and the required skill for each to help you understand it; when a baby exits in its natural way, on its head but gets stuck, and labor pains become difficult for the woman with her strength weakening; seat her on a chair and instruct women to hold her steady.
Compress her womb with cooked fenugreek and moist ointments, then the midwife inserts a small scalpel between her fingers to cut through the placenta or cuts it with her nail so that its moisture drains out; squeeze the woman's belly until the baby comes down.
If it doesn't come down, then you should inject the woman with fenugreek saliva mixed with linseed oil and after the injection tell her to roll over; make her sneeze with musk holding her mouth and nose tight so that the baby comes out immediately; if first its hands exit then they must be pushed back gently little by little.
If entry is not possible, place the woman on a pulpit and raise her legs upwards, then shake the pulpit against the ground with the woman holding herself steady to prevent falling during this shaking. If the hands do not enter (the birth canal) and the fetus dies, cut off both hands and extract the remaining parts of the fetus.
Or, tie his hands with a cloth and pull them gently; this will lead to the fetus being delivered. If the fetus emerges on its legs then you should push these upwards, before gradually rotating it until it is level, following which you can take one of its legs stretching this out little by little.
If the legs emerge at the hips, ask her to push and sniff musk perfume, for this will cause it to be delivered. If it is not discharged following our description above then turn the fetus little by little until you return it to its natural shape; it should come out smoothly now. Should all that we have described fail...
Take some saliva of the lizard, combined with oil of marjoram and linseed oil as well as gum dissolved in water; pound all these together very thoroughly before applying it to the woman's genitals and lower abdomen. Then seat her in a pool of tepid water up to her nipples.
If you see that her abdomen has softened then prepare a pessary of Myrrh, which she should hold for an hour; if it is retained over this period seat her on the chair before again sniffing musk perfume and plugging up both mouth and nostrils while squeezing her lower abdomen gently: the fetus will exit rapidly. If the fetus emerges on its knees and hands...
If it comes out in this manner then take hold of its hands, inserting these little by little; the woman should then lie on her back with her legs lowered while she lies supine upon a bed bringing both hands together before you gently squeeze over her abdomen. Then remove the pillows beneath her feet so that she is lowered, and if the infant does not come out then take both of her legs and move them vigorously, after which squeeze above the symphysis a little at a time until the baby rises up; then the midwife can insert her hand to help the birth along in small degrees.
Instruct the woman to roll over so that the baby may be delivered; when the infant emerges with one arm raised above its head, help it back into position by guiding its hand behind its neck. If you cannot manage this then have the woman stand and walk about for a while, failing which she should lie down again on her side.
Place her back on the bed and shake her legs vigorously before applying the ointment derived from saliva that I described earlier; should labour commence, have her sit down on a chair after which you may attempt to guide its hand and bring the infant into position as is natural for childbirth. You must also sneeze in the woman's face and instruct her to roll over so that she might give birth.
Should the infant emerge on its back with arms outstretched and facing away from its mother, the midwife must grasp one of its hands before gently twisting it around until it is in a better position for delivery; once this has been achieved, carry on as we have previously described using oil mixed with saliva.
Then, tell her to move a little at a time until she has managed it; seat her on the chair and have her lean towards her right side before telling her to roll over once more while you sneeze kohl into her face as this will cause the baby to emerge; when it comes out standing upright on one side.
If it reaches the mouth of the womb in this position along with its afterbirth then make a slit in said afterbirth using your fingernail to release any fluid that remains; if there is any still attached to the infant's face and neck, sever it from its navel so as not to choke it into death before pushing the child back inside until everything is in order.
Instruct the woman to push multiple times, for it facilitates an easy delivery of twins or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born and even three or four may be delivered, surviving in rarity; five is something unnatural, they never live at all.
Surrounding them, even if numerous, is a single placenta; they are separated by a membrane barrier between each, attached to the navel of every one. And it may be delivered as with a single fetus, an easy and natural birth or else an abominable delivery as described above.
Should any of the fetuses become difficult during birth and assume unnatural shapes, treat as described above. Let the midwife be gentle, acting with care and patience; she must employ every trick at her disposal to ensure a safe delivery in each situation.
An embryo may be conceived in the womb, one or two or three or four; even five, six and seven are possible, as well as ten or more. It has been said to me that a woman miscarried seven fetuses while another lost fifteen, all having been conceived together.
As decreed by the All-Mighty and All-Knowing, all that has been described ought to be in a midwife's knowledge; for it is possible such situations may one day arise, thus she must be prepared as necessary.
Chapter 76.
On the extraction of a dead fetus.
If you have treated the dead fetus as mentioned previously, but it does not come out and your only option is to proceed manually, then observe this: if the woman is strong and without any symptoms that may cause her harm, she must lie down on a bed on her back.
Its head should be tilted downward and its legs raised, with a servant holding it on both sides or it can be tied to the bed so that her body does not pull away during the extraction of the fetus.
Then the mouth of her womb is moistened with slippery ointments, made from the saliva of camel's snout, fenugreek and flax seeds. Then the midwife anoints her hand with these ointments and introduces it gently into the womb seeking a place to insert the hooks in the fetus, at suitable spots.
If you see that the fetus is about to descend head first, insert the hooks in its eyes or at the back of its neck, or in its mouth, jaw, under the chin, shoulder blades, near the ribs, or underneath its breastbone.
But if it is about to descend feet first, insert the hooks at the pubic region and middle ribs. The hook should be held by the right hand with its curve nestled between the fingers of your left hand; you then introduce it gently into one of those spots we mentioned earlier.
You continue to advance until you reach a vacant spot, then insert another hook opposite the first or even a third if needed so that pulling is equal and not biased towards one side; when extracting, do it in a smooth movement but with some rocking motion from side to side.
Just as it is done with a molar when extracting, you should keep the pulling steady; if one side gets stuck, the midwife should anoint her fingers and introduce them from that direction in order to turn whatever got stuck; once part of the fetus has come out, transfer the hooks to other spots.
It is a little thinner, do this until the entire fetus emerges; if one hand comes out before the other and cannot be pushed back due to compression, it should be wrapped with a cloth then pulled until when all of it has come out, cut it.
Either at the wrist joint or at the elbow, and thus you should do with the other hand and legs; if the fetus's head is large and there is pressure in coming out, or if there is water accumulated in its head, then you must insert a sharp probe between the fingers.
With it, you split the head and water comes out; or you crush it with an instrument called a 'mushdad', which is pictured alongside other instruments in the chapter following this one.
This is what you do when the fetus has a naturally large head; you must split open or crush its skull with the 'mushdad', as we have said, and then extract the bones with forceps. If the head comes out but gets compressed at the collarbone, it should be opened up so that the moisture in the chest can drain away.
In such a case, the chest will close up; but if it does not close and the body cannot be delivered whole, then cut it into pieces as best you can. If the lower belly is protruding or there is dropsy in it, an incision must be made so that moisture drains out.
If the fetus is in a position where its legs are leading, it will be easy to pull and present at the mouth of the womb; but if compression occurs at the belly or chest, you must pull with a cloth wrapped around your hand and split open the belly or chest so that their contents may drain out.
If all the other parts are removed and only the head remains inside, then the midwife should insert her left hand. If the cervix is open, she should reach in with her hand to grasp the head, pulling it towards the mouth of the uterus with her fingers. Then, she should introduce a hook or two into the uterine cavity and use them to pull out the head.
If the cervix has closed due to hot inflammation, then it should not be forced open. Instead, moist and oily substances should be applied topically, along with poultices. The woman should also sit in warm water that relaxes and softens the tissues.
If the fetus is lying on its side, and it can be repositioned to a normal position, then the methods described earlier for live fetuses should be employed. However, if this cannot be done, then the fetus must be cut into pieces and removed. It is important that no part of the placenta remains inside. Once you have completed your treatment, follow the instructions mentioned earlier for treating hot inflammation in the uterus.
If there is any bleeding, the woman should be immersed in astringent water and all other treatment methods should be applied. I have witnessed a case where a woman became pregnant while still carrying a dead fetus within her womb, which had remained undelivered after its death during an earlier pregnancy.
Then, the second fetus also died and remained undelivered within her for a long time until an ulcer developed at the site of the womb's orifice, becoming swollen to the point where it ruptured and began discharging pus. I was called upon to treat this condition, which required prolonged treatment over many months without any signs of healing. Finally, I applied one of the strongest astringent ointments available, which succeeded in drawing out all the matter from the ulcer.
A bone was expelled from the orifice, which surprised me greatly since bones do not normally develop within the abdomen. I concluded that these must have been the remains of a dead fetus and proceeded to thoroughly examine the wound, extracting many fragments of bone belonging to the skull of this fetus.
I have not ceased to follow that until I removed a lot of bones from it, and the woman is in her best condition. She has lived like this for some time with just a little pus coming out of the wound, and so I bring you this rare case here.
Because it contains knowledge and assistance for what the practicing physician attempts with his hands in treatment.
Chapter Seventy-Seven.
In it there are illustrations of the tools required to extract the fetus.
Illustration of a spiral used to open the mouth of the womb.
This book press, which is the same as those used to bind books, also has two spirals at each end of the wooden bars; however, this spiral should be more delicate than that of the book press and should either be made from ebony or boxwood. Each wooden bar should have a diameter equal to that of two fingers.
And the ends of each bar are curved like a finger; their length is one and half spans, while in the middle of both wooden bars there are two pegs made from the same wood as the bars with lengths slightly more than half a span and widths also slightly over that of two fingers: these are the pegs.
These are the two pegs that should be inserted into the mouth of the womb in order to open it when you rotate both spirals. Illustration of another tool for this same purpose, but which is more delicate and lighter.
It should be made from ebony or boxwood and fashioned like a set of tongs, although at the end it has two pegs as you can see; each peg is around one span in length and two fingers wide. If you wish to open the womb with these then...
Place the woman on a bed, with her legs suspended and parted between her thighs. Then insert these two probes, clasped together, into the mouth of the womb while holding the instrument's handle below, between her thighs. Thereafter, open your hand with the instrument as you would with forceps, equally on either side to the extent that you wish to dilate the womb.
