Therefore, do not consider any instrument too insignificant to have on hand, as it may well prove necessary.
Chapter 88.
On the extraction of the placenta.
If the placenta is retained after childbirth, you should instruct the patient to hold her breath and then make her sneeze with incense; press your hand over her mouth and nostrils. If it comes out by doing this, otherwise take a bowl.
And make a hole in the lid and place inside it herbs that open the mouth of the womb, such as fennel, wormwood, dill, chamomile, sage, ground ivy, and centaury; any or all of these medicines. Soak them with water and set this over fire; then place a silver tube onto the hole in the lid of the bowl, with its other end inside the mouth of the womb.
Hold this until the steam reaches the bottom of the womb, and then make her sneeze as we have said; the placenta will come out quickly. If it still remains after this treatment and does not come out, instruct the midwife to dip her left hand in sesame oil or slobberwort saliva.
Then she should insert her hand into the vagina and search for the placenta; if she finds it, grasp it and pull it out slowly. If it is stuck deep inside the womb, then insert your hand as described earlier until you find the placenta; gently tug on it little by little to remove it.
To avoid tearing the womb during extraction, it should be gently rocked from side to side before increasing the pulling force; this will help free it from any adhesions. If the mouth of the womb is closed, we have described treatments involving sneezing, the bowl method, and herbs.
If it does not come out despite all these methods, do not be violent with her and pull forcefully; instead, tie what has emerged back to the mouth of the womb and then onto the woman's thigh. Afterward, inject her with the quadruple ointment as this will cause it to rot within a few days.
It dissolves and comes out, but if it gets infected then a foul smell rises to the stomach and head causing discomfort; therefore, suitable millet should be used.
Hence, some ancients experimented with millet of barley and dried figs. Illustration: The device which a woman uses to inhale vapors when menses or placenta is retained.
It is made of lead resembling a funnel or it can be made of copper, the narrow end is placed in the vagina while the wide end rests on fire and incense put on hot coals; she holds until all that incense burns out then replaces with more.
Chapter 79.
Treating fistula without perforation.
Many boys are born with their anus un-perforated, sealed by a thin membrane; the midwife should pierce this membrane with her finger otherwise she must cut it with a sharp scalpel taking care not to touch the muscle.
She should then place a pad soaked in wine and oil over it, treat with ointments until healed; if you fear that it may close again, insert a lead pipe into the hole for many days, remove when child needs to defecate.
Sometimes, the anus may become blocked due to a healed wound or tumor; it is necessary to cut this scar then treat as mentioned with ointments and cauterization.
Chapter 80.
Treating fistulas occurring in the lower part of the body.
The fistulae that occur below are a complication and thickening that happen near the anus, from outside or within the space on one of the sides, and there may be one or more fistula.
If this complication persists, it opens up and discharges a watery, white moisture or thin pus. Some of these fistulae may extend to the anus and intestines; those that do not can be identified by what emerges from them when the patient defecates: faeces and wind.
Worms may also emerge from them, and there are fistulae that arise when those in the space extend to the bladder and urethra; others extend to the hip joint and coccyx. The fistula extending to the anus can be identified in a similar manner to one not doing so.
Insert your index finger into the anus and a thin probe of copper or iron into the fistula; if there is no bend, but one is present, use a thin lead probe or horsehair instead.
Until you feel the probe or hair on your finger, and if nothing comes out of the hole at all - neither faeces nor wind nor worms, as we have said - then know it is not extending elsewhere and proceed with treatment.
If the fistula extends to the bladder or urethra, it is indicated by urine emerging from it and the failure of any medication to heal the site; if extending to the hip joint or coccyx, the probe will reach these places.
If there is no bending toward the bone, pain in that area, constant discharge of pus, failure to respond to any treatment or ointment, and no healing, then these extending fistulae will never be cured.
The treatment is a futile effort for those ignorant physicians who attempt to operate on it, but for the non-penetrating and non-chronic wound healing can be expected with the use of iron as I will describe and have experienced it: The patient lies down in front of you on their back.
You should raise his legs and bend his thighs towards the belly, then introduce a copper or lead sound until you know where it ends by feeling it with your patient's hand near the anus; if he feels it at the anus, you must introduce your index finger into the anus.
If you feel the sound passing into your finger, without feeling any peritoneum or flesh between them then be certain that it has perforated and there is no need to tire yourself over it as such a wound cannot heal as we have said.
They say that it can heal in rare cases and one treatment of benefit is to heat a thin iron according to the width of the fistula, as described before, then introduce it hot into the fistula up towards the anus two or three times.
Until you know that all the excess hardened flesh like a bird's feather tube has been burnt, then treat it with twisted wool soaked in lard until those burned tissues are removed; afterward use emollient ointments and healing can be expected.
If this is not the case and it does not pass into your finger in the anus but rather there is a thick curtain of flesh or peritoneum between them and you see that the fistula follows a course under the skin, then make an incision starting at its external opening. As you pass the incision with the probe while it is in the fistula until you reach with the incision where the end of the probe has reached, and the probe comes out and falls away, then clean those coagulated meats which resemble a bird's quill without leaving anything from them.
Then you treat it with emollient salves until it heals, but if the blood dominates and prevents you from carrying out your work of cutting those meats, then what is best for you to do and quickest in terms of benefit is cauterization by fire or a sharp medicine because cauterization with fire gathers together two good conditions: burning away that excess meat.
And cut off the blood and dry up the moistures, then treat it with wet bandages dipped in suet or sulfur powder mixed with oil until the wound suppurates and expels that burnt meat in the pus; then treat the wound at this point with emollient salves promoting hard flesh.
These are the medicines in which there is astringency and drying up, for when it has healed then it will have knitted together; do not stuff any packing into this fistula. If the fistula should reach deeply within the anus and be far from the surface of the body, then insert your finger inside the anus and grope about with it.
If you feel the probe and there is a membrane or meat between it, and it is close to emerging, then acting upon this fistula must be done in a way that is greedy and hopeful: that is, you should use one of three approaches either cauterize it as we have said.
Or you could incise it until you reach its bottom so that you can securely insert the bandages and treat from nearby; do not go too far with the incision lest you cut through the muscle surrounding the anus, resulting in loss of control over defecation for the patient, then treat as we mentioned; perhaps he will heal as said.
Either you pierce that membrane after cutting it close to the anus, as we said, either with a sound or with another sharp instrument, and then clean all those coagulated meats that are in the incision, as far as possible.
Then you suture the entire wound along with the incision and the upper orifice so that it is sealed, while keeping the fistula open inside the anus, which will be less burdensome for the patient; and sometimes the fistula may be punctured in this manner.
And that is, if you introduce the sound into the fistula and it comes out on the side of the anus towards the surface of the body along with the skin and the orifice of the anus, then take a sound perforated at the end like a cobbler's needle in this manner:
Then introduce into it a twisted thread made of five threads or so, then introduce the sound with the thread into the fistula until you reach its bottom; if it is perforated at the rim of the anus from within in its vicinity.
Then extract the thread through that hole by introducing your finger into the anus and pulling out the end of the thread; then gather both ends together, drawing them tight for a day or two days; each time the thread is cut in the meat, you tighten it well so that those meats between the two ends of the threads fall away; then treat the wound until it heals and recovers.
If the mouth of the fistula is not perforated, then perforate it as best you can, though it should not be too deep so that you do not cut through the muscle; then proceed with it as we have mentioned until it recovers.
The image of the spinal knife used to make fistulas. One side is very sharp, and the other is not so that you do not cut what is not needed, as you see.
Chapter 81.
On the treatment of haemorrhoids, their ligation and healing of anal fissures.
Haemorrhoids come in two forms, they can either be inside the anus like red blisters or similar to grapes with some being small and others large and bleeding constantly.
They can be singular or multiple, and they can occur outside the anus at its edges; however, those that are found on the exterior of the anus are usually less moist, with a yellowish discharge or slight continuous bleeding and their colour is similar to the skin.
The treatment for those that are found inside the anus is to have the patient defecate and evacuate their bowels until the anus emerges and the lesions become visible; then, you quickly attach them with hooks or grasp them with your fingernail before cutting at their base. If they do not hold together due to moisture and relaxation...
...scrape it with a rough cloth or a piece of coarse bread, or pull at it with your fingers and then cut it; afterwards, sprinkle some sharp powders on it to act as cauterization or treat it according to what has been previously mentioned in the chapter on cauterization.
Then treat it with the remaining treatments until it heals; if the anus does not come out, inject the patient with an enema containing a slight sting to wash out its contents and encourage quick evacuation when the patient defecates. As for those haemorrhoids that are external, they are easily managed.
It is to take it with your fingernail or hook it with a hook and cut it off, then treat it as we mentioned until it heals. For those who dislike cutting with iron, you should use this piercing method: Take some spun thread and insert it into a needle.
Then pull the wart upwards and pass the needle through its base from the other side. Wrap both ends of the thread under the needle, which should be held horizontally, pulling the thread tight around the wart's neck until it is removed. Then tie a knot in the thread and remove the needle. Do this for all warts except one, which you leave unpierced.
Let it drain its excess blood, then place on the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cypress tar and put it on the anus, instructing the patient to remain still. Leave them until the warts fall off; once they do, treat with ointments and other treatments until healed.
As for fissure treatment, it often arises from constipation and a dry temperament. If chronic and unresponsive to medication, you should excise it with a scalpel or your fingernail until it becomes moist and the upper layer of skin, which prevents healing, is removed.
Then treat it until it heals properly; if not, repeat the excision more vigorously than before so that it bleeds well and is peeled off. Then treat it again; it should heal.
Chapter 82.
On the treatment of inverted and non-inverted corns, hard warts, and moles.
The callus is a round nodule, the color of the body, resembling the head of a nail and can be found all over the body but especially on the soles of the feet and toes; it causes pain when walking.
The area around the callus should be cut open, then grasped with forceps or a hook and removed from its root; you should treat it accordingly, and if you wish to cauterize it, do so as previously explained in the appropriate section. As for dry warts, they are small hard bumps that feel rough like a knot, round on top of the body's surface.
They often appear on the hands of boys, and to treat them you should pull or cut off the warts, or pierce it with a silk thread or hair until it falls off; if you wish, you can cauterize it as previously mentioned, either with fire or sharp medicine.
You can also remove inverted and non-inverted warts with the same instrument I mentioned for treating corns; as for corns, they are small rough nodules on the body's surface that go very deep, and if exposed to cold, they feel like being stung by ants.
They can occur anywhere on the body, but are most common on the hands; to treat them you should take a tube made from goose quill or strong eagle feathers and fit it over the corn so that it surrounds it completely, then rotate your hand holding the tube until what's around the corn is cut open.
You should insert the tube deep into the flesh, then remove the corn from its root; if you wish, you can do this with a copper or iron pipe in the same manner:
Make the top of the tube solid and tightly twisted so that it can be easily manipulated and twisted by the fingers; if you wish, cauterize the mole as explained in the section on wart cauterization in the chapter on cauterization.
Chapter 83.
Pictures of instruments used for injections in cases of piles, diarrhea, and colic.
The syringe can be made of silver, porcelain, or hollow or solid brass, and this instrument can be made in small and large sizes depending on who will use it. So those which are used to treat small boys will be small, and for those with narrow or painful anuses their syringes will be very gentle. Picture of a large syringe:
Its upper funnel, to which the bladder is attached, is wide like this; it has a barrier where the bladder is tied as you can see, and its lower end, which is inserted into the anus, is very smooth and solid with two holes on one side and one hole on the other.
The size of the hole should be as thick as a rod or slightly thicker, and the bladder in which you put the medicines can be made from an animal's bladder or sheep gut; it is shaped like a small pillow and its diameter is one palm-width.
It is pierced with many holes in a spiral pattern and the distance between each hole should be one finger's width; then pass through those holes a strong thread of ten-ply yarn or similar, and gather up the bladder like a pillow. When you have put the medicine inside, tie off the head of the syringe tightly between two stones and then inject with it. This is a picture of the circle of the bladder and holes:
And the method of clyster treatment is to fill the rectum or bladder with medicine, which has been heated to a temperature that the patient can tolerate. Take care not to make it very hot or very cold. Then have the patient lie on their back and lift their legs up.
A board or a cloth is placed underneath, and the tip of the syringe is greased with oil, egg white, marshmallow saliva, or something similar. Then gently insert it into the anus, grasp the bulb with both hands, squeeze vigorously until all the contents of the syringe have been emptied into the bowels.
Remove the syringe and have the patient lift their legs to the wall. If the medicine stimulates a desire for defecation, try to hold it in as much as possible; if they sleep through the whole night, so much the better for the treatment. But if they are given an enema in cases of diarrhea and wish to hurry up its evacuation, let them repeat the enema until they are cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or painful swellings in the anus, let the syringe be very smooth and gentle, rather than those used for enemas on children. Here is a picture of a gentle syringe with its cover attached.
Chapter Eighty-Four.
On the Treatment of Wounds.
I have already discussed, in the introduction, the treatment of wounds which resemble cauterizations; and I shall now treat them with the hand or medicines perfectly. Wounds differ according to the object that causes them and also according to the part of the body affected.
The objects which may cause wounds are many, such as the blow of a stone or the cut of a sword, knife, spear, stick or arrow; and there is also a great variety of other things.
And regarding surgical procedures according to the body part, it can be like a wound on the head or neck or chest or abdomen or liver and similar organs.
And I shall describe the treatment of some wounds to make them a measure and a rule for all other wounds, and I start with simple head wounds in particular because compound ones will be mentioned in the third chapter of this book.
So I say, when a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no bone fracture, then examine it. If it is due to a blow from a stone or the like and has split the skin only, and the wound is large, and you fear the patient might develop hot swelling, then hasten and lance it at the site of impact. Do not delay this, and remove blood from him according to his strength if there is no hindrance to cupping, especially if the blood that comes out of the wound is minimal. Let the cupping be done on the opposite side of the injured part.
Warn the patient against overeating and drinking, and avoid heavy foods. If there is an obstacle to cupping, let him reduce his food intake, stay away from meats, and not drink alcohol. Apply to the wound, if hot swelling occurs, a cotton pad soaked in rose oil alone or with astringent wine.
If you are not concerned about hot swelling, then apply to the wound, if it is fresh and its blood has not dried, the powdered medication that I will describe soon. But if the air has caused a change [in the wound], then treat it with some ointments.
Until pus comes out, then treat it until it heals. If the wound is large and due to a sword cut or the like, and its lips do not close with sutures, then bring them together by stitching as I will describe in the chapter on abdominal wounds.
If the skin is exposed and attached to the bone, but its grip is only in a small flap, cut it off. Then treat the wound with medicines that will heal it into solid flesh instead of the skin, and instruct the patient to nourish themselves with foods like broths and purees that are strengthening.
If the skin becomes gangrenous and is not attached to the bone, cut away all of it and discard it; then treat the wound with medicines that will heal it into solid flesh instead. If any artery or vein bleeds from these wounds and bleeding cannot be stopped by drugs alone, search for the artery itself.
If you hit it and it does not fall away on its own, sever the artery with a scalpel and tie it off; if necessary bind it. If what we have described above is of no use to stop bleeding, cauterize it until the blood flow stops. These small wounds are simple matters that require little treatment beyond covering them in powder while they still bleed.
Before it is exposed to air, the powder should be made from one part frankincense, two parts styrax and three parts quicklime or unslaked lime; pound them all together with a pestle and sieve until fine, then fill the wound and bind firmly so that it adheres well.
It will dry in the blood and then bind it on top with bandages; leave them so that they are not removed while there is no swelling, for the medicine shall not be washed away until it heals. Lime alone can do this also if you cannot find frankincense or styrax.
Sometimes caustic soda can be used for this purpose in many large wounds, provided it is carefully placed and bound; as long as the wounds are still fresh with blood and have not yet been exposed to air. However, if they have changed somewhat, especially during summer, then this should not be attempted.
There is no effective meaning in placing this poultice so treat it as we have mentioned, by applying some mature salves or a gruel made of barley flour with water and honey until the pus drains out. Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
If a fracture occurs in the bone along with the wound and it is minor, extract it using forceps; I have mentioned the treatment of skull fractures later on, under neck surgery. If the wound occurs in the neck, there is no difference between its treatment and that of a simple head wound.
But if a nerve in the neck has been severed, there is no remedy other than to avoid treating it with anything that causes severe constriction such as zinc vitriol and lead salts, for they harm the nerve and cause it to spasm.
Nothing cold should be applied to the wound, for the nature of the nerve is cold and it connects with the brain which is the noblest organ; treat it with gentle medicines such as slaked lime washed repeatedly in fresh water, moistened with oil or rosewater, and tutty.
If you also wash it with fresh water and the other metallic stones in this manner, and use moist salves, but if the wound is large then use stitches or bind its lips together with bandages, then treat it until it heals.
If the wound is deep and there is a hollow space beneath it where pus has accumulated, cauterize it in its lowest part. If an artery is severed in the wound and you see blood escaping from it, cut it off, or tie it tight, or cauterize it if need be.
If the wound has cut through some or all of the tracheal rings, but the vessels remain intact, then gather together the edges of skin with stitches around the trachea and do not touch it; instead, smooth it over and return it to its natural shape. Then apply tight bandages, leaving them on for several days.
Afterwards, treat it with ointments containing astringent and drying properties until healing occurs. If the wound has affected the bone, then search for any bone fragments; if there are some, extract all of them or as many as you can reach. Whatever cannot be reached, leave to rot within the wound so that healing may occur and their removal becomes easier.
If the wound is fresh, then dust it with a powder as described previously. If bone fragments cause decay within the wound, then treat this as an abnormal fistula and refer to the section on chest surgery and the area between the shoulders for further instructions.
If the wound is caused by a spear or knife and you observe a cavity, examine it to see if air escapes from it during the patient's exhalation; this would indicate a fatal injury. However, if there is no visible cavity but the wound remains fresh with blood present, do not dust it immediately nor apply bandages tightly, for fear of trapping blood within the cavity.
In such a case, the blood would rush back to the heart and cause the patient's death. To avoid this, apply an attractive ointment and dissolve it twice daily. If no such ointment is available, place an old piece of cotton in the wound to absorb any moisture that escapes from it; also ensure the patient sleeps with their injury elevated.
If the wound has persisted for three days or more without inducing convulsions, severe palpitations, labored breathing, or other negative symptoms in the patient and their overall condition remains satisfactory, then assume the injury is not life-threatening. Proceed to treat it with cauterization or any other appropriate means until healing occurs.
If healing is impossible and the swelling persists, then know that it has become a fistula. Treat it from its opening; let the patient rest until the wound drains and whatever collects within it.
Whether it is large or small, if it has affected the bone and broken off fragments, then explore the wound and proceed to extract those fragments if they are free. If not, leave them be for now.
If the wound becomes infected, it will be easier to remove them [the fragments]. As for other wounds that occur in various body parts, their treatment follows the rules we have mentioned.
Chapter eighty-five.
On abdominal wounds and the evisceration of intestines, as well as their suturing.
A wound in the abdomen may be large, small or of medium size. A large wound may result in the evisceration of intestines, making their reinsertion and suturing more challenging and complex.
A small wound can also present challenges in a different way, requiring an even more difficult reinsertion of the intestines. If they are not promptly returned to their proper place, the intestines will swell and thicken, making reinsertion more difficult. Therefore, among these three types of wounds, the medium-sized one is considered preferable as it does not complicate the return of the intestines in the same way as the other two types.
Keep in mind that there are four types of suturing techniques for the abdomen: two general methods suitable for abdominal and other bodily wounds, while the other two specialized methods can be used for various wounds but are particularly useful for the abdomen.
And I shall describe it to you, one by one, with explanation and clarification: if the wound is small and something of the intestine comes out, and it is difficult to push back, that may be for one of two reasons; either due to the small size of the hole, as we said, or because the intestine has become inflated beforehand due to cold air.
So, if that is the case, you should warm it by dipping a wet sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or you may have cooked in it *Artemisia herba-alba*, *Teucrium polium* and *Achillea santolina*, and wipe the intestine with it until the inflation subsides.
Alternatively, you may use a drink that contains astringent properties, which is better than water alone in reducing the inflation. Once you see that the inflation has subsided, moisten the intestine with water that has been cooked with *Oenanthe crocata* or *Foeniculum vulgare*, as this will facilitate its re-entry with minimal effort.
If, after this treatment, it is still impossible to return [the intestine], make a small incision in the hole with an instrument similar to that used for making fistulas. The first method of treatment, if successful, is preferable over incision, and resorting to such an incision should only be done out of necessity and after all other methods have been exhausted. This is an illustration of the said instrument:
One side of it is curved and limited, while the other side is unlimited; the thin end should not be as sharp as a scalpel, but rather slightly blunt like a nose. This instrument resembles a small mallet, as you can see in the illustration. If the wound has become enlarged and the intestine has entered, its re-positioning must be done carefully to restore it to its natural shape.
If possible, the intestine should be returned to its original position in the abdomen. However, if the hole is wide and located in the lower part of the abdomen, the patient should lie on their back with their legs elevated higher than their head. If it is in the upper abdomen, then they should elevate their head and chest above their feet.
And likewise, if the incision is on one side of the abdomen, your aim and purpose should always be to elevate that side where surgery is performed more than the other side. Thus it should be employed in both major and minor surgeries.
As for minor surgeries, place it as you see fit, then have a gentle servant ready at your hands to hold the incision with both his hands and gather his lips together before revealing one piece after another to the surgeon.
This is the description of a single common stitch, which involves taking one needle or several needles according to the size of the wound and leaving an amount equal to the thickness of your little finger from the edge of the incision before inserting a single needle without any thread into both sides of the skin. On both sides of the peritoneum beneath the skin from inside so as to penetrate it from that side, having gathered together both edges of the skin and both edges of the peritoneum thus forming four folds before pulling a doubled thread around the needle several times on both sides until the lips of the wound meet firmly.
Then leave a portion equal to the thickness of your index finger and insert another needle, threading it as you did with the first one so that you keep doing this according to how many needles are needed until you complete sewing up the entire wound, ensuring that the needles are neither too thick nor too thin.
As very thin needles quickly cut through the flesh and thick ones are also hard to insert into the skin, they should be of medium thickness. The needle should not be inserted too close to the edge of the skin.
So that the flesh does not break off quickly and the wound opens before healing, but also do not space it too far apart with stitches so that the wound may refuse to heal. Then cut the ends of the needles lest they injure the patient in their sleep, and make him dressings from pieces of linen cloth on each side holding the ends of the needles.
Leave it there until you know the wound has healed, and this type of stitching with needles is more suitable for small wounds as only one or two needles may be sufficient.
As for the method of common stitching, you gather together the four edges, that is to say, the two edges of skin and those of peritoneum in one go with a needle which has a twisted thread of moderate thickness then when you have passed through these four edges with the needle return it on the same side from which you started.
So that the thread is interwoven from above the wound to make the stitches like those on a sack used for lashing loads and you leave between each stitch as far as the thickness of the little finger; this kind of stitching all men know.
Using this method of stitching I sewed a wound in the belly of a man who had been stabbed with a knife and the gash was more than a span long; about two spans of the small intestine protruded from his gut, and the wound was right in the middle of his stomach.
I repositioned it after his gut had been protruding from the wound for twenty-four hours and the wound healed in about fifteen days; I treated him until he recovered completely, and afterwards he lived for many years, managing all his affairs normally; the doctors had said that there was no hope of recovery at all.
It is strange that I did not treat it with ointment, for I was in a place where there were no medicines; so I would apply to the wound twice daily old cotton wool and make sure to wash it with honey water until it healed.
As for one of the two types of special suturing, I quote Galen's description: you begin by stitching from outside the skin and push the needle inwards; when it has passed through the skin and into the straight muscle that runs the whole length of the belly.
Leaving the peritoneum margin on the side where you inserted the needle, push it through its other edge from inside to outside at the opposite margin of the ribs; when you have done this, push it again through this same margin of the ribs from outside inwards.
Leave the peritoneum margin on this side and push it through its other edge from inside to outside; as you do so, pierce also with the needle the peritoneum towards the ribs margin on that same side until they are all pierced.
Then start once more from this same side and line up with the margin of peritoneum on the other side; bring the needle out through the skin close by, then return it to that same skin and follow along the peritoneum margin on the other side together with this ribs margin.
Bring the needle out through that same skin; do this again, once more after another time until you have sutured the whole wound in this way. The characteristic feature of this type of suturing is that it resembles the stitching of furriers when sewing furs together.
By sewing the peritoneum once from one side with the skin edge and leaving it on the other side until you are finished, while for the second type of specialized stitching he mentioned as well Galen this is his words exactly.
And that you stitch it as some healers do, by bringing each part together with its corresponding natural counterpart: joining the edge of the peritoneum to its other edge and the rib cage's edge to its other edge, which is done as I describe to you.
You should insert the needle into the rib cage's edge near you from outside, passing it through to the inside and leaving the peritoneum edges; then you bring back the needle, pass it from outside to inside through both peritoneum edges, and pull it out as well.
Pass it through from inside to outside into the other rib cage edge on the opposite side; this type of stitching is better than the simple, easy stitching mentioned earlier - these are Galen's words exactly; some have said that abdominal stitching can be done with two needles. That is, you pass a single thread through both of them and start by passing the first needle in from your side to the other side, then insert the other needle from its opposite side towards yours, stitching it like cobblers do.
Know that if there is a hole in the center of the abdomen, it will be more difficult to stitch than other areas of the abdomen; as for treating the wound, its treatment follows the rules of all wounds: when you catch the fresh wound with blood still inside before air changes it, return the intestine, stitch and secure it firmly, then apply healing powders.
If the wound has been affected by air, apply some of the available ointments until it becomes inflamed and the stitches fall out, and the abdominal wall and flanks unite. Then treat it as you would other wounds until it heals.
If you do not have medications available, apply old cotton wool from the start of inflammation and change it twice a day as I instructed until it heals. You will not need further treatment in most cases if the wounds are simple, but if you fear that nearby vital organs may become involved with the wound's pain.
You should dip soft wool in moderately warm oil or rose oil and place it around the areas between the groin and armpit. If they feel pain or infection in their intestines, this often indicates an issue, so inject them with a tepid black astringent drink.
Especially if the infection has reached the intestines and become an open wound penetrating its cavity, note that anything involving the thicker intestine is easier to heal whereas issues with thinner intestine are more challenging.
As for the intestine known as the small bowel, it does not heal from surgical wounds due to its many large veins and delicate structure close to nerve tissue. However, if what protrudes from the wound is the omentum and you catch it while still fresh, replace it according to how you would restore the intestines.
If a period has passed and it has turned green or black, you should bind it with thread above the discolored area to prevent bleeding as there are veins and arteries in the omentum. Then cut off anything below that binding.
Make the ends of the thread hang from below the incision, extending out of it so that you can easily pull and remove them when the entrails fall out and the wound becomes infected; he mentioned the wound that occurs in the intestines, but if there is a rupture in the intestine and it is small...
It can heal in some people, because I saw a man who had been wounded in the abdomen by a spear thrust and the wound became chronic, turning into a fistula from which faeces and wind issued; so I treated him even though I did not expect a cure. I kept consoling him until he was cured and the wound closed; when I saw that the wound had healed, I feared for the patient that something bad might happen inside his body, but no such misfortune ever befell him, and he remained in perfect health. He was healthy, eating and drinking, having intercourse and going to the bathroom; he could perform his duties without difficulty, and some experienced people have said that if there is a small wound in the intestine it should be sutured this way.
This is done by taking large-headed ants, then bringing together the edges of the wound; an ant with its mouth open is placed on the lips of the wound and if it bites and tightens its jaws, its head is cut off because this ensures adhesion; another ant is placed next to the first one.
You keep doing this with several ants according to the size of the wound, then you close it up and suture the wound; those heads will remain stuck to the intestine until it heals completely, without any side effects on the patient's health; it may also be possible to suture the intestines with fine thread.
The procedure to remove the animal intestine adhered to it [the needle] after insertion is as follows: take one end of this thread from the intestine and pull carefully, then tie a fine spun linen thread to its end and insert that thread into the needle. When the intestine thread is present, it can be used to suture the intestines and then returned to the abdomen. This type of suturing with ants and intestines is done out of greed and hope.
However, if the wound is large and wide, especially in one of the thin intestines, there is no remedy and no cure for it whatsoever.
Chapter 86.
On the treatment of rhinitis and fistula.
Know that any wound, swelling, or ulcer that becomes chronic, ages, and fails to heal, continually discharging pus without interruption is called a fistula in general, regardless of the affected organ. We refer to it as rhinitis, or the fistula.
It is in reality a solid, white, hard conglomeration without pain that has a cavity like the cavity of a bird's feather.
This is why some have called it a feather. Sometimes it can be moist, continuously discharging pus; at other times the moisture may stop temporarily. This discharge can be abundant or scant; thick or thin.
As mentioned earlier in the classification of wounds, any ulcer that fails to heal or develop flesh has one of nine possible causes: insufficient blood supply in the body; poor quality of blood; or the presence of hard tissue inside and on its lips, preventing good flesh from growing. Either it is due to excessive pus and dirt, or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter sustaining it is of poor quality, or because the medicine used for its treatment is not appropriate, or due to an epidemic outbreak in the town, or a specific peculiarity of that particular town.
Either it is due to excessive pus and dirt, or because the ulcer itself is putrid and the matter sustaining it is of poor quality, or because the medicine used for its treatment is not appropriate, or due to an epidemic outbreak in the town, or a specific peculiarity of that particular town. As observed in the city of Zaragoza, where diseases take a long time to mature and wounds heal slowly by nature; or it could be because there is one bone or several bones involved. I have explained all these causes, their signs, and treatments with medicines in the appropriate section.
Here, I must acquaint you with the treatment of fistulas and colds that involve manual intervention. Fistulas can occur in all parts of the body; some end at major veins or arteries while others terminate on nerves.
Some fistulas open into the peritoneum, intestines, bladder, ribs, vertebrae of the spine, or complex joints like those in the hand and foot. Others terminate near vital organs.
Those fistulas that have such characteristics as mentioned above are among the difficult-to-heal diseases, which do not respond to treatment and attempting to treat them is futile ignorance. However, if there are fistulas that do not reach these specific sites I've described, then there may be hope for their treatment.
If you wish to explore and gain knowledge about such fistulas, take a copper or iron probe. If the fistula runs straight, use this to examine it. But if it has bends, then use a thin lead probe as lead is more flexible.
When you enter [the fistula] and it turns towards the bend, if the fistula has multiple openings that you cannot detect with a sound, inject one of its mouths; the moisture you inject will reach the other orifices and flow out.
Start the investigation in any way possible to determine if there is bone or nerve, whether the bottom of the fistula is deep or shallow, or whether it has multiple openings; ascertain all this as best you can by asking about your patient's experience with pain when prodded at the site.
And other such signs and causes of the swelling, once you have a clear understanding of all this, then proceed with treatment confidently. Look to see if it is...
If the fistula is superficial and close to the surface or in a healthy area away from joints, nerves, arteries, veins, or other sensitive areas I mentioned earlier, then cut open the fistula as described above and remove any abscesses, rotten flesh, and excess tissue.
And any growths, such as flesh or warts that form at its opening, treat them until they heal. If the fistula is deep and straight, you should cut it open deeply as much as possible, then clean out all its rotten flesh.
Then use the caustic thread in sharp medicines and insert it to the bottom of the fistula that you can reach with an iron probe, do this repeatedly until the sharp medicine consumes all the corruption left at the base of the fistula.
Then treat it with salves that promote healthy flesh growth until it heals. If this does not work, cauterize it as previously described. If the cause of the fistula is bone and you are certain of this, then probe and cut it open as I told you unless there is a reason preventing you from doing so.
If a bone, nerve or vital organ is exposed as we said, and there is some corruption and blackening of the bone, scrape it clean until its corruption disappears then treat it with whatever will make it heal so that it may recover. If it does not heal and suppuration continues as before.
Know that you have not reached the complete removal of corruption, so uncover everything above it again and investigate its scraping and purification to your utmost ability then force [the issue], if it heals otherwise know that this corruption is in a far away depth so uncover all that is above it once more.
And investigate its scraping and purification to your utmost ability, if the corrupted bone is a small one and you can pull it out then pull it with fine forceps suitable for that purpose.
If [the bone] is among many, then force their pulling all of them and leave nothing behind to your utmost ability but if something from the ligaments prevents you from doing so and there is no danger in cutting those ligaments then cut those ligaments as well as any flesh that may be present and release the bones.
If you cannot extract the bones at this time then cover the wound with something that will cause putrefaction and leave it for a few days until the flesh and ligaments surrounding those bones also become putrid, take care to prevent the wound from healing over any incisions made during your work.
But rather place upon it, if you fear this will occur, a cotton pad soaked in sulfur powder with oil or a cotton pad soaked in Egyptian ointment or one of the green ointments for when those ligaments become putrid and the bones bare their extraction and pulling becomes easier.
If it is a single large bone such as the tibia or femur and similar [bones] and only its surface has become corrupted, then scrape it thoroughly until that blackening and corruption disappear then force the wound closed. However if a large part of it has become corrupted and the corruption has reached into the marrow then there is no alternative but to saw through it.
And cut it all away until the damage ends; then treat it so that it may heal. I will tell you about a case of cellulitis in a man's leg, to serve as an example and aid for your treatment. This man was young.
Around the age of thirty, he experienced pain in his leg due to some internal movement within the body, causing the tissues to become infected and swell into a large tumor. There was no external cause; over time, with the doctors' mistakes, the tumor opened up.
Pus and bodily fluids oozed out in great quantities, and his leg became foul-smelling; multiple openings formed, all discharging pus. He was treated by a group of doctors for almost two years but none of them were skilled craftsmen.
Until he came to me; I saw his leg, with the discharge flowing in great quantities from those openings and the man's body had wasted away and his skin turned yellow. I inserted a probe into one of those openings and it reached all the way to the bone; then I examined all the openings.
I found that they connected with each other from all sides of the leg; so I opened up one of those openings and, after cutting away some flesh, exposed a part of the bone which I found to be rotten, eaten away, blackened, putrefied, and perforated right through into the marrow. So I cleaned out what was revealed.
I removed all the rotten bone, thinking that was the only damaged part and I had got rid of it; then I treated the wound with healing drugs for a long time but it still would not heal up. So I uncovered the bone again, beyond where I had first opened it.
I discovered the corruption was connected to the bone, so I also removed what appeared to me of that corruption and then tried to fix it but it did not mend or fuse. Then when I examined again, I kept cutting away the bone part by part trying to heal it but it still would not mend until I had cut away about a hand's breadth.
I extracted it with its brain and then I healed it with medicines so that it quickly fused together, healing rapidly. And this repetition in the procedure and opening was necessary due to the patient's weakness and his low capacity for endurance, as well as my fear of his death because he had fainting spells from severe vomiting at all times. He recovered fully.
A hard flesh grew in the place of the bone, and its condition improved within his body. His strength returned and he recovered, walking with no difficulty or pain whatsoever. If a protruding bone breaks anywhere on the body...
You should spread it in this manner: take a bandage and tie it to the end of the protruding bone, then tell someone to pull it upwards while you fasten another thicker woollen bandage, stronger than the first one.
Then bind it to the flesh below the bone and stretch its end so that the flesh is pulled downwards, while you uncover the area which you wish to saw through, lest the saw harm the flesh. Place a plank or board under the bone from beneath to provide support.
Because if you do that, the saw will not be hindered from cutting through the corrupted bone. You should saw slightly above where the corruption is so as to avoid any further corruption within the hollow of the bone which may not appear on its surface and thus necessitate a second surgery.
If the bone is corrupted and it isn't protruding but its parts are connected, and the corruption is in the middle or in some of it; expose all sides of the flesh entirely then place a piece of wood underneath before sawing it from the first side where the corruption exists.
Once you've finished sawing it, saw it from the other side and let the sawing be slightly away from the corruption as we said; if the corruption is in a joint cut that corrupt joint itself and saw the bone where they are connected from the other side.
If the corruption is in the connection of two joints, there's no other way but to scrape it; if the corruption is in the palm of your hand or the sole of your foot then dealing with this problem will be very difficult however you should peel off the corrupted tissue however possible and scrape it while cleaning it regardless.
By any trick you can think of, whenever no tendons or nerves stand in your way; and know that the chisels and saws used to cut these bones are many according to how the bone is positioned, set up, thick or thin, large or small, hard or soft. Therefore you should prepare for each type of job the appropriate tool that is suitable for that particular task.
Know that the jobs themselves may guide you to the type of tool you need if you have long experience and knowledge in the art of this craft because the skilled craftsmen who witness various diseases will know it well. He may come up with tools that suit him for each disease, I will show you some of these tools at the end of this chapter.

Chapter 78.
On amputation and bone resection.
The limbs may become gangrenous either from an external cause or from an internal one. If you treat this corruption with medicinal treatments and it is not successful, and if you see the corruption advancing in the member uncontrollably.
You should then amputate that member to where the corruption has reached, thus saving the patient from death or a great affliction which is greater than losing the limb. A sign of this is when the member turns black as if it has been burned by fire.
Or it may become gangrenous after turning black, such that the gangrene spreads to the rest of the body; in this case, proceed promptly with amputation. This is also true if the corruption was caused by the bite or sting of certain animals like the sea scorpion, snake, tarantula, and so on.
If the corruption or sting is in the tip of a finger, amputate the entire finger without delay lest the corruption spread to the hand; and if it has already spread to the hand, amputate at the wrist joint so that it does not advance further up the arm.
Similarly, if it has reached the arm, amputate at the elbow joint; but if you see that the corruption is heading towards the shoulder, do not amputate the shoulder under any circumstances, for this will lead to the patient's death. Instead, employ other treatments as best you can.
The same goes for the leg: if corruption has reached a toe, amputate it at one of its joints; and if it is in the metatarsus, amputate the entire leg. But if it has advanced to the knee, then cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If the corruption has reached above the knee, there is no option but to let it be and surrender the patient to death. The method for amputating or dissecting a limb is as follows: tie a ligature below the place where you intend to cut, and tie another one above that location.
One servant stretches the lower ligature downwards, while another pulls the upper one upwards. You then remove the flesh between the two with a broad scalpel until all of it is exposed and can be cut or dissected. It should be done on all sides by placing pieces of linen cloth nearby.
To avoid the saw damaging healthy tissue and causing further pain or a hot swelling, if there is bleeding during your work, cauterise the spot quickly or use some blood-clotting powders on it before returning to your task until you are finished.
Then bind the wounded limb with an appropriate ligature and treat it until it heals. I can give you an example of this exact condition, which a man had in his leg: there was blackening along with a burning sensation akin to fire.
The corruption started at the tip of his toe, spreading throughout his foot as time went on. Due to the severe pain and burning sensation he experienced, the man self-amputated when he saw it was necessary for him to do so in order to prevent further damage from occurring; cutting off all affected tissue just above the ankle joint resulted in a successful healing process thereafter.
After a long period of time had passed, the same corruption reappeared in his index finger. He came to me for help and I attempted to deter its spread by applying various medicines directly onto his hand after thoroughly cleaning it; however these efforts proved unsuccessful as it continued to advance into adjacent digits.
Until corruption spread in the hand, prompting me to cut off his hand, which I refused out of hope that he would refrain from such an act and fear for him; should we amputate his hand, death will ensue since his body had already weakened considerably. When he despaired, he left for his home.
I was informed that he had committed the offense and I cut off his hand entirely, leading to recovery. This story serves as a guide for similar cases of this illness.
Chapter 88.
Treatment of abscesses and the method of injecting them with medication.
Should a tumor occur in some fleshy organs and persist for an extended period, eventually bursting or draining away all the pus it contained, leaving behind an empty cavity resembling a vessel.
The skin over it resembles a rag, thin and not so corrupted as to affect the bone, nerve or ligament. Thus, it deserves to be called an abscess rather than a fistula unless its corruption persists long enough to affect any of these organs.
It is then called a fistula or sinus and should be treated by cutting away all the skin, especially if it has thinned considerably to resemble a rag, while ensuring that its corruption does not adhere to the wound.
If you hope for the skin to adhere and the corruption has not reached such an extent, yet there is some flesh remaining in the skin, treat it with injection; inspect the abscess, if large and its pus emits a foul odor, inject it with Egyptian medication.
Which is equal parts vinegar, oil, honey and verdigris; mix them in a container and cook on the fire until the mixture thickens to the consistency of honey. Then take what you need, dilute it with water and honey, inject into the abscess, bind its mouth, and leave the medication for two hours.
Then express the contents, repeating this for several days until the abscess is cleansed and the foul smell disappears. It can also be injected with lye water if you do not have the aforementioned medication; this is made by adding water to the ash of grapevine wood or oak.
Then strain and inject until you are sure the abscess is cleansed; if the patient cannot tolerate Egyptian medication or lye water, then inject with a mixture of water, honey, and some powdered verdigris.
Or inject with a mixture of honey and wine, for the honey will cleanse and wash out, while the wine adheres to the abscess; especially if the wine has some astringent and drying properties. If you do this several times and are sure the abscess is healed, then inject with something that promotes flesh growth.
For example, take some palm ointment and dissolve it in rose oil with astringent wine; or inject with other medications and salves described previously in the chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the abscess is narrow and does not allow the injection tube to enter,
Widen it slightly with an iron instrument, or place a wick soaked in Egyptian ointment or Serapion's salve until it is sufficiently wide; and if the mouth of the abscess is also too large, suture its lips together so that only enough remains to allow insertion of the injection tube without excess.
And if the mouth from which pus flows is raised upwards, then cut it open in its lowest part so that the pus may flow downwards; for when pus accumulates deep inside a cavity, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut open the cavity towards its lower part as intended, then erect the member so that pus may flow out of it easily in whatever way seems suitable to you and aim for nothing at all of the pus to be retained within.
As for the healing medicines that are applied to the cavity from outside and how they should be put on it, take one of the appropriate healing ointments you require and spread it onto a linen cloth; let the cloth be large enough to wrap around the entire cavity. Then with scissors cut an opening in front of the cavity's mouth, slightly wider than its own mouth; then spread some soft ointment on another cloth that fits over the opening and place it so that it is spaced away from the mouth of the cavity to allow easy drainage of pus; do not remove the large cloth until...
leave it there for several days; as for the one on the mouth of the cavity, remove that at every opportunity so that any pus accumulated can be released and you may discover how much pus is draining from it and whether it is ripe or unripe.
and also examine the cavity itself to see if its owner feels any pain there or whether there is swelling or not; so, as your treatment continues in this way and you observe that the discharge on the mouth of the cavity is negligible...
know then that the cavity has healed or is close to healing; so, untie it on the following day or by the third day and when you have undone the binding of the wound, replace the cloths with new ones and renew the ointment; if after a long time thin pus still flows from the cavity, do not despair of its sticking together.
His healing is quick because there are people whose flesh grows slowly in their wounds, and others have the opposite. If after many days, pus emerges from the wound that is not ripe, know then that the wound has not healed. If the closure of the wound is delayed.
If it takes a long time, then increase the drying of the medications and let them be moist in composition but dry in strength, such as palm ointment if an extra amount of pitch has been added to it. The most effective treatment is to take palm ointment.
It can be dissolved in rose oil, and old wine of moderate strength is sprinkled over it; then knead it with this and use it. A similar action may be taken if palm ointment is not available: honey that has been boiled until thick is used, or take myrobalan, aloe, and costus, grind them all together, and sprinkle the powder on honey while it is still over a fire. Then apply it to a cloth and tie it over the wound, or use honey that has been boiled until thick, sprinkle the drugs on it, place cloths over it, and tie them.
Another treatment for this is to use single or combined preparations of long-leaved mezereon, iris root, chaff flower flour, and centaury; grind them together, sieve the mixture, sprinkle it on honey as previously described, then apply that. If after a long time the wound has not healed using these treatments, know that there is damage deep inside.
If it affects the bones or other solid nerves or ligaments, treat it with an abscess treatment as described previously.
Chapter 89.
On the Treatment of Whipping Pain and Crushed Fingernail, Amputation of Superfluous Finger, and Splitting of Fused Fingers.
Whipping pain is a fleshy growth that forms under the thumbnail of the hand or foot, and it may also occur on other fingers. If left untreated for too long, it becomes swollen and hot.
If it becomes corrupt and purulent to the point of eating away at the nail bed, or even destroying the entire nail, and perhaps reaching as far as the bone so that it develops a putrid odor and the tip of the finger becomes broad and its color turns livid, then if you treat it with what has been mentioned in this chapter and your treatment is not effective.
You should then remove with a blade all the excess flesh that has grown from the nail, and cauterize the wound afterward; cauterization is extremely beneficial in this case. If the bone is intact and also the nail.
If the curved part of the nail has pressed flesh inward and is constantly jabbing and irritating it, you should place a thin probe under that corner of the nail which is causing the pain and lift it up; then cut away that flesh gently.
Apply a caustic medicine to the remaining flesh until it is all gone, then treat with salves until healed. But if the corruption has spread as far as the bone, you should remove that bone and extract it; for so long as there remains any corrupted bone, healing will be impossible.
If you see the decay and corruption spreading into the finger, cut it off at one of the phalanges as described previously, then treat until healed. If the nail has been struck or bruised and intense pain results, you should first perform venesection on the patient.
First, make a diagonal incision on the nail with a sharp scalpel from top to bottom and be careful not to cut into the flesh beneath the nail as this will cause severe pain for the patient and lead to excess tissue growth at that spot; then treat the area with something to alleviate the pain.
The extra finger that is sometimes generated in some people's hands may be fully fleshy, or it might have bones and nails; the growth of some occurs at the joint where two fingers meet.
It might also grow on one of the finger bones, and those that grow on a finger bone do not move; as for those that move, they may appear at the joint. Cut off any fully fleshy growth by slicing it off at its base with a wide scalpel.
But removing growth from the joint is difficult and should be avoided; as for those that grow on a finger bone, first cut off the flesh in a circular motion down to the bone and then use one of the appropriate saws to cut into the bone.
And treat it until it heals. As for the fusion of fingers, this happens frequently; it may be something one is born with or caused by a healed wound, burn, or similar injury and must be surgically separated.
To restore the fingers to their natural state, split them apart with a wool thread or cloth soaked in rose oil so they do not quickly fuse again; or place a thin sheet of lead between them until the wound heals as it should.
The same goes for if the fingers have fused with the palm; cut into this fusion however works best to restore the limb's form.
Chapter Ninety.
On the excision of varicose veins.
Varicose veins are thick, twisted, dark-blue vessels filled with blood which occur in most parts of the body; they especially appear on the legs – mostly on the calves and thighs belonging to porters, gardeners and carriers. At first, one should vigorously use a remedy that expels black bile from the body.
Next, bleed the patient from the ulnar vein; as for treating it with cauterization, there are two methods: one involves cutting open and removing the dark blood while another requires tying off the vein and removing it entirely. The procedure of opening is done like this: first, bathe the leg thoroughly in hot water.
until the thick, cloudy blood softens; then bind the patient's leg tightly from above the thigh to below the knee with a bandage and cut open the vein in one spot or two spots or three – making wide incisions.
Next, draw out the dark blood with your hand from below the leg upward and then downward until you have removed as much of it as seems sufficient or that the patient can bear; then tie up the wound and tell him to avoid food that generates black bile while repeating the purging and bleeding.
If the vessels become full and this harms the patient, you can remove it by shaving the hair from his leg, then bathing in a warm bath until it reddens and exudes sweat or he exercises vigorously.
If he does not have a bathhouse to warm up his member, then cut the skin facing the vein lengthwise – either at its end near the knee or towards its bottom by the heel; next, open it with hooks and peel away the vein from both sides until you can feel it.
When it first appears, you see it as a deep red color. Once it has broken through the skin, it also looks like a tendon. Then insert a rod beneath it until it rises and comes out of the skin; tie it to a smooth, blunt hook. Afterwards make another incision nearby that one by about three fingers' width.
Then remove the skin from over the vein so that it is visible, then lift it with the rod as you did before and tie it to another hook like you did initially, then make another incision or multiple incisions if needed.
Then pull it out and cut it at the end of the incision near the heel, then grab and pull it out of the second incision; pull it towards the upper incision and continue this until you have pulled it all the way through from the third incision which is located highest up; once it has all come out, cut it.
If it does not respond to pulling and stripping, insert a needle with a strong doubled thread and tie it; pull it and put the rod beneath it again and twist your hand in each direction until it comes out. Be careful that it does not break as this would make it very difficult to strip.
If any of this procedure causes harm to the patient, then apply a piece of woolen fabric soaked in rose water or oil; treat it until it heals. If the arm is stiff and twisted with varicose veins, then...
If the arm is not straight as mentioned, you should make incisions on either side of the varicose veins and at points where it twists; attach hooks to these and strip out the entire vein. Take care when making the incision that you do not cut or damage the vein as this will make stripping very difficult.
