It should not have a barb like the other hooks and it should not be sharp at the end so as not to injure the vein, instead being thickly bent and smooth because if it were thin it would cut the vein with its thinness but rather it must be much thicker than we said.
Chapter ninety-one.
For removing the urban vein.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot regions such as Hijaz and Arabia, and in arid, infertile countries. It may also occur elsewhere on the body besides the legs due to putrefaction beneath the skin.
As such things occur in the bodies of snakes, worms, pumpkins and larvae generated between skin and flesh; a sign that this vein is starting to appear is when there is severe inflammation in the leg followed by blisters at the site which then begin to release the vein.
Like the root of a plant or animal, when its tip appears it should be wrapped around a small piece of lead weighing from one to two drachmas, tied in place and left hanging from the leg.
Each time a part of it emerges, wrap and tie that in lead; if its length becomes excessive then cut off some of the vein but continue to wrap up any remainder without cutting it from its root before it has all emerged for if you do so, it will contract back into the flesh causing swelling, putrefaction at the site and a poor ulcer.
Therefore, it should be treated with care and removed gradually until all of it is out, leaving nothing in the body. In some people, this vein may measure up to five spans and ten palms [in length] when it comes out.
I have heard of a case where it came out from a man measuring twenty spans. If it breaks while you are treating it, insert a bougie into its orifice and push it down along the body until all the material is emptied; then try to induce suppuration in this part with medicine for several days.
Then treat it with the treatment for tumors. This vein may have multiple branches, especially if it appears in the ankle joint or in the leg itself; there will be many openings from which these branches emerge. Treat them as we mentioned earlier when discussing classification and according to what was previously explained.
Chapter ninety-two.
Regarding cutting into worms that develop beneath the skin, also known as "cow disease.
This disease is called "cow disease" in some of our countries because cattle are often affected by it. It is a small worm that develops between the skin and flesh, moving up and down throughout the body.
It can be detected by touch as it moves from one part of the body to another until it pierces the skin in a particular spot and emerges, forming from the putrefaction of some humors, much like worms, snakes, or pumpkin seeds in the abdomen.
The concern with this worm is that, if it moves through the body and reaches the head and then the eye, it might open up there and emerge, potentially blinding the patient. This occurs frequently. When treating and removing it, wait until you can feel its movement and detect its presence.
You should tighten the area above and below [the abscess] with a strong bandage, then make an incision over it and extract it. If it sinks into the flesh and you cannot find it, cauterize the area with fire until it is burned away. The greatest concern is that it may damage the eye, as we have said.
If you see that it has reached the head near the eye, then tightly bandage below it on the forehead and make an incision over it to extract it. The patient should regularly cleanse their body with medicines that ease the expulsion of putrid, foul humours and be cautious about foods that cause putrefaction.
Chapter 93.
On incising the disease known as 'the bulging one'.
This disease, which is called 'the bulging one' in our country, is a pain that appears in some body parts and then moves from one part to another. I have seen cases that match my description.
I was called to see a sick woman in the countryside. She showed me her arm, with a slight swelling on the radial artery. After an hour had passed, I saw that swelling moving along the forearm, ascending towards her shoulder as quickly as mercury.
If it moves from one place to another, the pain disappears from that spot and remains in the shoulder. After an hour passes, it spreads throughout the body until it reaches the other arm. She then told me that it circulates through her entire body, as I had witnessed.
I was amazed at how quickly it moved from one body part to another. I had never seen this disease with my own eyes before, presented in such a manner. I have, however, seen many people who experience pain that shifts from one part to another, but I had not witnessed it as clearly as I did with this woman.
I only perceived this because the woman was from a rural area, with dry skin and prominent veins. This is why it could be sensed; but one should not expect this to occur in people who are well-off, have moist bodies, and whose veins are less visible.
So, if you wish to treat it and the patient feels pain; should it appear before your eyes as mentioned above, strap over it from both sides with a bandage, press firmly until that trapped wind is released, then cauterize the area.
If you do not see it, treat the body with a shaking motion and other wind-removing agents such as stink seed, scabiosa seeds, and similar medicines.
Chapter 94.
On the removal of arrows.
Arrows vary depending on their type and the part of the body where they are located; these differences include large or small sizes, hollow or solid glass bodies, and variations in the number of angles with either three or four sides.
Some arrows have tongues, while others have splinters; those that are classified by the body parts they affect can be divided into two types: either vital hollow organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder, and so on. If an arrow strikes one of these organs and you observe the signs of impending death as described, you should refrain from extracting the arrow; in most cases, it will lead to the patient's demise. If these negative signs are not present and the arrow has not penetrated deep into an organ, proceed with its removal and treat the wound. One sign of brain injury is a severe headache accompanied by intense pressure and dizziness when an arrow pierces the skull and damages the membrane covering the brain.
If these negative signs are not present and the arrow has not penetrated deep into an organ, proceed with its removal and treat the wound. One sign of brain injury is a severe headache accompanied by intense pressure and dizziness when an arrow pierces the skull and damages the membrane covering the brain. Redness in the eyes, a red tongue, convulsions, delirium, vomiting of bile, and sometimes blood comes out from the nostrils or ears. Speech may be lost, the voice gone, and a moist discharge like white porridge emerges from the wound site with an effluent resembling the watery part of flesh.
If these signs appear, desist from treating the patient and extracting the arrow if it has not already been removed. As for the signs of an arrow having pierced the heart, it is when it is close to the left breast and he can feel that it has lodged in something solid rather than empty.
Sometimes there is a movement in the arrow resembling the pulse, and black blood flows from the wound, followed by coldness of the limbs, cold sweat, and loss of consciousness. Then know that death is inevitable. A sign of an arrow having pierced the lung is frothy blood issuing forth from the wound.
The blood vessels following the neck become swollen, the patient's colour changes, and he breathes deeply while seeking to inhale cold air. If the arrow has pierced the diaphragm in the chest, it is close to the small ribs and breathing becomes laboured with severe pain.
He heaves deep sighs and there is movement in all the members of both shoulders. If the arrow has struck the liver, it is followed by severe pain, with blood emerging from the wound resembling the liver's redness. If the arrow has pierced the stomach, undigested food may emerge from the wound.
The situation is clear if the arrow has struck and lodged in the belly, with excrement emerging from the wound or intestines that have been perforated. In this case there is no hope for treatment nor extraction of the arrow. If the arrow pierces the bladder and urine comes out...
And if a part of it protrudes outwards and the pain intensifies for the patient, then know that they are doomed to die. As for other body parts such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, upper arm, vertebrae, collarbone, thigh, leg, and similar organs, one can generally assume recovery.
When the arrow has not struck an artery or nerve and is not poisoned, I will inform you of some observations regarding such arrows so that you may deduce your treatment accordingly. An example would be when an arrow hit a man's eye socket at the root of his nose.
I removed it for him from the other side, beneath the earlobe, and he healed without any harm to his eye. I extracted another arrow from a Jew's tear duct under the lower eyelid; the arrow had disappeared, leaving only its small tip attached to the wooden shaft.
It was a large arrow, one of the composite bows with a square iron head that was smooth and had no ears. The Jew healed without any damage to his eye. I extracted another Arabic arrow from a Christian's throat.
It had two ears, so I cut it open between the jugular veins; it had sunk into his throat. I gently extracted it, and the Christian survived and healed. I removed another arrow from a man's stomach, estimating that he would die from it.
After thirty days or so had passed without any change in his condition, I cut open the arrow and maneuvered its removal; he healed without any further incident. I saw a man with an arrow wound on his back that had already begun to heal over.
After seven years, the arrow emerged from his thigh, and I saw a woman who had been shot in her belly; the wound healed but the arrow remained inside without causing any change to her condition or discomfort in her natural functions.
I saw another man who had been shot in the face; his wound healed but he still felt little pain, and there are many similar cases like this. I removed an arrow for a commander of the sultan's army that was lodged in the middle of his nose, slightly deviated to the right.
The arrow disappeared completely, so I was called to treat him three days after the injury; I found that the wound from the arrow was very narrow and, upon examining it with a fine probe, could not locate anything. The patient experienced discomfort and pain underneath his right ear.
I hoped that the discomfort was caused by the tip of the arrow, so I bandaged the area with a strong drawing plaster in hopes that it would cause swelling and reveal its presence; however, no such incident occurred.
I continued with the bandages for many days without any incident, and during this time, the wound healed over; the patient despaired of removing the arrow until one day he felt it inside his nose and informed me about it. I then applied a sharp, corrosive medication on the area for several days.
I continued to open and probe the wound until I felt the thin end of the arrow attached to its shaft, then increased the use of that sharp medication in order to expose it; after a period of time - about four months - we could see the tip of the arrow.
Then, when the wound widened and allowed for it, I inserted the forceps into it, pulling and moving it, but it did not yield to come out. So I kept coaxing it and trying different instruments until one day I grasped it with precise forceps as shown in the image at the end of this chapter.
The physicians had deemed his nasal cartilage unrepairable, yet I set it, and the wound healed. The patient recovered fully without issue, and I share this to guide you in removing some arrows, serving as a benchmark for other cases not detailed here.
As the intricacies of this craft cannot be fully grasped through description nor covered exhaustively in a book, it is the skilled practitioner who extrapolates from limited knowledge to manage diverse situations and create novel solutions with available tools when faced with unusual cases.
When extracting arrows from the body, they can be removed in two ways depending on their entry point: either by pulling them out directly or through an incision made opposite to where they entered.
If the arrowhead is visible under healthy flesh, you can attempt to pull it out directly. However, if this proves difficult initially, it's advisable to let it remain for a few days until the surrounding flesh becomes infected and easier to manipulate, which will facilitate its removal. The same applies when an arrowhead is embedded in bone.
Should it resist extraction after some time, leave it alone for a few more days and attempt pulling and moving it daily. If this proves ineffective over several days, you must drill small holes around the arrowhead in the bone from different angles using delicate drills to create space for its removal.
If the arrow embedded in the skull bone has penetrated deeply into one of the brain's ventricles and some of the symptoms mentioned have appeared, cease pulling on the arrow and leave it until its condition improves over several days.
So proceed to pull out the arrow, and if it is embedded very firmly and does not yield readily to pulling, use the drills around the arrow as I have described for you; then treat the place until it heals. But if the arrow has penetrated deeply into some part of the body, And if it is lost to sensation, probe for it with a sound; if you feel it, pull it out by means of some instrument suitable for the purpose. If you cannot manage this on account of the smallness of the wound and the depth of penetration of the arrow, and there are no bones, nerves or arteries in that place,
It will be difficult; so make an incision to enlarge the wound and seize the arrow in such a way that you can pull it out. If it has ears, grasp them with some instrument and free the flesh sticking to them on every side by whatever means you can; but if you cannot manage this without tearing the flesh, break or cut the ears so as to get free.
And when you try to extract the arrow, whatever part it may be in, use your hand in twisting with forceps in every direction until you get free of it; but proceed gently and carefully, lest the arrow should break and make it more difficult for you to pull it out. If you cannot manage this at once,
Leave it for a few days until the flesh around it becomes rotten, and then treat it again; at that point, it will be easier to do so. If you encounter any bleeding, use the treatment we mentioned in its section. Be very careful not to cut an artery, nerve or tendon, but rather employ tricks.
Remove the arrow in every way you can, gently and carefully, as I have described to you. When pulling out the arrow, it is important that the patient assumes the same position they were in when the arrow struck them; this is more suitable.
If that is not possible, then employ whatever form you can manage. If the arrow has emerged on the opposite side, either some of it may be protruding outside or you might find and feel the tip of the arrow just beneath the skin; in this case, you will see it sticking out.
Make an incision over it and let the size of that incision match what the forceps can accommodate. Then pull out the arrow; at this point, its removal should be easy. If you find that it is stuck in bone, then twist your hand around until the arrow affects the bone and makes some space for itself. Pull once more.
If this does not work, then leave it for a few days before attempting to remove the arrow again; at that point, you should be able to do so. If the shaft of the arrow is still in there, push on it with your finger. However, if the shaft has fallen out and you wish to employ pushing as a method, insert an empty tube into the wound such that its hollow interior encloses the tip of the arrow. Push the arrow out using this device.
If the arrow is hollow, push on it using a device that fits inside its cavity; this should make its removal easier. If the arrow is poisoned, then excise all of the flesh that has been contaminated by the poison if you can. Treat what remains with an appropriate medicine.
If the arrow that has struck is in the chest, or in the abdomen, or in the bladder, or in the side, and it is close to where you can feel it with a sound, then make an incision there, taking care not to cut any veins or nerves, and remove the arrow.
Then suture the wound if it requires stitching, and treat it until it heals. Image: The forceps used to extract arrows.
Their tips are shaped like a bird's beak, designed to grip arrows or other objects firmly without letting go. Forceps come in various sizes: large, small, and medium, depending on the size of the arrow, the wound, and its width.

Image: The solid cannon.
This is solid at the end, shaped like a pestle, to facilitate its entry into the hollow arrow and pushing it through.
Chapter 95.
On venesection, or opening the veins.
There are thirty veins in the body that are commonly opened for bloodletting, sixteen of which are in the head: the two pulsating veins behind the ears known as the 'sensitive ones', the two arteries visible on the temples, and the two veins at the corners of the eyes known as the 'observers'.
There is one vein that rises in the middle of the forehead, a vein at the tip of the nose, two veins on the neck (the 'jugulars'), two veins on the upper lip, and two more on the lower lip; these are known as the 'four-fold', plus there are two veins under the tongue.
The five veins that are bled in the arm and hand, one of which is the cephalic vein on the lateral side, which the public calls the head vein; another is the radial artery, the middle one, compounded of a branch from the basilic vein and a division of the cephalic, called by the common people 'the body vein'.
And the basilic vein, which lies on the medial side and is also called the axillary vein; the public call it the abdominal vein or arm cord. The ulnar artery lies on the ulna bone to which it adheres, becoming clearly visible above the thumb.
And the vein between the little finger and ring finger has two branches; in the leg there are three, one beneath the knee bend on the lateral side, a second is the saphenous vein located at the heel on the medial side and sciatic nerve which lies near the heel on the lateral side.
And the three veins in the other leg are similar; as for the two veins behind the ears, their bleeding is beneficial for chronic colds, migraines, tinea and chronic malignant sores of the head; and here I describe how to bleed them.
This is done by shaving the patient's head and vigorously rubbing their nape in the position of the two veins with a coarse cloth; then have them strangle their neck with a turban until the veins appear, located behind both ears at the lower part of the head; feel for them with your finger and where you can feel their pulse beneath your finger make a mark there with ink.
Then take a knife-like lancet, known as a fleam, and insert it under the vein into the skin until you reach the bone with the blade; raise your hand to lift the vein and skin upward then cut the vein along with the skin cleanly and make the length of the incision equal to two fingers pressed together.
Or, you send from the blood that amount which you desire and then bind them with bandages until they heal; however, it has been mentioned earlier in this book regarding their cutting and cauterization. As for the two arteries found in the temples (temporal arteries), they are beneficial to be opened by incision for treating chronic migraines, severe headaches, continuous inflammation of the eyes, and excessive drainage of acute humour into the eyes.
The method of their incision is as follows: the patient should tightly bind his neck with a turban, until the two arteries become prominent to touch and their pulsation becomes apparent beneath your finger; at this point you mark them with ink, then lift the skin above the artery towards its upper side using your index finger.
You insert the sharp scalpel from below and lift the artery upwards, then sever it as you did with the other two arteries; drain blood according to your needs, untie the patient's neckband, and place your finger on the artery for an hour.
Then, you place a cotton pad and a bandage on top, securing them tightly; leave it until it heals. The mention of this artery (the frontal artery) and its incision or ligation has been previously made in the beginning of this book. As for the opening of the forehead's artery, it is beneficial after the arteries of the temples have been opened to treat chronic diseases of the face such as tinea, ulcers, and unpleasant rashes.
The method of its incision is as follows: the patient should bind his neck with a turban until the artery becomes apparent, then you take the instrument known as the axe; this is its image:" [Image]
You place the protruding spike at the head of the axe on top of the artery, and strike from above using a comb or another similar object; allow blood to flow according to your needs, then untie the patient's neckband. You should secure the wound until it heals.
It can be opened with a wide scalpel, but the blade should not be sharp at the tip like other scalpels; instead, it should be slightly broad and blunt-tipped, and you should make the incision diagonally because the bone is nearby, so the thin scalpel may break if it strikes the bone.
As for the two veins located at the corners of the eyes, they are beneficial for eye ailments such as scabies, conjunctivitis, and drooping eyelids, as well as various facial diseases. To open these veins, the patient should strain their neck by tying a cloth around it; you then make an incision while standing over their head, making sure to angle the scalpel slightly towards the length of the vein and using a small, relatively wide blade.
As this area has no flesh, if the scalpel is thin-tipped it may break; instead use a thicker blade and take only as much blood as needed before placing a piece of cotton on each vein and binding them for one night. Opening the nasal vein is beneficial in cases of severe fever.
It is also useful for severe headaches and facial diseases such as acute erysipelas which appears on the nose, especially if it becomes chronic. To open this vein, have the patient strain their neck and hold their nose with your left hand while taking a long, thin scalpel in your right.
Insert the blade into the center of the nose tip, between the nasal bones and in a straight line because the vein cannot be felt there; as soon as you insert it, blood will emerge. Make sure to advance the scalpel carefully with your hand until you have taken enough blood, then bind the area for one night since it heals quickly.
Opening the veins of the temples is useful for dyspnea, early leprosy, and melancholic diseases which appear on the skin's surface such as vitiligo, impetigo, and malignant ulcers or abscesses. Have the patient tie a bandage under their neck to strain these areas; the surgeon should then stand at their head.
The patient is seated on a chair, then the vein along the calf is opened up with a fairly wide incision and an appropriate amount of blood is drained or according to what you see is required; you then do the same for the other vein before removing the bandage.
You should tie up the two veins with a medium tension so that the patient doesn't suffocate and you leave him until tomorrow, for the wound will heal; as for the four [veins] of the arm, their benefit is in being opened after opening those of the calf since it benefits against blisters on the mouth, gum disease, bad sores, cracked lips and bad sores.
Those that occur in and around the nose; how you open them is by having your patient sit in front of you, tie their neck with a cloth then turn their lips up so that you can see one vein to the right of the lip and another on its left, noting their darkness.
And note that they are surrounded by thin, dark veins which you should cut off; if it is unclear to you and you don't know what they are then aim for the largest and most prominent one; do likewise with those two in the lower lip.
As for the two veins under the tongue, their benefit is in being opened after opening those of the calf as a remedy against swellings that occur in the throat from illnesses of the uvula and mouth; how you open them is by having your patient sit facing the sun between your hands while raising his tongue to look underneath it on one side for a vein and another on its opposite, noting their dark colour.
You open them up, taking care not to cut too deeply since there are arteries underneath which may cause bleeding; as for the three veins in the elbow, they are those commonly opened by all people and their opening can be done in two ways.
Either suturing with a broad basil or olive-shaped lancet, or incising with a knife-like lancet which is called the nashl [this is its image], image of the broad basil-shaped lancet: .
It is broad, as you can see, suitable for opening thick, full, prominent veins that are clearly visible and contain thick, cloudy blood. This is the image of the olive-shaped lancet: .
This lancet is less broad and has a thinner tip, suitable for lancing thin veins that contain thin, bile-like blood. This is the image of the nashl lancet: .
This nashl is suitable for incising, and there are various types of broad and thin ones according to the width and narrowness of veins. One may use it as a guide for others, and it is well-known among craftsmen.
As for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of lancing it is that it draws blood from illnesses located beneath the neck and throat towards the chest and abdomen. The lacerator should be cautious when lancing it and place it on a higher part of the neck because there is an artery underneath; if they make a mistake and go too deep with the lancet, they may cut this artery.
This would result in bleeding, so lancing should not be done with a suturing lancet but rather by incising with the nashl. If the basilic vein is not clearly visible, one should avoid it and move to another or ask for some of its branches.
Alternatively, one may lance at the location of the brachial artery as it is clearly visible and can be incised with a nashl lancet as mentioned earlier. If you wish to specifically lance this vein, you should first feel for its location in order to identify the pulse spot then mark it with ink.
Then bind the arm, make an oblique incision on the vein with a curved knife as we said before, and ensure that the cut is away from the artery. When you see, while tightening the ligature, swelling in the place which was marked with ink, then that swelling is indeed the artery, so avoid it.
If you see that the blood, when let out of a vein by an incision, spurts like urine from a boy and is thin red in color, know it to be arterial blood; so then proceed quickly, apply your finger for some time, then remove your finger, if the bleeding stops, it often does stop, bind up the arm.
Leave it, warn the patient against negligence and let him keep quiet on his neck without moving for some days until he recovers. If the bleeding does not stop despite your efforts, and no remedy is at hand in time, then cut through the artery if it can be seen; its ends will contract and the blood will cease to flow.
Or take a pistachio nut shell, cut it and use one half, bind it tightly over the vein with a ligature and bandages for another day; if bleeding stops then leave it alone otherwise treat it as mentioned before by applying styptics to stop bleeding which is not very difficult in most cases.
Know that the reason for this is the small size of the wound and the tight binding around the arm. As for the ulnar artery, its benefit from venesection is to draw blood away from both head and body because, as we said before, it is a branch composed of both the basilic vein and cephalic veins.
The person performing the venesection should be careful to be on top of the patient's neck during the procedure, as below it there is a nerve; if too much pressure is applied with the knife and this nerve is injured then numbness may occur which can be difficult to cure or even impossible in some cases. This nerve often presents itself when touched.
If the vein is hidden in some people and is thin, not easily seen, then you should make your venesection of it a simple incision with the lancet, avoiding the tendon if at all possible. If the vein lies between two tendons, incise the vein lengthwise. As for the cephalic vein, the benefit of its venesection is that it drains blood from the head.
It is beneficial for ailments of the eyes, and in this vein especially, if you wish to perform venesection, it is best done by incising with an olive-shaped lancet or a broad basilic lancet, as it is the safest of all veins because there are no arteries or nerves beneath it.
However, when performing venesection, you should avoid the head of the muscle with your lancet and aim for the softer spot; it does no harm if it is not hit on the first strike to return to it again and again, but in some people, it may swell up if it is not cut on the first strike.
But that swelling does it no harm; as for the procedure of venesection, its complications, and what should be done beforehand to remedy them, first you must know that venesection is either employed solely in maintaining health.
Or it is employed in treating diseases; now, venesection used to maintain and preserve health, and to prevent the onset of illness, should be performed on one of the three veins at the elbow: namely, the cephalic vein, the radial artery, or the basilic vein. Venesection for this purpose should take place in early spring when signs of fullness appear.
Venesection for maintaining health should take place on a Sunday or Tuesday, after three hours have passed since daybreak. However, venesection used to treat diseases has no specific time limit; rather, it is done whenever the need and necessity arise, whether night or day.
In every hour and at all times, it is not appropriate to bloodlet boys until they have reached the age of fourteen years, nor should it be done on elders who have surpassed sixty years. When someone intends to perform bloodletting for any reason...
One should clean their intestine before bloodletting with a soft enema if there is a lot of hardened excrement retained, so as not to draw towards the veins during bloodletting any noxious waste from the intestine that would harm the vital organs; nor should one be bled when they are fatigued or drunk until such time as this has passed.
One must take care not to be bled immediately following a bout of diarrhea or vomiting, after birth, when excessively fatigued by coitus, or tired from overwork, exercise, lack of sleep and fasting; whatever weakens the body physically or mentally; then you should consider whether the blood is thick before carrying out the procedure.
One should thin the blood with food, drink and medication if possible; they may enter a bath if there is no reason not to or practice some exercise so as to thin their blood; you should carry out venesection in the early part of the day as we have said and on that day try to clear your mind of all unpleasant mental distractions.
Such as worries, anger and fear; or physical factors like excessive fatigue and exertion, coitus etc.; you should prepare the room with items which people are accustomed to using such as various scents, flowers and amusements according to what is available.
Then the phlebotomist sits on a cushion which is slightly higher than that of the patient; they should rub his arm two or three times with their hand before binding it tightly around the upper arm two to three times, taking care not to pull too hard.
For when the ligature is not moderate, whether through excess and thus preventing blood flow or if too relaxed and also stopping blood flow, then after the ligature one must rub together both hands of the patient.
Until the veins become engorged and perceptible to touch, then the phlebotomist should wipe the lancet with a little old oil specifically for this purpose; he then places his left index finger on the same vein just below where he wishes to make the incision so that the vein does not dodge and thus avoid the strike. Because there are veins which you will find act like a string, dodging the strike at phlebotomy; and some are filled with air so that when the lancet is placed on them they retreat beneath it, deceiving the phlebotomist who does not open the vein properly even though he may have opened it slightly.
Therefore the phlebotomist should be certain and slow with all of this; he then lowers his lancet but if he does not open the vein at that first strike, he must repeat it just below or above the initial spot without being too hasty unless the area is swollen.
If it becomes swollen or the patient is alarmed then leave him for a day or two without applying a ligature as this may cause hot swelling; he must not go to the bathhouse either, but if after that time phlebotomy is desired again then do so, and if you thrust your lancet yet the incision was small with thin flowing blood...
And if you fear that the required amount of blood will not come out then return your lancet to the same hole, gently and straight; increase it a little but do this swiftly before the area becomes swollen for in many people an area can become swollen even from a small incision.
If you see that it has swollen, do not repeat the procedure at all for it will be of no assistance to you. Place on it some of the residue from boiled oil; this facilitates the flow of blood and is better for this location than the oil itself or other ointments. Likewise...
Use the residue from boiled oil in all your venesections of veins when there is difficulty with the flow of blood. And so may theriaca and schenzepra, if one of them is placed on the location; the blood becomes thinner and more fluid, should it be thick...
If a large swelling occurs at the location of venesection, it often does so and especially to those who have only bled once before or whose vein was opened by a small incision; immediately apply a sponge soaked in warm, slightly salted water to the swelling and bind it for an hour – it will resolve.
You should do this after all the blood has drained from that vein or another; if some blackness or greenness remains at the location after several days, it will be harmless. If you wish, apply something of dissolved gum mastic and myrrh to it.
Or something of the juice of veronica and such, a swelling often occurs with venesection of the basilic vein; place your hand on it – if you find that it yields to pressure, this is an undesirable swelling so beware of applying any of what has been mentioned above to it.
For it may bleed the blood of an artery; bind it with a styptic to harden the location then treat it with all other treatments until it heals. You should draw from each person according to their strength and the color that dominates in their blood.
If the blood is black, let it flow out until it reddens; and if you see it thick, send it forth until it thins; likewise if it is sharp, allow it to exit until its sharpness has gone; and it behooves a person who is full-blooded and strong and needs to have the blood removed in one go.
To widen the vein for bloodletting, use a broad lancet; and if the patient is weak, do the opposite. You should remove the blood in multiple sessions, and the hole should be narrow. The best instrument to use for opening the vein is one that is beveled, square-shaped, and makes an incision rather than a puncture.
This type of bloodletting is safe from excessive bleeding and damage to the nerve, and it is the best and safest way compared to vertical or horizontal cuts. If a person is accustomed to fainting during bloodletting, they should be fed some bread soaked in sour pomegranate juice or vinegar before the procedure.
If the patient is experiencing a burning sensation and blood needs to be removed three or four times, if their temperament is cold, they should consume bread soaked in wine, honey infused with spices, or basil-infused drink before each bloodletting session.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and it is due to excessive loss of blood, the patient should be given meat broth and a light basil drink. The use of perfume with musk is recommended; its scent should be applied liberally on their chest. Other measures mentioned in the section on fainting should also be employed.
For those wishing to invigorate their arm and promote blood circulation, if the reason for bloodletting is to induce a significant amount of purging and their strength is weak, the blood should be let out in small amounts over consecutive days, according to how much they can tolerate.
For those who wish to relieve their arm and release the blood again, and if their body is strong, they should do so seven or nine hours after the initial incision. As for those who want to draw out the blood from their body towards the opposite side that has tilted, it is necessary to relieve them on the second or third day.
If there is an excess of hot and acrid blood in the body, causing a fever, it is necessary to draw out this blood in one go by extracting a large quantity and widening the opening until unconsciousness occurs after ensuring that all other conditions for venesection have been met. You should place your hand on the patient's pulse while the blood is flowing so as to prevent death occurring at the same time as unconsciousness, for this often happens when the bleeder is ignorant and careless. It is not necessary if you wish to relieve the arm and release the blood again.
If the mouth of the vein has closed and blood flow is obstructed, you should press on it firmly or twist vigorously as this is very detrimental. Instead, either leave it until you bleed again or remove with a knife blade any congealed blood from the mouth of the vein.
Or apply to it some salt dissolved in water, or some theriac, or some schichenaia and press gently until the blood flows out. If the vein has swollen, then leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If it is necessary to release blood again, you may either bleed above that spot or in the other arm or vein.
To open the vein of the arm, one should make an incision in place of the radial and ulnar arteries if they are not found or are too small to see, since it is composed from them. The procedure for this is as follows: the patient must immerse their hand in hot water until the forearm reddens and the veins become clearly visible; then a bandage should be applied above it with moderate tension.
Then make an incision in the vein with a slight oblique angle, neither across nor along it, and let the opening be wide; perform the procedure above the wrist joint slightly. If blood does not flow out, return the hand to the vessel of hot water and allow the blood to flow into the water.
Do this until you achieve your aim. If it is summer, you may not need to return the hand to the hot water; mostly this is necessary in wintertime. Opening this vein is safer than opening any other. This is because there are no major veins or nerves beneath it. Opening the radial vein on the right hand is beneficial for liver disorders. The procedure consists of tying a bandage around the wrist, or using your hand, after immersing the limb in hot water until the vein swells.
This is because there are no major veins or nerves beneath it. Opening the radial vein on the right hand is beneficial for liver disorders. The procedure consists of tying a bandage around the wrist, or using your hand, after immersing the limb in hot water until the vein swells. It will be clearly palpable to the touch; then make an oblique incision in it, and if you cut through completely this does not harm the patient at all. Be careful with your scalpel so as not to go too deep since beneath lies the nerve of the fingers and there is no flesh covering them here. After that, return the hand to the hot water.
Let it bleed freely, for if you do not return the hand to hot water, the blood will congeal in the vein's opening and stop flowing. Once you have taken out as much blood as necessary, apply some oil and salt to the vein so that it does not close too quickly.
You should treat every narrow vein in the same manner, and as for the benefit of phlebotomizing from the left hand, it is beneficial for illnesses related to the spleen. Thus you should perform phlebotomy here just as with the second [vein], they are equal. As for phlebotomizing the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower body such as uterine disorders.
Menstrual retention, kidney diseases, chronic ulcers of the thighs and legs, and similar illnesses can also be treated with phlebotomy. To perform this procedure, immerse your leg in hot water and massage it vigorously until the veins become engorged; then bind them above the ankle joint using a tight bandage, positioning the vein over the heel towards the thumb.
Many branches of this vein spread across the leg, so phlebotomize it at its widest point or near the heel where all its tributaries converge; this is safer and more effective. When phlebotomizing on the leg, be careful to avoid the nerves underlying the vein.
Perform the phlebotomy with a slight twist of the lancet, as if you intend to amputate it; use a sharp instrument for this purpose. If blood does not flow freely, re-immerse your leg in hot water and allow the blood to drain completely before proceeding. In case the phlebotomist misses the vein on first attempt...
...re-attempt the phlebotomy slightly higher up; this area is safer and carries less risk, provided you avoid damaging the nerve as explained earlier. You should perform the same procedure on the saphenous vein of the other leg with equal care. As for the sciatic nerve, its location, as mentioned before, is at the heel on the outer side of the foot.
Phlebotomy of this vein is beneficial for hip pain caused by hot blood. To perform the procedure, have the patient enter a bath and quickly bind their leg with a long, thin bandage from the hip to four fingers above the heel; this will make the vein prominent.
If it appears, then cauterize it in any condition that suits you - either obliquely, which is better, or by amputation or by incision. If its location is healthy and intact, it is usually very hidden in most people. If you cannot find it...
And if it is not apparent to the senses at all, cauterize some of its branches, which are those that appear on the back of the foot towards the little and ring fingers, being careful to avoid the nerves, and release as much blood as you wish.
Then release the tension, place some cotton wool on the cauterization site, and bind it tightly; it will heal quickly.
Chapter 96.
On cupping, and how to use it.
Cupping tools can be made from horns, wood, copper or glass; cupping has two forms: the first is wet-cupping where blood is drawn out, and the second is dry-cupping without incision.
This dry cupping has two forms...
They also include the temples on both sides of the neck, under the lower jaw below the mouth, shoulders, sacrum at the base of the spine, middle of upper arms, legs and calves.
Cupping draws blood from the delicate vessels spread throughout the flesh; for this reason it does not deplete the body's strength like bleeding, and cupping should therefore be used with or without fire in all diseases arising from fullness until the whole body is purged.
If need arises for bloodletting due to illness or as a customary practice, we employ it at any time, be it in the beginning of the month, its end, midway through, or whenever the situation may call for it.
For there are individuals among us whose excess blood, when left unchecked and requiring removal through bloodletting, results in a sense of heaviness and headaches. Others may experience a flushed appearance on their face, head, and neck. Some find relief in itching their faces, sides, and even develop dark circles under their eyes. And there are those who scratch at the very site of bloodletting.
Still others find themselves laughing excessively, while some detect the taste of blood in their mouths and experience bleeding gums. Blood may even be present in their saliva. Certain individuals sleep more than usual. There are those who, during sleep, envision blood, redness, corpses, and wounds akin to these. Whenever we observe any such symptoms...
Especially if these symptoms manifest during the middle third of the month, we then prescribe bloodletting after a couple or three hours have passed since daybreak. As for the benefits of bloodletting from the nape, it alleviates heaviness in the head and reduces excessive fluid flow towards the eyes.
However, it is imperative that this be done after the entire body has been purged of excess humor. This form of bloodletting may serve as an alternative to venesection at the temple. Caution must be exercised when employing this method on individuals with a cold constitution or those suffering from catarrh, for it can cause them great harm.
Hence, it should not be used on the elderly or those with cold-related ailments in their head. Overindulgence in this practice can lead to forgetfulness.
For this reason, one should instruct the barber to slightly lower the cupping glass away from the head, lest forgetfulness be induced. As for cupping at the scapula, it serves as an alternative to venesection at the ankle and wrist.
For this, it is beneficial for asthma, shortness of breath, disruption of the respiratory mechanism, coughing and congestion; a cupping session on the upper back should be performed with care as performing this too low can result in weakening of the heart and stomach.
Cupping of the carotid arteries is beneficial for headaches, eye disorders, migraines, croup and toothache; it serves as an alternative to venesection at the basilic vein, however you should instruct your cupper not to cut too deeply in case they sever an artery which would cause bleeding.
Cupping underneath the chin is beneficial for oral thrush, gum disease and other similar diseases of the mouth; it may serve as an alternative to venesection at the cheeks. Cupping on the shoulders is useful for a fluttering heartbeat caused by congestion or excess heat.
Cupping on the front of the elbows is beneficial for the same conditions as venesection at the basilic vein, cephalic vein and median antebrachial vein since it draws blood from all those smaller vessels within the flesh and drains those small veins into larger ones until the blood reaches these three thicker veins.
You should instruct your cupper not to cut too deeply as the region is devoid of much flesh and underlies nerves and arteries. Cupping with a single cup over the coccyx will benefit piles in the anus and sores on the perineum.
You should instruct your cupper to use a larger, copper cup as the region requires strong suction and a glass cup may break; this could cause further cuts in the flesh. Cupping on the legs alleviates congestion very noticeably.
For it attracts blood from all over the body and is beneficial for chronic pains in the kidneys, womb, and bladder. It aids menstruation and helps with blisters and boils. It serves as an alternative to cupping of the calves and Achilles tendon, but it greatly weakens the body and causes fainting in most people. Cupping of the Achilles tendons has a similar benefit to that of the legs.
The method of placing the cup is to first put it in place while empty and then suck it with moderate suction. Do not keep the cups in place for too long, but rather place them quickly and remove them swiftly so that the humors are drawn towards the site evenly. Keep repeating this action continuously until you see that the area has turned red and opened up.
Once you see the redness of blood, make an incision and resume sucking slowly. Then consider the state of the bodies; for those with soft flesh and loose pores, only one incision should be made lest the area become ulcerated.
Instruct the cupper to widen the incision and deepen it slightly, adjusting the suction gently with light movement. If the blood is thick, two incisions should be made; the first to create a pathway for the thinner parts of the blood, and the second to extract the thicker blood.
If the blood is very cloudy, repeat the incision a third time to reach its limit. In general, if we wish to extract little blood, one incision suffices; but for more blood, multiple incisions are required, especially if we suspect thick blood.
A deep incision should be made, with the moderate limit being just through the skin. We must consider which oils to use when placing cups, and which waters to avoid for the cupped person. For those with thick, hard, dry skin and narrow pores...
One should apply ointments to the cupping sites, using opening, softening and dissolving oils such as oil of marjoram, violet oil, sweet almond oil or pumpkin seed oil if it is summer time.
If it is winter, oils such as narcissus oil, iris oil, chamomile oil or lily oil should be used; if the excess [humors] are thick and cold then use mugwort oil, rue oil, pine oil or dill oil.
If the person undergoing cupping has wide pores and soft flesh, one should avoid using ointments; they should wash their cupped areas after cupping with rose water or cold water or gooseberry water or pumpkin water or purslane water.
For those with moist blood, the cupped areas should be washed with vinegar or myrtle and sumac water; if their humors are thick, use old wine, mugwort water, dill broth or chamomile water. One must take caution when having a cupping session in a bathhouse or immediately after bathing.
Instead, it should be performed one or two hours after leaving the bathhouse; a person undergoing cupping must not sleep afterwards. Here is what should be done by those having dry-cupping and wet-cupping before and after the procedure.
One should first assess if the person is choleric and their blood is predominantly acrid and inflammatory; in this case, one must take refrigerants such as pomegranates and dandelion with vinegar, lettuce, vinegar of vinegar (syrup of violets) and julep. Their diet should include broths made from capons or mutton, salads and similar.
For those of a cold temperament, it is advisable to drink mead, mibi wine or sengkangebin bzori and consume aromatic medium vintage wine, which falls between old and new vintages. They should be instructed to eat less and include chicks, sparrows, pigeons, and partridges in their diet. On the day of a venesection or bloodletting, drink more than you eat.
In some cases, it may be necessary to administer triacle, musk medicine or shelltha to certain people before bloodletting or venesection, or afterward, in order to strengthen the principal organs and thin the blood. It should not be given to those of a hot temperament.
Unscratched cups are used on the liver, spleen, breasts, abdomen, navel, kidney region, and hip joint because these organs cannot withstand scratching. The purpose is either to draw blood from one organ to another.
Placing cups on the breasts in cases of nosebleeds, or to release cold air trapped in an organ, as when we place cups on the abdomen and navel, they loosen the organ, warm it up, and alleviate pain by dispersing that air.
Cups may be placed on the kidneys if a blockage or stone is present, as their strong suction force might open the blockage or dislodge the stone from its position. Similarly, they are used on the liver and spleen to deal with gas, which cups loosen and warm to alleviate pain. These unscratched cups can be used simply for suction.
They may be used with fire or filled with warm water for cases of sciatica. The cup, which should be large, is filled with hot water alone or with water in which suitable herbs have been boiled.
Then it is placed full on the spot and held in place, then removed and replaced repeatedly as needed. This is an illustration of a fire syringe:.
Its mouth should be the size of two open fingers, as illustrated, and its depth is half a span. At about the middle of one side there should be a small hole large enough for a needle to pass through. It can be made from tin or yellow copper with a thick rim.
It should be smooth, flat and polished to avoid injuring the limb when placed upon it. In the middle there is a crossbar of copper or iron where the wax candle sits when lit. This fire syringe can also be made in larger sizes than described here or smaller according to what is required.
The diameter of the piston should be adapted to the patient's age and condition; fire syringes for children or emaciated patients are different from those used on robust adults. To use this fire syringe with heat on a limb, first light a tightly-woven linen wick or small pitch candle. Place it in the middle of the copper cylinder at the centre of the syringe.
This ensures the fire rises upwards towards the bottom of the syringe, away from the patient's body, so it does not get burned. Then place the syringe on the limb and cover the small hole we mentioned with your finger until you are ready to remove it.
Steam will then escape from that hole and the piston should loosen in its housing. Relight the wick as required and replace it if necessary. A water syringe used for treating shingles does not have a copper cylinder or a hole.
It is simply filled with water and placed on the limb. This is an illustration of such a syringe:.
This is the syringe; the larger it can hold a greater volume of water, the better.
Chapter 77.
On the use of leeches.
Leeches are mainly used in cases where it is not possible to apply cups due to the small size of certain body parts, such as the lips and gums, or when the affected area lacks sufficient flesh, like fingers and the nose.
The method of using leeches involves selecting those found in fresh, clean water free from any contamination. They are then left for a day and night in fresh water to ensure they are hungry and their stomachs are empty. The body is first purged by means of venesection or cupping, and the affected area is then cleaned.
Once the leech is placed on the affected area, it will fill with blood and fall off. If possible, suck out any remaining fluid from the wound using a cup. Otherwise, clean the area with vinegar followed by water, then massage and squeeze it. Should bleeding continue after the leech has fallen away, indicating excessive discharge, apply a cold, damp cloth to the site.
The cloth is placed over the wound until discharge ceases. If bleeding persists, apply powdered botargate or oak gall or similar astringents to stem the flow; alternatively, place peeled broad bean halves on the site.
Leave the broad beans in place until they adhere to the wound, at which point bleeding should stop. If leeches must be reapplied, use fresh ones if available; do not reuse previous leeches. Should a leech refuse to attach itself, clean the area with fresh blood.
When you insert a needle into the site until some blood emerges and then place [the medicine], if she feels something of the blood, it will adhere to the position. If you wish for it to fall off, sprinkle on it something of aloe or salt or ashes, and it shall fall at that very spot.
Chapter Three.
In algebra.
This chapter is also essential to what is needed in the practice of medicine, which is setting fractures and dislocations of bones.
Know, O my sons, that this chapter is claimed by the ignorant among physicians and laypeople, those who have never browsed an ancient book on it nor read a single letter from one.
For this reason, this art of knowledge has become non-existent in our country, and I have never come across anyone skilled in it at all. I acquired what I have of it only through lengthy reading of the books of the ancients and careful study to understand them until I extracted that knowledge from them, then I practiced and trained throughout my life.
I have outlined for you in this chapter all that is within the scope of my knowledge. My experience with it has gone beyond what I have presented to you, after having brought it close to you and purified it from the complexities of lengthiness and abbreviated it to the utmost brevity, made it clear to the utmost clarity, and depicted for you many images of the instruments used in it.
For this is from an increase in clarity, as I did in the previous two chapters, and there is no strength but by Allah, the Most High, the Almighty.
Chapter One.
There are maxims and comprehensive statements regarding bone fractures, which must be presented first. Before we start mentioning the broken and dislocated body parts individually, it is necessary to mention some general principles at the beginning of this chapter. It requires that you, as well as anyone keen on learning this noble profession, understand and grasp its true nature first and foremost.
Thus, I say that whenever a fracture or dislocation occurs in any part of the body, one should hasten to apply venesection and/or purgatives, or both, immediately. Unless there is a contraindication, such as debilitation or if the patient who suffered the injury is a child or an elderly person; unless it is extremely hot or very cold weather indeed.
Then, one should restrict their diet to cold legumes and poultry or goat meat, abstaining from alcoholic beverages, fatty meats, over-eating, and any nourishment that tends to increase the blood volume in the veins until there is no fear of swelling nor concern about an influx of fluid into the affected area.
At this point, the patient should return to their initial regimen as per their usual practice; once they see that the broken bone is beginning to heal and knit back together, it is appropriate for them to nourish themselves with highly nutritious dense food such as porridges, rice, headmeat and trotters, cow's tripe. And eggs, soft fish, strong alcoholic drinks and the like; with this regimen, bone healing is faster and stronger. Bear in mind that broken bones of sturdy men or elderly people will never heal back together exactly as they were before the fracture, but they can knit nonetheless.
Their bones became thinner and harder, and what was once connected and fused together were the softest of bones, like those of young children. However, nature grows around the broken bone on all sides a substance similar to glue which is thick and adheres, binding and tightening it until one piece adheres to another.
It binds some to the other until it becomes incredibly strong and secure, just as it was originally, so that nothing hinders its function.
For this reason, the patient's diet should consist of foods that are dense, sticky, and thick, as we have said. And know that fractures can vary according to the different types of bones, for a fracture of the shinbone differs from that of the skull, and so on.
Similarly, all the other bones differ from each other; we shall come to mention every type of fracture and explain it in its own chapter, distinct from others. The nature of a bone fracture can also vary within itself.
It may be a simple fracture without splintering, or it could run the length of the bone with splinters and protruding fragments; sometimes there is also an associated skin wound. Fractures can also occur as a minor crack.
Each type of fracture has its own specific method of repair, as will be explained in detail later. A broken bone can be identified by its deformity, swelling, and sensitivity to the touch; it may also produce a crackling sound when pressed with the hand.
If there is no obvious deformity, no crackling sound, no sensitivity or disturbance felt when palpating the bone, and the patient experiences only mild pain, then it is not a fracture but rather a sprain or minor crack which should not be manipulated by stretching or pressing.
Now apply to it the medicines which will be mentioned later on that suit the location, then bind it gently, and know that if a bone is broken into two pieces without shattering or splintering, but has moved apart from its counterpart.
You should proceed immediately to align and straighten it before it becomes a hot swelling, but if a hot swelling occurs then leave it for a few days until the swelling subsides, then straighten it with any degree of gentleness and cunning that you can.
Know that its consolidation and straightening is easier than for a bone with splinters, then it should be bound as will be mentioned later; if there are splinters in the bone then the broken limb must be stretched from both sides, whether it is an arm or leg.
Either by hand if the limb is small, or with two ropes, or a rope and a hand; place the limb on an even surface in its natural shape so that when the broken bone parts are stretched then you can reset those protrusions into their places with all your skill at cunning and gentleness.
Try your best to avoid causing the patient pain or anguish by what you do, and strain every effort to join one bone to its fellow in the best possible way; at that time you must touch and feel them with your hand, for if you find anything awry then correct it and straighten it as best you can.
Beware of stretching too hard or using excessive force as many ignorant people do, and often their actions cause a hot swelling or permanent injury to the limb, something I have witnessed numerous times from their practice.
Then, after making it level, perfect and tight, keep that organ still and quiet; warn the patient not to move it while awake or asleep, when shifting positions, in a state of agitation, during defecation, and at all times of movement as much as possible, being careful to ensure that the organ's position is one where pain can be avoided.
For when the patient feels pain or discomfort in any position, they should move it to another that causes no pain; being careful again to ensure the organ is level and straight.
Otherwise, the organ may become crooked if forced; and as for how to tighten a broken organ: know that these organs differ in their size, shape, and form - those which are small, such as the arm, finger, or forearm.
Thus, for smaller organs, the strips of cloth should be soft and gentle; while for larger ones such as the thigh, back or chest, the wraps must be broad and firm since wide bindings hold a large organ firmly in place and tighten it equally on all sides without fault.
Once you have finished levelling, begin by applying the appropriate plaster. On a soft and tender spot near the break, begin to apply plaster; then start binding with three or four wraps directly over the fracture itself, tightening your hand slightly with each wrap before moving up towards the upper part of the injury.
But as you move away from the fracture, ease up on the tightness gradually until you reach a point that is just right; then take another bandage and bind it over the broken spot once more.
Bandages are applied, then the bandage is taken to the lower side of the fracture and its tension should be adjusted as mentioned previously for the upper one. Then, between the bandages, some soft padding or cloth is placed to even out the angulation of the fracture.
If there is no angulation, then nothing should be placed [between the bandages]. Another bandage is wrapped around it and on these bandages plaster splints are applied firmly from your side if there is no puffiness or hot swelling in the member.
If there is puffiness or hot swelling, then apply something to alleviate the latter and eliminate the former and leave it for some days; then tighten the splints upon it. The splints should be made of wide halves of reeds that have been carved carefully, or they may be made of wooden grille slats.
which are made of pine, palm leaves, heather, or madder and the like of what is available thereof; and each splint should be of this size, shape, and form.
except that the splint which is placed directly on the fracture itself should be slightly thicker and wider than the other splints; as for the length of the splint, it should be made according to what suits the broken member in terms of its size being large or small. Illustration of a splint: [image].
Then another bandage is tightened over the splints in exactly the same way as the first one; then it is tied from above with tight threads according to what we mentioned about tension, which is that your tightening should be on the site of fracture more than elsewhere. And the further away from the fracture, the less the tension.
The threads should be of medium thickness and fineness, and they should be made from wet linen especially. For if the threads are thick, as I have seen some ignorant people do with their threads made from braided linen strips, it is a great mistake.
Because the tension with them is beyond moderation; and fine threads are also not suitable because you cannot achieve the desired tension with them. There should be no less than a finger's width between one splint and another.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the splints after they have been properly tightened, then place underneath them some soft padding or loose wool so that it does not harm him in any way. But if there is a fracture along with a wound and the skin has been pierced, we shall come to this separately.
Be aware that not every broken limb should be put into splints on the first day, for if it is a large limb, splints ought to be applied only after five or seven days - or longer - depending on how sure you are about avoiding acute swelling.
This is the description of the bandages that were formerly used to treat fractures, dislocations and contusions: a brief generic bandage for treating fracture, suitable for most temperaments - especially boys and women - because it does not become unbearably hot or cold.
Take the mill dust, which is the flour clinging to the walls of the mill when it is set in motion; mix this as is without sifting and knead it with egg white until you have a paste that is neither thick nor thin. Then roll out the dough.
A description of another dressing that forces fracture, dislocation and bruise; take mastic, ladanum, acacia, rasin, gallnuts, pitch: each ten drachms; myrrh and aloe: each five drams; sloes twenty drachms; Armenian or Roman clay: twenty drachms. All are pounded and mixed with sloe water or egg whites if the patient's temperament is hot, then this dressing may be used; it has a good composition, quickly setting broken bones and suitable for most people due to its moderation.
A description of another dressing that is also beneficial for fracture and bruise: take gallnuts, psyllium, white marshmallow, each ten drachms; myrrh and aloe, each five drams; acacia six drachms; Armenian clay twenty drachms; pound all very fine, sift and knead with water or egg whites, then apply.
A description of a dressing for joints and bones that have moved from their places; it soothes pain associated with this, bone splinters, and fractures: take grey wool, soak in vinegar and cooked oil, apply to the site; this dressing does not have bone-setting power.
But it is excellent for soothing hot swellings and driving off pains especially: a description of another dressing that sets broken bones: take the leaves of the fig-tree, and the leaves of wild poppy; pound both together and dress while moist; a description of an abbreviated dressing to be used when bone fracture has set.
And if you wish to disperse the rest of a swelling, take marshmallow root, chamomile, violets, and caraway seeds; each in equal parts: pound all together and knead with liniment unless the part is sensitive, then use coriander water or plain water.
Description of another dressing, more powerful in its ability to dissolve [humors], which is used when there is a hard swelling upon the union of bones: take from each one part of marshmallow root, flaxseed, fenugreek, crown of the king, chamomile flowers, violet flowers and marjoram.
All of these [ingredients] are ground and kneaded with aloe vera juice or sweet water, or with paint; whichever according to the warmth of the limb and its fiery nature; however, Hippocrates does not mention in his book that it should be placed on a broken bone when it is set.
Except for the caustic [medicated plaster], made solely of wax and oil, he describes it as being medium between thick and thin; however, Galen recommends that things containing dryness with a touch of heat such as myrrh, aloe vera, frankincense, etc., be placed on the broken limb when setting it.
Description of a dressing beneficial for weakness and pain: take gum tragacanth, chickpeas, human hair cut up or bird feathers, marshmallow root, and salt in equal parts; grind them, sift them, then knead [them] and dress [the wound]; as to the amount that the bandage should remain...
... then remove it: you should examine the patient; if there is no pain, itching or movement of the broken bone from its place, then do not remove [the dressing] for many days; however, if there is severe itching in that area or disturbing pain or swelling...
Then promptly remove it at the right time without any delay whatsoever and move the dressing away from him; then take a soft cloth or fresh sea-sponge, moistened with tepid water, immerse [it] in lukewarm water and wash that place until the itching calms down and pain subsides; then leave the limb to rest for an hour.
Then you should apply padded wool, soaked in vinegar and oil or rosewater essence, and bandage it overnight until the hot swelling subsides, the member's pulsation calms down, and the pain vanishes. Then return to a light traction with a gentle dressing, not as tight as before.
Be gentle with the member until it is healed; if you see that the swelling, redness, pain and pulsation have all disappeared but still need dressing and traction then return to what you did first; equal in its manner. If none of these symptoms appear on the member, then do not bother doing anything mentioned above.
Do not remove it except after three, four, five or seven days; sometimes twenty days can be left in place depending on the member's condition as we have mentioned until if bone union is required and flesh fusion approaches.
Then increase the traction more than before and also thicken the diet of the patient beyond what has been mentioned, if you see that the fracture site has dried out and weakened more than it should know then that food is prevented from reaching this location.
Soak it with lukewarm water when you remove the dressing every three days and ease up on traction a little; doing so makes food reach the member quickly, thus aiding in quick recovery. As for what is done by amateurs among bone setters of breaking the member again..
If it doesn't heal properly the first time and heals with a misalignment, this is an error on their part; great deception! Had this been correct, previous generations would have noted it in their books and practiced upon it but I've seen no trace of any such thing from anyone amongst them whatsoever. And it is right that this should not be performed.
Chapter Two.
In accidental fracture of the head.
There are numerous types of accidental head fractures, with varying shapes and causes. One type is caused by a sword strike, which may result in the complete shattering of the bone down to the periosteum underneath. As the adze does with wood.
This type of fracture, characterized by the bone being completely shattered like wood by an adze, is hence called "adze-like." Alternatively, the sword may only cut through part of the bone and chip its surface without penetrating all the way through; this kind of fracture is referred to as an "absolute avulsion," with the resulting wound being either large or small.
Another type of fracture is "crushing" or "contusion," caused by a blow from a rock, a fall onto a stone, or the like. This fracture may either penetrate close to the membrane beneath the bone or remain on its surface.
The wound resulting from these fractures can also be either large or small. There is another type of fracture, a slight fissure hidden within the bone's thinness akin to that of hair.
This type is named "hair-like" due to this reason. Another fracture occurs from a fall or rock impact, where a piece of the bone is driven inward, causing an indentation similar to that seen on a copper pot after being struck.
This phenomenon is most commonly observed in the skulls of children, whose bones are softer. All these fracture types may have splintered or un-splintered fragments, and we will discuss their treatment in detail later. We identify all these fracture types through careful examination.
Inspect it with probes and remove the rotten flesh from it, and as for the fleshy type, it is known by exposing the bone, wiping it clean, and applying ink to it; if there is a fracture, it will appear black. As for treating the fracture, first consider the symptoms of the patient.
If you observe symptoms that clearly indicate fear, such as vomiting bile, prostration, loss of reason, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, bulging and reddening eyes, and the like, do not approach or treat the patient; death will inevitably befall them in most cases with these symptoms.
If you encounter symptoms that do not alarm you and believe the patient will recover, then begin their treatment. This is when the injured person comes to you soon after the injury, especially during the winter days.
You should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day under any circumstances. If it is during the summer days, you must hasten the removal of the bone before the seventh day, lest the membrane beneath the bone becomes corrupted and presents the symptoms we have described.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and is accompanied by crushing and bruising, you must cut away the crushed and bruised part as I shall describe: shave the head of the injured patient.
Expose the bone in any way that is feasible and according to the shape of the wound, choosing the method that causes the least distress to the patient. If you encounter bleeding or a hot swelling during exposure, treat it accordingly.
Pack the wound with cloth soaked in rose wine and oil; cleanse it to ease the swelling and prevent bleeding. Then proceed to remove the bone, which can be done in one of two ways.
As for the single-faced saw, you must cut the bone with a fine-toothed, narrow-bladed saw. This is its image.
Then you use another saw after this one, which is slightly wider. This is its image.
Then you also use another saw, which is wider than the second one. Indeed, you must have several different saws, some being wider and some shorter, all very sharp at their edges. They should be made of Indian iron or good steel.
Use gentle blows when striking the saw to prevent shaking and damaging the head. If the bone is strong and hard, you should first drill around it before using the saws with drills they call non-diving drills.
Because they do not go beyond the limit of the skull bone to what is behind it, due to the drill having a round edge below its sharp head, resembling a ring or small circle, which prevents it from diving and penetrating beyond the thickness of the bone.
You should have a large number of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone, so that you are prepared with a drill whose sharp edge matches the length and width according to the skull's thickness. Here are images of three types. Of the drills, there is large, medium, and small.
As for the way to drill around the broken bone, place the drill on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you feel that the bone has been penetrated. Then move the drill to another spot, leaving a space between each hole equivalent to the thickness of a rod or something similar.
Then cut with scissors between each pair of holes, doing so as gently as you can, as we have said, until the bone is removed either by hand or with something else from among the tools prepared for this.
Like forceps and fine hooks, you should be very careful that the trephine or scissors do not touch any of the peritoneum. Once you have drilled out the bone and removed it from the peritoneum if it was attached to it, you must then strip it.
You should smooth any roughness left in the bone with another instrument similar to scissors but it must be thinner and finer than the other scissors. If there are any small pieces of bone or fragments, you can remove them gently using one of your instruments.
Then you should treat the wound with sutures and ointments, which will be mentioned later. The other method is very simple and without risk. Galen mentions it and praises it greatly; these are his words. You should start by exposing the bone in the area where the fracture is most severe and noticeable. Once you have exposed that part, place the tip of this lenticular scissors underneath it. This is an image of it:
The lenticular part of it is smooth and does not cut anything, while the sharp part has edges running along its length as you can see. This allows the lenticular part to rest on the peritoneum with the sharp edge of the scissors facing the bone. You then strike the scissors from one side.
With a small hammer, strike the scissors gently to cut through all of the bone as it rotates. While doing this, you are safe from damaging the membrane; there is no risk at all, even if the surgeon is the most unskilled and cowardly person, or even drowsy.
If there remains any membrane attached to the bone, in some places of the bone, gently scrape it away with the edge of the same lenticular scalpel and remove it carefully; for it will separate from it without injury or fear.
If the bone fracture has not penetrated the membrane and only its surface is broken, leaving roughness and small fragments, you should smooth out that roughness and extract those fragments with fine chisels of various sizes, so that you can use in each place the most suitable and appropriate one according to what the operation requires.
The same bone and its shape: you should use the widest chisels first to smooth out the bone, then use finer ones after that, and continue doing so until you arrive at using the finest and thinnest of them all.
As for the other small fissures and fine fractures, you should treat each one according to what will repair it - something that is not hidden from anyone with even a little skill in this craft who has read and understood what we have written about major fractures.
If the membrane remains exposed after removing the bone, you should take a piece of linen cloth as large as the wound, soak it in rose oil, and place it over the opening; then take another folded or triangular cloth. Soak this cloth in a mixture of rose oil and wine, place it on top of the first cloth, and do so as gently as possible to avoid burdening the membrane; then use a wide bandage above it without tightening it beyond what is needed to hold the wound together.
Leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the hot swelling subsides, then remove it and use afterward medicines that are more drying, such as iris roots, flour of caraway, powdered benzoin, and mezereon among others; these are made into powders.
