Sprinkle the powder on the wound as it is dry. In general, use a medicine that cleanses without burning and take great care to keep the wound clean, free from grease or dirt, and prevent any pus from forming.
Because if pus gathers over the brain's membrane, it will corrupt and rot it, causing great harm to the patient. This can happen in the skull cavity when bone is removed, especially if treatment is neglected.
If there is any blackening on its surface and the patient presents with the symptoms we mentioned, then know that he will surely die. Unless this blackening was caused by a medicine applied to it and this medicine had the power to cause such blackening.
You should take one part honey and three parts rose oil, mix them well, spread it on a cloth, then place this on the skull cavity before treating with the appropriate medicine until healed.
Chapter Three.
On setting a broken nose.
Know that only the upper parts of the nose, both or one, can break as these are bone; the lower part is cartilage and can only be bruised, bent, or flattened.
If one side of it is broken, you should insert your little finger into the nostril and align that fracture from within with your index finger and thumb from without until the nose returns to its natural shape. Be gentle in doing this, and take care not to cause pain to the patient.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and you cannot reach it with your finger, it should be aligned using a slightly thick rod. If the fracture occurs on both sides, do likewise. The setting must take place on the first day of the fracture.
If that is not possible, then after the seventh or tenth day when the hot swelling subsides, you should insert a wick made of linen cloth into the nostril; if the fracture is on one side, use one wick; if it is on both sides, use two wicks.
The wicks should be thick enough to fill the nostril. Some early orthopedists suggested wetting the wicks with butter and changing them daily, but I do not agree; instead, they should be moistened with egg white mixed with flour.
Then you should leave the wicks in place until the bone is stabilized and the cartilage has hardened. You may also insert goose feather quills, wrapped in soft cloth, into the nose in the place of the wicks; this provides a stronger restraint for the broken nose, and it also allows the patient to breathe more easily.
This step is not essential, but you may do it if you wish; alternatively, you can use wicks. If a hot swelling occurs during your work, apply a compress to the nose made of qirouti or cotton dipped in vinegar and rose oil, or some ointment of diakylion.
If there is no hot swelling, then it should be treated externally with fine semolina flour and myrrh powder mixed with egg white, after which a soft dressing is applied, and do not bandage the nose at all; if the nasal bones are broken in small pieces.
If they are broken into small pieces, then it is necessary to make an incision and remove them with the appropriate instrument; after which you stitch up the wound and treat it with ointments that promote healing and closure of the wound. If there is an internal injury in the nose, it must be treated with sutures and lead pipes should be used. until it heals.
Chapter four.
On the setting of lower jaws if they break.
If the lower jaw is broken without an open wound, and if the fracture is external only, with no break in two pieces or depression inward, then identifying it will be easy.
If the fracture is on the right side, insert the index finger of your left hand into the patient's mouth; and if it is on the left side, use the index finger of your right hand to gently lift the bump caused by the break from inside towards outside.
If the jawbone has broken into two pieces, you must use traction from both sides to realign it until it is straight and even; if there are any injuries to the teeth as well, they should also be treated.
If the teeth are loose, they should be extracted and then reattached using gold or silver wire; an incision is made in the broken jaw to place gold foil inside it before applying a bandage over that area of the jaw with a folded cloth underneath. A large splint or piece of leather shoe sole matching the length of the jaw would be used on top of this layer as a final measure for stability and support during recovery process. Then you bind it from above according to how you see fit, so that the binding is tight enough to hold but not too tight as to cause disruption and order the patient to rest and be still. You should make sure their diet consists of soft porridge. If you suspect any change in shape or form has occurred then immediately untie the bandage on day three.
Then you bind it from above according to how you see fit, so that the binding is tight enough to hold but not too tight as to cause disruption and order the patient to rest and be still. You should make sure their diet consists of soft porridge. If you suspect any change in shape or form has occurred then immediately untie the bandage on day three. You then repair the dressing with flour of the mill and egg white, or with semolina powder after removing the splint from it. You place on top a soft cloth, as long as that dressing adheres to it and there is no change in the condition of the bone you can leave it.
Do not remove it until the wound heals and the fracture has set. Often, this can take three weeks. If during that time a hot swelling appears then treat it as we have mentioned several times before to reduce inflammation.
If the fracture is accompanied by a wound, you must first examine it to see if there are any bone fragments present and treat them accordingly. Gently extract those fragments using an appropriate instrument. If the opening of the wound is narrow then use a scalpel to widen it as much as necessary.
Once you have extracted all the bone fragments, then close the wound if it is wide and apply an appropriate ointment to it. Stitch the wound until it heals.
Chapter Five.
On the setting of a broken clavicle.
The clavicle most commonly breaks at the front near its acromial end, and there are three types of fracture that can occur: a simple, complete break without any fragments; a comminuted fracture with two large pieces; or a fracture with several small bone fragments.
If there are fragments in the fracture, which is more difficult to set, or if the fracture is accompanied by a wound, then the treatment if the fracture is not associated with a wound is as follows: you should have two servants present; one of them holds the humerus next to the broken clavicle while the other extends the neck towards the opposite side.
Then you should align the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it ought to be, without any bumps or bends. If you need more extension, you should place a ball of rags or wool under the patient's armpit.
The bone should be of the size you need. You extend and lift the clavicle, pressing the ball with your hand until the fracture is aligned as it ought to be. If you cannot pull the end of the clavicle outwards because it has moved deeper, then the patient should lie on their back.
A medium-sized pillow should be placed under their shoulder, and the servant presses down on their shoulder until the clavicle bone that has moved deeper rises upwards. Then you can proceed to fix the fracture, aligning it with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the clavicle has broken off and is moving, then it should be incised upon, and that fragment should be gently removed. If the fragment is lodged within the bone, then maneuver to cut it with one of the cutters prepared for that purpose. Once the instrument to protect the pleura is placed beneath the clavicle, which is an instrument made of wood or iron and this is its image:
It resembles a spoon without a bend, its width according to the size of the bone, large or small. As for its length, it should also be suitable for the task at hand. Let it have two ends as you see; one end is wider and the other narrower.
If the wound hole that you have opened for extracting a bone splinter is wide and you feel safe from the hot inflammation, then bring together the lips of the wound with stitches. If the hole is small or if you fear the hot inflammation, pack the wound with lint and bandages according to the size of the incision.
If a hot inflammation appears, moisten the hole with rose oil and vinegar or wine, and apply a dressing to it. As for binding bones in place if there is no wound nor incision, you should use a dressing made from millstone powder mixed with egg white. You should place soft padding over it, then a pillow under the armpit if required. Take a very long turban, about an inch wide or so, and place a folded bandage on top of the padding and dressing. Then take a splint made from thin wood. It should be three fingers wide and the same in length, then wrap it with a cloth, followed by wrapping both the cloth and splint together into the turban at the site of the fracture. Then secure the turban around the fracture as you would normally wind it. Place it around the neck, under the right armpit and also under the affected armpit, then wind it back and forth over each side several times as you see fit to ensure that the fracture is secured in place properly.
Your priority throughout should be to ensure that the splint does not move away from over the broken bone. For this reason, you should check on the patient daily and whenever you see that the bandage has loosened or moved away from over the broken bone then rectify it by tightening the bandage again. Then ensure that they sleep on their back with a small pillow under the affected armpit at night.
The patient's arm is tied to his neck, raising the broken clavicle along with the shoulder. The bandages are not removed until twelve days have passed without any incident of itching or swelling at the fracture site.
Then change the bandage if you see fit to do so. Apply traction to the fracture site, and leave it until the clavicle heals and consolidates over a period of twenty-eight days. Some people may take less time to heal than others.
Chapter six.
On the setting of a broken shoulder. The shoulder is rarely fractured at its widest part; rather, it is the bones that comprise the shoulder which are prone to fracture. By palpating the injured area one can identify whether or not there is indeed a break present and this will guide how one proceeds to set such fractures.
Set the bone back into its normal position using whichever technique is best suited to the fracture. Then, pack the area with ground flour mixed with egg white and a soft poultice, place a pad of folded cloth above this, then secure in place with a broad splint made from wood.
If the splint is narrower than the shoulder or slightly wider, and if there are any bumps underneath it in the area of the shoulder, then smooth these out with a soft poultice until the splint sits evenly across the shoulder. Then secure this from above using a long bandage.
The bandage should be tightly secured and one must ensure that the splint does not move from its position. One must check the dressing daily; each time the bandage loosens, it should be tightened once more, and the splint repositioned if it has slipped out of place. The patient should lie on his healthy side, and a shoulder fracture will typically heal over twenty days.
If it takes one day or twenty-five days, once this period is complete, you can safely remove the bandage as there should be no fear of fracturing or disturbing the bone, which is strong and resilient. However, if a fragment of bone protrudes and punctures the skin, make an incision to expose it and carefully extract the splinter. Apply the same treatment for fractures of the collarbone, addressing any hot swelling that may occur.
Chapter Seven.
On the repair of broken ribs.
The chest can fracture in the middle, which is less common, but more frequently fractures occur at the ends and splinter into fragments. Symptoms of a central fracture include the lower part tilting downwards accompanied by severe pain, difficulty breathing, and coughing.
Perhaps blood is coughed up, and the depression in the broken bone is visible to the touch. To repair it, have the patient lie on their back with a pillow under their shoulders. Then press down on their shoulder blades while bringing their ribs together gently by hand from both sides, doing your best to realign them smoothly.
Adjust until the bone regains its proper shape, then apply a bandage and splint made of light wood such as willow or heather wrapped in cloth. Gently secure the splint to the broken bone, ensuring it does not move. Wrap the bandage around in circles several times, securing it tightly but carefully. Check the bandage regularly and tighten it whenever necessary.
If necessity requires its removal due to a sore or pain that appears in the wound, proceed with removing it promptly. Extract the dressing and treat any issues that arise according to the necessary treatment protocol. Then reapply the dressing if deemed appropriate. Treat it head on, applying firm pressure until the wound heals.
Chapter Eight.
In the setting of broken ribs, be aware that fractures occur in thick parts adjacent to the back and at their front ends; this is because they are cartilaginous. This fact should not escape detection upon palpation with your fingers.
Set the fracture by aligning it with your fingers to achieve the correct position, then dress and splint the broken bone if needed. If the ribs are fractured inwards, this can result in severe pain for the patient.
And puncture it like a puncture that occurs in someone with an internal thorn; this is due to the bone piercing the diaphragm. It can also result in shortness of breath, coughing and spitting up blood - these symptoms are difficult to treat. The ancients employed many clever methods for dealing with such cases.
Some have said that the patient's diet should include foods which cause gas and wind to generate bloating, expanding the abdomen outwardly in order for the fracture to heal; we reject this idea as it may exacerbate a hot swelling if one is present or cause one to occur if not already present.
Others have suggested applying a cupping scarifier to the affected area, then extracting with force; this is reasonable but risks drawing fluid into the wound due to its weakened state. Some have also proposed covering the site with wool soaked in hot oil.
And the sutures become prominent between the ribs until they are filled, making the ligament level when you wrap it around in a circle. Then treat the patient with dietary and medicinal treatment for fracture, but if the patient finds this very difficult to bear. And the bone was piercing the diaphragm, causing harm and we feared for the patient. Thus, we should make an incision over the site and expose the broken rib, then place beneath it the instrument that protects the peritoneum which was described earlier, and cut the bone carefully before removing it.
Then we suture the lips of the wound if it is large, and treat it with ointments until healing occurs. If a hot swelling appears during this time, then quickly soak some dressings in rose oil and place them over the site. Also, treat the patient internally with medicines that reduce swelling.
They should lie on their side, whichever is less painful for sleeping, until healing occurs.
Chapter 9.
On setting broken vertebrae of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if they incur a fracture - which is rare but most commonly due to contusion - and you wish to know whether the patient will heal or not... Look at the patient's hands: if you see them relaxed, numb and lifeless, unable to move or extend/clench, such that when you prick them with a needle they feel no pain nor any sensation therein - then know in most cases they will not heal but die.
If they can move their hands and feel them when pricked/prodded, then know the spinal cord is intact and the patient will heal with treatment. If the vertebrae of the back are affected in a similar manner, and you wish to know if they too will heal...
Examine his legs; if you see that they have relaxed and developed the same symptoms as the hands, then when he lies on his back, wind and stool are discharged involuntarily, and when he lies on his belly, urine is discharged involuntarily. And if he lies on his back and wishes to urinate but is unable, then know that he will die; do not trouble yourself with treating him. If none of these symptoms present themselves, the case would be less severe. The treatment for what has occurred is to aim to reduce the hot swelling.
Apply rose oil, or roast egg yolks and apply them to the injured vertebrae three times a day until the hot swelling subsides; then use one of the strengthening dressings that dry out [the area], bind it tightly with bandages, and instruct him in rest.
He should only sleep on the side that does not cause him pain until he is healed. If there was a splinter or some such injury to the bone and it has now sealed over, then you must cut open the skin and remove this bone. Then you must sew together the lips of any large wound, then treat it with healing ointments until it is healed. If the last bone of the coccyx, or tailbone, has been broken, then insert your left thumb into his anus.
With your other hand, align the broken bone as best you can and wrap it with a bandage or splint if necessary, then bind it tightly.
If you feel a broken splinter in there, then cut it open and remove the splinter; treat the wound with one of the treatments mentioned previously until it heals.
Chapter ten.
In setting the fracture of the hip bone.
Fractures of the hip bone are rare; when they do occur, the bone shatters at its ends and splits vertically, leaning inwards. The patient experiences pain at the site, pricking and numbness in the leg on that side.
To set it, pass your hand over it until you identify the fracture and its shape. If the fracture is limited to its ends only, restore its shape according to what appears suitable to resemble its natural form.
If the fracture is vertical or if it leans inwards, place the patient on their stomach until you can reset this fracture; once you have done so, bandage it and then apply a wooden splint or one made of leather, securing it tightly.
Do not fear displacement of the fracture or dislodgement of the splint, and correct any concavity at the waist with filling material to enable even compression; instruct the patient to sleep on their back or unaffected side, should hot swelling occur.
Cease any traction or resetting until the hot swelling subsides, then treat it as previously described and return to resetting and compressing as appropriate; should fragments of bone or shattered pieces at its ends be present, they must not be removed.
Do not disturb these but rather reset them externally as described earlier, then leave the splint in place until healing is complete.
Chapter eleven.
On setting fractures of the humerus.
The humerus is the bone between the elbow and shoulder joint. If it breaks, its setting proceeds in one of two ways: either by taking a curved, smooth rod of medium thickness that looks like this:.
Attach two ligatures at its ends, then suspend it from a high place and have the patient sit on a chair; next, lay his broken arm upon the rod such that his armpit sticks to the middle of its curve, then hang something heavy above him.
Or a servant stretches it downwards while the doctor aligns the fracture with both hands until he puts the break back in place; the other method is to have the patient lie on his back and attach his hand at his neck by means of a ligature.
Then instruct two servants to hold the arm above and below the fracture, respectively; each one of them stretches it in his direction. If you want more stretch, fasten a ligature under the break with another above it, then have each servant pull in their respective directions.
If the break is near the shoulder joint, the ligature should be placed under the armpit and another one below the fracture towards the elbow; likewise, if it's close to the elbow, you place the ligatures on those spots.
Then align the fracture gently without violence; once it's aligned properly and has healed well, you can tighten it unless a hot swelling appears, in which case leave its tightening for the seventh day and place over it a piece of wool soaked with vinegar and rose oil.
When the swelling has subsided, then apply the bandage; and the way to do this is: place the pad on the fracture, cover it with a roll of new cloth, gather the arm around the upper-arm itself, placing the hand open upon the shoulder.
Cover the cloth and bandage on upper arm and forearm to serve as a splint, unless something prevents you from doing so or if there is any change in the broken bone, in which case use a splint instead.
Use a splint that is wider and stronger than the others, placed directly on the fracture itself; leave a finger's breadth between each splint, with their length determined by the fracture plus three fingers' width at either end.
Then bandage it as described in the first chapter.
Make sure that the bandaging is tighter around the area of the fracture itself.
The further the fracture is from its original position, the looser the bandaging should be. If you consider it appropriate to apply splints and bandages as we have described at the moment of treating the injury, then do so.
If you fear that there may be a hot swelling, leave off the bandaging and splints until the seventh day as we have described earlier; then check the bandage every three days to prevent itching or puffiness developing in the area, or to ensure that nourishment continues to reach the limb unhindered by excessive tightness of binding. Repair any such problems according to what has been mentioned above.
If you are confident that none of these problems will occur, leave the bandage in place for many days; have the patient lie on their back with their hand resting on their stomach and a pillow stuffed full of wool placed beneath their upper arm to support it comfortably.
Inspect at all times, day or night, to ensure the broken limb maintains its shape and the bandage does not loosen. Repair any damage diligently. Provide the patient with a diet as previously described; begin with light food until the bone begins to mend.
You should gradually increase the patient's diet. The upper arm and leg usually take forty days to heal, at which point you must remove the bandage; they may then use a bath and be treated with ointments suitable for that purpose. If the break was severe and damaged.
Do not remove the bandage until fifty days or two months have passed.
Chapter Twelve.
On setting broken arms.
The arm consists of two bones, called the ulna and radius; the smaller one is next to the thumb, while the larger rests underneath it. The larger bone may break alone or the smaller one by itself; they can also fracture together.
Should the upper smaller bone fracture, it will be easier to set and heal quickly. If the lower larger one breaks, it is a worse injury that takes longer to recover from. The worst scenario would be if both bones break at once. If only the smaller bone is broken...
The physician must extend the limb gently until it is aligned when setting the smaller bone. If the larger one breaks, a more pronounced extension is necessary. In the event of both bones breaking, even greater force must be applied.
The hand's shape should be positioned during setting and extension, laid out flat on a pillow, with the thumb elevated above all fingers, and the little finger lower than all other fingers. The patient should sit cross-legged.
The pillow should be at an equal height to avoid any strain for the patient.
Then the assistant should extend the arm from below, either by hand or with a bandage. Another assistant stretches it from above as well. The physician then aligns the bone to restore it to its best possible shape. If there are fragments in the broken bone, they must be removed.
Return each fragment to its place as best you can. If an extruded fragment is seen, and it pierces the skin but cannot be set, then make an incision over it and remove it in the manner described previously. In the case of a fracture accompanied by a wound, this has been dealt with in a separate chapter.
Refer to that chapter for the treatment of this. If a hot swelling appears at the beginning of setting, apply a liniment of al-Qiratti made with rose oil and white wax. It should be of medium thickness between thick and thin, and gently tied onto the swelling until it subsides.
Remove the al-Qiratti preparation, apply a dressing made of fine wheat flour and egg white, then replace the splints. The splint that goes over the fracture should be slightly wider and stronger. Note that there are usually six splints used for an arm fracture.
If the fracture is in one or both ulna bones, then tighten your splinting over the fracture site stronger and tighter. As you wrap upwards or downwards, make the wrapping slightly looser according to what was mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
Let the bandages that are wrapped around the fracture be soft and moist, not very hard; let the thread with which it is bound be made of linen, especially one that is medium between thin and thick as we have described, and inspect the limb and binding after several days.
If something needs to be rectified, like itching occurring in the limb, it is best that the limb be sponged with warm water until this itching subsides and let the limb remain unbound for a night so as to rest then resume binding.
If the binding has loosened and the bone has shifted or something similar, then repair all of that to your utmost ability; also look over it carefully for if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive binding you must loosen it slightly and let several days pass so as nourishment may reach it then resume binding.
If nothing we have mentioned happens to the patient then it should not be unbound except after twenty days or something similar, then hang the patient's arm from their neck and let his forearm rest horizontally; he must do his utmost to avoid erratic movements and should sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm will be healed in thirty days or perhaps in thirty-two; and it may even heal in twenty-eight days, all depending on temperaments and conditions of strength.
Chapter 13.
On the setting of fractures of the hand and fingers.
The carpal bones of the hand and phalanges are rarely subject to fractures but rather contusions occur frequently; thus, whenever a fracture or contusion occurs in the hand...
The patient should sit cross-legged with a chair in front of them at the same level.
Then he places his hand on it, stretched out; then the bone-setter extends the broken bones and the physician aligns them until they unite well. When this is done, the bandage should be applied along with the splint if no hot swelling occurs, then a splint is placed over the top according to the location.
Having wrapped it in a soft cloth, if the fracture is directed downwards towards the palm of the hand then make something like a ball out of some cloth and have the patient grasp this with their broken hand before you then tighten it with a long strip of cloth.
The splint should be made of soft leather so that it conforms to the flexures in the palm and you tighten this as required; if, however, the fracture is directed outwards then a splint must be applied above and another below at the hand such that the hand remains open and outstretched.
The tightening is then carried out as the hand rotates and you tie between the fingers; if, however, the fracture involves one of the finger bones then for the thumb this must be aligned before being tightened with the palm or, if preferred, a small upright splint may be applied to support the fracture.
The bone must not move; for fractures to the other fingers such as the middle, index, little or ring then these are aligned and tied alongside the next healthy finger or you may tie them all together which is better still or place a small upright splint upon them.
As we said for the thumb, check during and after reduction for hot swelling which you treat as previously described.
Chapter Fourteen.
The Setting of the Femur Fracture.
The thighbone is often fractured and can be felt because it moves forward and backward, and its fracture is reduced by a bandage above the break and another below it; the patient must lie on his face during this operation.
Then each bandage is drawn taut, one to either side evenly; this if the fracture be in the middle of the bone, but when it is near the hip joint, a soft bandage should be used, made of wool or some such thing.
In a fracture near the hip joint, the bandage is applied higher up towards the groin to give extension upwards; and the other below the break. The same with fractures close to the knee, when the bandage must be placed lower down so as to extend downwards. Then the surgeon should reduce the bone by both hands to restore it to its natural shape.
The bone will unite well, and then it is necessary to maintain the bandaging and reduction unless there should be a hot swelling of the limb; but if such occurs, wait several days until this subsides before resuming treatment.
As for the bandaging, a stiff and wide turban should be wound twice or thrice around it in splints; leaving some remaining length to be pulled tight over this. Then twist the leg so that the heel reaches near the buttock joint and pass a long cord between thigh and shin just below the knee, bringing its ends up on either side above.
The remaining length is then wound around the leg and thigh, followed by splints applied to the thigh at the fracture site itself; these are made into one single splint for the shin bone also. After that, stuff soft cloths between the thigh and shin so as to achieve an even tension.
First, start with a strong pull from the center towards the fracture site with three or four wraps; as you move further away from the fracture site with your binding, make your pulls less intense and more gentle and relaxed.
Then take the ends of the thread that you had passed between the thigh and the leg, use them to tie together what follows from above on the splints; then pass the ends of the thread downwards until you reach the ankle, where you will also tighten both ends of the splint from the other side.
So that the bandage does not shift from its place, you should then leave it tight until there is no longer any pain or swelling in the limb nor any other sign of inflammation; but if anything like this occurs, then immediately loosen and re-tie it as needed to correct whatever occurred according to what we have already explained several times.
If there is a piece of bone protruding and piercing, you should try to smooth it over if possible; otherwise make an incision on top of it and remove the fragment, then treat the wound in the manner described previously until it heals. It may be possible to bandage this fracture without having to add splints to the leg as well.
It can be bandaged in the manner described previously for the arm and forearm, although bandaging it thus does not necessarily result in a limp; however, if you were to splint only this bone without adding splints for the leg as well, then there is no doubt that the patient will always have a limp.
Note that the thigh bone typically heals in fifty days or a little more, and sometimes less; all of this depends on variations in temperament as well as other factors at play.
Chapter 15.
[On fracture of the knee-pan.]
The knee-pan is rarely subject to fracture and may more often be bruised, but if it does become fractured, this will either take the form of a fissure or fragmentation of its component parts; this occurs both with and without injury.
You will perceive all of this through sensation, and set it by aligning the dispersed component parts with your fingers until they are joined together and aligned as well as you can manage given consideration for gentleness and precision; then apply a dressing and, should it be necessary, a round splint.
You will then bind it tightly above the corresponding ligature, after which you should closely monitor for all conditions described previously in relation to other fractures such as hot swelling and so forth; treating each symptom accordingly until healing is complete.
Chapter Sixteen.
[On setting the fracture of the leg.]
The leg comprises two bones, one thick and known as the tibia, the other thin and called the fibula; they are subject to fractures of a similar nature to those affecting the humerus and radius. For this reason, its setting is akin to that of the arm and involves similar procedures; if both bones are fractured, the leg will turn in all directions, but if only the thinner bone is affected it will turn forward; should the thicker one be fractured below it may present complications which need not be outlined here.
Apply extension and alignment, then bind the splints accordingly; however, should it be a severe fracture with many fragments, you will need to exercise less force in extending it and proceed more gently during the setting of the bone.
In the treatment of the leg, there is an additional procedure compared to that for the arm: Once you have adjusted the splints and finished all your work, take two flat splints made from pine wood, which are used to level room floors between the cracks in boards, or they can be made from palm leaves or similar.
Choose those that are slightly thick and not too thin, with a length equal to the leg from knee to ankle. Then wrap each one in two pieces of cloth along its length, placing one on the side of the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let them extend from the knee to below the foot. Then tie the two splints at three points, at both ends and in the middle. With these bindings, the leg will be prevented from leaning either right or left and it will be properly aligned. Alternatively, a gutter-like wooden device along the length of the leg may also be used.
This is placed to prevent movement, and it is especially necessary in cases of fracture with accompanying wound. Then examine the leg each day, taking great care regarding swelling or puffiness, as well as other matters. If any such issue arises, address it appropriately until the problem is resolved. The leg bone will heal in around thirty days.
Chapter Seventeen.
On the breaking of leg bones and fingers.
The heel is never broken, but the leg bones can be fractured. Fingers are rarely broken as well; however, they are more often bruised.
If the leg bones are broken and you see that they have shifted against each other, let the patient place their foot on the ground as if standing upright while walking. Then stand up and put your own foot on top of the raised bone fragment and tread it down until they are level.
In order to return it back into its proper place, then take the bandages and splint from above and put a small board underneath the sole of the foot which has two flat ends; tighten them around the foot after wrapping it with rags and doing whatever else is required.
After three or four days, loosen the bandage and you will find that the bones are level whether they were broken or dislocated; perform as seems fit to you. And if some fingers are dislocated, manipulate them into place and align them as I have described for setting fingers of the hand.
Then make a splint for the broken finger running along its length and make it slightly wider than the finger; place under the foot this board that I described to you earlier, securing it tightly in place. If two or three bones are fractured then even more so.
Apply a splint of appropriate size to each broken finger and wrap it in soft cloth; tighten the board underneath the foot, ensuring that each end of the board extends slightly beyond the sole so as to achieve good alignment.
You should not forget to monitor all the symptoms we have mentioned regarding fractures and treat each symptom as required.
Chapter 18.
On the fracture of the woman's pubic bone and the man's penis.
When a woman's vagina is broken and she can only sit in a squatting position, then bend her slightly towards the back. Have someone hold her from behind, then have the midwife pack her vagina with cotton until it fills up completely and forms a ball-like shape inside her.
Then shake the woman and lift her hips slightly, and that cotton will come out until it reaches the opening of her vagina in a ball-like shape and the bone fracture is healed. Then place a bandage on her back, and whenever she wants to urinate, gently remove the cotton so that she can urinate.
Return it to its original position, then go back to the first bandage you applied. Repeat this process for around seven days and the fracture will heal. Alternatively, if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, tie one end of a hollow reed tube to it, and insert the entire bladder into her vagina.
Blow hard into the tube until the bladder inflates inside her vagina, and this will set the fracture. Then pack it with cotton and keep applying treatments for a few days as described earlier to heal the fracture. If the pubic bone of either men or women is broken, use this method.
Treat and stabilize it as described for the hip bone fracture earlier, you should be able to figure out the correct treatment easily for these rare fractures especially if one has some skill and understands my book well - indeed because most of the time the fracture itself will guide your treatment.
For a man's broken thigh, take the windpipe of a goose and insert his penis into it, then wrap it with a cloth bandage and bind it up. Leave it like this for around three days until healed.
Chapter 19.
In the setting of bone fractures with concomitant wounds.
One who presents with a fracture and a wound, especially if the bone is large such as the femur or humerus, should be bled immediately if the conditions for bloodletting are met, as we have explained previously.
If the wound is bleeding, one should quickly cauterize it by applying powdered green vitriol; if that is not available, then one should proceed with setting the fracture on the same day and not delay.
If there is no hot swelling, but if a warm swelling occurs, then one should delay setting the fracture until the ninth day, when the hot swelling has subsided; and one should not approach it on the third or fourth days at all, as it will cause bad symptoms.
If the broken bone is protruding through the skin and exposed, then one should try to reposition it gently with their hands; if this cannot be achieved by hand, then one should use an instrument made of iron that is seven or eight fingers in length for the purpose. Its width should be equal to the wound.
For this reason, it is necessary for the physician to have three or four of these instruments at hand, as required by the type of fracture being treated; they should be round with a slight thickness so that they do not bend when pressure is applied during use.
The instrument should have a sharp point with a hook at its end, and it should be thicker towards the top and much thinner from the middle to the bottom; this is its illustration:.
It is called 'perim' in Greek, meaning a small lever; the sharp, hooked end should be placed on the protruding bone and it should be pushed with one swift motion. Once the bone has been moved back into position to some degree of evenness, use the perim to level out the fracture ends.
If the broken end is thin and cannot be grasped firmly by the instrument, then you should cut off the end of that bone so that the instrument can grasp it properly. If you are still unable to reposition the bone as described, then cut it with an instrument similar in shape to those mentioned previously.
Or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible; then remove any remaining roughness or thin scales from the bone. If, after repositioning the bone, the patient still experiences severe pain, understand that the bone has not returned to its natural position.
If you are able to reposition the bone back to its natural place, then do so as it will greatly benefit the patient. Once the setting of the bone is complete, dip a cloth in black astringent wine, especially if it's summer; and avoid applying pitch or anything oily on the wound.
To prevent putrefaction and corruption, then apply splints after you have set the bone; leave the wound uncovered by cutting a hole in the bandages with scissors to fit the size of the wound.
Be extremely cautious and avoid tightening the bandages over the fracture at all costs, as ignorant physicians often do this, which can lead to death, gangrene, or a severe cold in their patients. Ensure your binding is loose and relaxed, contrary to the usual tight binding for fractures.
If the wound is bad or if it's a large one, and you're concerned about some of the adverse symptoms we've described, and if they are experiencing distressing pain in that area, then it would be inappropriate to apply splints here; instead, make bandages from sturdy cloths for use at those points where splints would have been applied, securing them tightly.
So, if after a day or two you see that the wound has begun to discharge pus, then remove from it the cloth you put on with ointment; and use instead our usual treatments for wounds involving poultices and ointments such as the 'four-ingredient' ointment and its likes; you should also release the bandage.
And check the wound each day in both evening and morning until it heals; you should elevate that member so as to let pus drain from it easily downward; if, after several days have passed since the wounding, there is still no healing or stopping of discharge then know there are small bone fragments present.
You should therefore explore the wound with a probe; whatever of those fragments is exposed, extract it and remove it; as for any that are not so exposed but instead press into that member causing pain, then saw them in order to cut them away and extract them by every means possible.
If the wound should exhibit cold or gangrene or some other form of corruption and putrefaction, then you should apply to each condition whatever treatment best suits its nature from among those previously described in their respective sections.
And one more thing you should bear in mind when I say it: if a large bone is broken and sticks out from that member, like the thigh or upper-arm bones and their likes among the larger members, then don't attempt to pull it back nor extract it. Many times death occurs from that, so leave it until it becomes gangrenous; perhaps it will fall off by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then you treat the wound if you see a place for treatment, otherwise leave it.
Chapter Twenty.
Treatment of complications that occur after some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the healing of a fracture, especially near joints, resulting in disfigurement and potentially hindering the natural function of the affected limb.
If the complication is soft, apply medicines with astringent properties such as aloe, frankincense, myrrh, galbanum, and acacia, mixed with a tonic drink or egg whites, or vinegar.
Cover the complication with a compress, applying firm pressure and leaving it on for many days without loosening. Repeat this process several times until the complication resolves, or use a lead plate as a tight bandage.
Lead has properties that help heal various complications in limbs. If the complication has become hard and stubborn, and removal is necessary, make an incision from above to remove any protruding excess, or smooth it with a suitable tool until gone. Treat the resulting wound until healed.
Chapter Twenty-One.
Treatment of fractures after healing, addressing limbs that remain weak compared to their original state.
If a broken bone heals but the limb remains thin and weak afterward, this is due to many causes. One cause is either the frequent loosening and retightening of bandages in an improper manner, or excessive tightness of the bandages preventing nourishment from reaching the limb, or excessive and frequent application of plasters, or immoderate movements made at the wrong time, or a lack of blood and weakness in the patient's body.
To treat this, one should feed the patient and enrich their body until there is more blood within it, as well as using a bath, making them happy and joyful, and so forth. Then, apply pitch to the limb to draw nourishment to it, or keep applying plasters with warm water. Until nourishment flows within it and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter Twenty-Two.
On treating broken bones if they heal crookedly, preventing proper movement.
Whenever there is a limb that has been healed after breaking, but it heals crooked or bulges with the broken bone complex and ugly. Unless the limb is prevented from its natural function, it is not necessary to accept the claim of those who say that one should break the limb at the head; many ignorant physicians and bonesetters did this in our country.
This action is highly blameworthy, leading to great danger, the least of which is damage; but if the crookedness and complexity are fresh, one should apply plasters with water in which relaxing herbs have been boiled, such as marshmallow leaves, roots, crown imperial, and so forth.
It is dressed with relaxing dressings such as carefully made diachylon, or saliva from the root of marshmallow is taken and mixed with chicken fat and sesame oil, and it is dressed [with this]; or fatty figs are taken, pounded with pigeon droppings and similar medicines that are called anti-healing agents.
The contracture might be resolved by constant, gentle rubbing with the hands and movement of the member in all directions at all times; if the curvature has become old, severe, and hard, then it becomes necessary to treat it with iron.
It is necessary to make an incision above [the penis], separate the bone connection, and cut away any remaining contracture or bone with small scissors, taking care to be gentle yet firm; then treat the wound as mentioned earlier until it heals.
Chapter 23.
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its position, causing hindrance to movement, deformation of the limb, and severe pain for the patient; therefore, whenever a person experiences a dislocation, it should be immediately reduced.
It should not be delayed at all; if it is, the area will become swollen and inflamed, making reduction of the dislocation difficult. Therefore, it must not be postponed or moved during this time, as it often leads to spasm and severe pain for the patient.
However, if this occurs, it is necessary to immediately perform venesection on the patient and leave them until the swelling subsides somewhat; then the limb should be loosened with hot water and oil before being reduced gently. Each limb will be treated as described in its respective section.
I have also arranged the chapters on jaw fractures according to what has been previously presented regarding bone fractures, from the top of the body down to the bottom.
Chapter 24.
In treating the lower jaw.
Fractures of both jaws are rare; when they do occur, the dislocation can be of one of two types: either a slight dislocation from their normal positions with some relaxation, or a complete dislocation resulting in full relaxation. Until the patient's saliva flows towards their chest, and they are unable to hold it in or close their mouth, and their tongue becomes impeded while speaking; if the dislocation is slight then in most cases it will correct itself with minimal intervention.
If the dislocation is complete, you must act quickly and not delay in correcting it; this involves having an assistant hold the patient's head while the physician inserts their thumb into the origin of the jaw inside the mouth if only one side is affected.
Alternatively, if both sides are affected, the physician should insert both thumbs and use their other fingers externally to apply pressure, instructing the patient to relax and move their jaw in all directions while the doctor gently moves and pushes the jaw back into its proper position.
If it is difficult to reposition the jaw, especially if both jaws are affected, apply hot compresses and oil to facilitate the process as described earlier; do not delay in doing so. Once the jaws have been realigned and the mouth can close without relaxation, then apply cloth pads with a cerate made of wax and rose oil.
Then it is gently tied with a loose bandage, and the patient sleeps on his back with his head resting between two pillows so that he does not move to either side, nor need chew anything; but let his food be soft broth.
When the pain is gone and the jaw has healed, let him eat whatever he likes; let him do so gently and not force himself to open his mouth too wide when eating or drinking, nor yawning until the jaw has healed completely.
If it is difficult to restore the jaws when they become dislocated at some point and do not return to their places, often this leads to fevers and constant headaches; perhaps the patient's stomach becomes upset or perhaps he vomits several times without anything coming out. If you see these symptoms, know that the jaw has suffered irreparable damage.
Often such patients die within ten days.
Chapter 25.
Regarding the repositioning of the clavicle and shoulder joint.
The clavicle rarely dislocates towards the inner side due to its connection with the chest; it may, however, dislocate outwards and this can be felt by touch. To treat it, let the patient lie on his back and stretch out both arms.
Then press the affected area with your palm firmly until it returns to its place; then bind it and bandage it tightly. As for the end of the clavicle that is close to, and articulates with, the shoulder, it rarely dislocates but if it does at some point you must replace it as we mentioned before and as you see fit.
Then you apply the dressing, bandages, and compression, and instruct the patient to rest and remain still until they recover. With this exact treatment, you can also reset a dislocated shoulder.
Chapter 26.
In resetting the shoulder joint.
Know that the shoulder can dislocate in three ways: first, it can dislocate towards the armpit and down; second, it can dislocate towards the chest; and third, it can also dislocate upwards past the shoulder, which is rare.
It does not dislocate to the back of the shoulder blade or forward towards the neck, but mostly it dislocates and comes out downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with less muscle mass, as it pops out quickly and can be reduced just as fast.
As for those with more muscular build, it is the opposite; that is to say, the dislocation comes out with difficulty and goes back in with difficulty as well. Perhaps some people may suffer a blow or fall, causing the shoulder to become hot and swollen, which may be mistaken for a dislocation. It is necessary to examine this carefully until you are certain.
At that point, you should begin treatment. To identify a dislocation downwards towards the armpit, compare it to the healthy shoulder; you will find an obvious difference and notice the head of the humerus is not in its socket. When touching under the armpit, you will feel the head of the bone like an egg.
The patient will not be able to lift their arm up to their ear or move it in all directions. Similarly, if the dislocation is towards the chest or upwards, these differences are apparent upon examination and cannot be missed.
This jaw can be easily reset if it is soft or the patient is a child, and the procedure is as follows: have an assistant lift their hand above the patient's head; then place your thumbs under the patient's armpits and lift the joint forcefully upward to its correct position, while the assistant lifts and extends their arm upwards.
Then lower it back down and it will quickly return to its place. If this does not work, and the jaw has been dislocated for many days, the patient should be bathed in hot water using a concoction of herbs that relaxes and softens, such as marshmallow root, fenugreek, and rosemary, which have been boiled together.
The patient should then lie on their back with a woolen ball placed under their armpit, of moderate firmness. The physician places their heel on the ball and grasps the patient's hand in their own, extending it downward.
The physician should then press down on the ball with their foot, and the jaw will return to its place. Alternatively, the procedure can be performed with an assistant of greater height standing alongside the patient; the assistant places their shoulder under the patient's armpit and lifts them upward until they are suspended in midair. Another assistant should pull the patient's hand down toward their lower abdomen. If the patient is light, something else should be added to weigh them down. The jaw will return instantly; this procedure can also be performed in another manner.
An alternative method involves inserting a long pole into the ground, with its head rounded like the end of a mortar, neither too thick nor too thin; place a soft cloth on top of this and position the patient's armpit over it while they stand perpendicular to the length of the pole.
Then extend his hand downwards from the other side and also stretch his body forcefully from the opposite side, then the joint will quickly return to its place. If it is difficult to put it back with all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a plank about two arms' lengths long. Its width should be about four fingers, its thickness two fingers; let it have a rounded head so that it can easily be inserted into the armpit. It will look like this: [image].
Then tie soft rags around the rounded head so that the plank does not hurt the patient; place it under the armpit and stretch out the whole arm with its hand downwards on top of the plank, and then tie the latter to the upper arm, forearm, and tip of the hand.
The arm is then placed on a crossbar of the ladder horizontally, and the hand stretched downwards while leaving the rest of his body suspended from the other side; this way the joint will quickly re-enter its socket. Once it has entered in any manner possible, one must place under the armpit a wool ball of moderate size.
After this, you must apply the dressing prepared from flour dust with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder as if it were being rotated; then pull down on the ball forcefully from under the armpit, wind a bandage tightly around the dressing above, and tie his hand to his neck.
He must not move his arm for seven days, and during this time the patient should receive little nourishment so that the limb becomes stronger; which is quicker in healing it. After five or seven days you may untie him and start exercising him gradually through movement. If the joint does not become lax again he will have healed.
If the joint dislocates repeatedly due to moisture or another ailment, cauterization with three pieces of metal should be employed as previously discussed in the section on cauterization. If you perform all this and loosen the bandage after seven days yet the joint remains unstable,
I rebandaged and tightened it several times, but it kept slipping out of place, becoming limp and flaccid. You should know that the nerve in the shoulder joint has either snapped or stretched too far, meaning that this joint will never stay put.
For the jaw that lies towards the chest and breasts, one should push it back into place with both hands until it is repositioned properly; afterward, apply any other treatment necessary for healing. If there's later pain in the affected area or sluggish movement...
The patient should go to the bathhouse many times until that stiffness eases up, returning to its original state.
Chapter 27.
Treatment of the elbow joint.
The elbow dislocates with difficulty, and it's similarly difficult to get back into place too; it can pop out in all directions but especially forward or backward. Its dislocation is obvious because you can see and feel the joint, so there's no mistaking what shape it's gotten itself into.
When you compare the dislocated elbow to its healthy partner, it's obvious that something is wrong due to the depressed joint surface. The arm cannot bend and will not touch the shoulder. One must act quickly to reduce this dislocation before swelling sets in.
If it should present a hot and hard swelling, it may be difficult to cure, and perhaps never heal at all, especially if the jaw is displaced backwards; for this is the worst of all kinds of dislocation, being the most painful and deadly.
Immobilizing it, if it is possible to reposition, the servant extends his hand with both hands and arm stretched out; the physician's hands are above and below the elbow, pushing the joint with both thumbs or the base of their palm until it returns to its place.
If the jaw is dislocated forward, it may be repositioned by bending the hand in one swift motion so as to strike with the base of their palm against the shoulder that aligns with it; if this does not cause the jaw to return then employ a strong and vigorous pulling action which requires two servants to extend the arm.
The patient is held by two servants to prevent movement during the pull, then the arm is rotated in all directions after wrapping a folded cloth or wide bandage around their hand; prior to handling the joint with his hands, the physician greases them so as to facilitate smooth movement of the joint.
The joint is then pushed back into place with force, and after repositioning it should be bandaged firmly with a compress that applies pressure and drying agents along with egg whites; the arm is suspended from the patient's neck for several days.
The bandage is then removed, and if the joint remains stable in its position the compress can be unbound; however, should the joint not feel firm enough it must be re-bandaged with more pressure and left again for several days until it does feel firm at which point the binding may be unfastened. Once this has been done, palpate the area around the joint to ensure there is no pain or swelling present post-repositioning and unbinding.
If there is difficulty in movement, use moistening treatment in the bath and gentle massage with friction until it becomes supple. Alternatively, place a leg of a well-fed sheep on the joint, then bind it and leave it there for a day and night. Then remove it and take him into the bath; if he perspires, rub the joint moderately.
Then return the leg again, once and twice and thrice, with entries into the bath until it softens. If you wish to put on him moist cow manure lightly with butter, tie it onto him; do this several times and it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter 28
On the treatment of wrist dislocation.
The wrist often dislocates, and its reduction is easy compared to other joints; however, it should be quickly reduced when the dislocation occurs before any swelling or hot inflammation develops around the joint.
Reduction of the dislocation is done by placing the patient's wrist on a board, extending the assistant's arm, and applying force with the palm of the hand over the joint's prominence to push it back in place; however, one must ensure that the reduction did not occur towards the palmar side.
If the dislocation is towards the dorsal side, the patient should place their palm on the board during extension and reduction; if it's towards the palmar aspect, they must place the back of their hand against the board so that the physician's hand can be placed over the same prominence to achieve reduction. If immediate reduction occurs, nothing further is needed; otherwise, apply a compressive dressing to reduce swelling.
Leave it alone and do not attempt reduction again as this joint cannot tolerate such manipulations, especially after a few days have passed since the dislocation occurred; however, leaving it reduced improperly will cause an angular deformity of the joint without much harm to the patient unless the hand becomes flaccid and unable to grasp objects.
When you know the nerve has been severed or badly bruised, there is no remedy except to treat it with cauterization; this may help and sometimes does not work at all. Once the wrist joint has been set then apply a dressing as described above, then bandage firmly and leave for five days.
Then remove the bandage and exercise the hand; if movement is not possible and there's any sign of stiffness, then ease it by applying warm water and massage repeatedly until it becomes supple.
Chapter 29.
On the treatment of dislocated fingers.
Fingers may dislocate in any direction. If a finger dislocates towards the back of the hand or palm, straighten it and push the dislocation with your thumb until it returns to its normal position; then tie up the end of the finger and bandage it so that it hangs towards the side where it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release and straighten the finger until it is completely upright on that day; if it's night time bandage as before but leave off during the daytime to exercise and train movement of the finger and re-bandage at night continuing this process each day until it regains its strength.
And so you do the same if it dislocates towards the palm of the hand, bandaging in the direction of that side and treat as before until cured; likewise for any other direction of dislocation.
Chapter 30.
On the treatment of dislocated vertebrae.
When a vertebra from the back or neck is completely dislocated, or when many vertebrae are displaced from their proper places, there is no treatment for it because death quickly overtakes the patient.
A sign of this is that the patient's stool comes out involuntarily, and they cannot control it; often some of their limbs become limp: either their legs or arms, or just one of them. If a single vertebra becomes displaced from its proper place, it can usually be put back into position.
The dislocation can occur in four directions: if the vertebra moves backward, it is called a hunchback; however, there is no treatment for this and complete recovery cannot be expected. If the hunchback has occurred due to a fall or blow of some kind, then...
Early writers have described various treatments for this, in long texts that are not of much use; I have selected the most useful parts and simplified them to make my meaning clear. The instrument used is different from what they describe.
If the hunchback is caused by a forward curvature of the chest, there's no remedy and it can't be cured; the same goes for those curved to either side. The only treatable type is that which curves backward in the upper back region; this can be treated with the method I am about to describe: lay the patient face down...
on a flat bench, near to a wall; place some soft, damp material underneath them so as not to hurt their chest. Next, insert into the ground at one end of the bench a vertical wooden post and another at the other end, towards their feet.
A servant then holds the first post, ensuring that it is not too firmly fixed into the ground; another servant holds the other post in a similar manner. Some soft yet strong bandages are then wrapped around the patient's chest and under their armpits, and one end of this cloth is attached to the post by their head and tied there securely.
Then you should tighten another bandage over his hips and above his knees, as well as at his ankles; then gather all the ligatures together, tying them to the other board which is placed near his feet. Then each servant should pull towards their side of the board using the ligaments, without shifting either of the boards from where they have been positioned.
But they are not documented as we said, and the doctor places his palms firmly on the bead until it returns to its original place or he puts a board upon it then leans onto the board with both feet until it goes back into place; if this treatment does not work, take another piece of wood that is about three arms-length long.
You should carve a space into the wall, as we said it must be near the patient's bed, in which you place one end of the board; then put the middle of the plank on the hump and have the doctor place his feet at the other end while he pulls firmly so that pressure is exerted upon the bead.
And it will return to its place; if you wish, you can instead use the screw which is turned by hand; this means embedding in the ground near the patient's head at one end of the bed two planks that are an arm-length long each and then another pair of similar length at his feet on the other side of the bed.
And there should be a space of one span between each board, with holes drilled into every board through which the screw will pass; all these boards must then be fastened tightly to the ground so that they cannot move at all and you insert a cylindrical stick – this is the screw around which you wind the rope – inside the holes in both boards.
At one end there is a hole in which you fasten a rod about one span long by twisting; the two other boards are made likewise, then you should tighten the ligature that was wrapped around the patient's chest to the screw near his head and the bandages that were tied around his legs to the screw located at his feet.
Then, at each coil, a servant stops and twists his hand with the tool that is used to twist the coil, while the physician smooths out the hump as we have described. This is an illustration of the coil, the mallet, and the patient.
Once the vertebrae are realigned and the area is even, you should apply a drying bandage with egg whites, followed by a dressing. Then place above the bandage a splint made of a board that is three fingers wide or so, and its length should cover the hump site.
Over it, place some smooth beads and secure with the appropriate bandage. The patient should consume a light diet until healed. If there remains some protrusion in the area after healing is complete, one must employ remedies that relax and soften while continuing to use the board as described for an extended period.
A lead plate may be used for this purpose. Occasionally, a protrusion may appear at the end of the spinal vertebrae, leading to the assumption of dislocation. However, this could be an additional bone that has protruded, and one must not subject it to such treatment, as death might occur.
Chapter Thirty-One.
On the treatment of dislocated hip.
Be aware that the hip and shoulder joints are susceptible to dislocation but not to minor displacement or concavity, which can affect other joints. The hip joint can become dislocated in four ways.
This is due to the hip joint dislocating inward, outward, forward, or backward. It most commonly dislocates inward and rarely forward or backward. A sign of inward dislocation is when you pair the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, resulting in an apparent length difference.
The knee is more prominent than usual, and the patient cannot bend their leg at the groin; the area around the groin becomes noticeably swollen because the head of the femur has moved there.
The signs for someone with a dislocated jaw towards the outside are opposite to these symptoms, and for those with a forward dislocation, their leg will be fully extended but cannot bend without pain in the knee; if they try to walk, they cannot do so forward.
Their urine is retained, and their groin becomes swollen; when walking, they step on their heels. The signs for a dislocated jaw towards the back are an inability to straighten or bend the knee without first bending the groin, and their leg is also shorter than the other.
The groin is relaxed, and the head of the femur is clearly at the hip joint. As for treating the various types of dislocated jaw, if it is an old injury that has been with the patient for a long time, and no attempt to treat it was made before now, then there is no hope for treatment.
You should not attempt to treat it, but for a recent dislocation, regardless of type or direction, you should start by twisting the joint and stretching it inwards and outwards while moving it both ways; perhaps...
It might return to its place and require no further treatment; if not, you should prepare a strong servant who will pull on the leg from below with either their hands or a bandage tied above the knee, while another servant pulls upwards.
By inserting their hands under the patient's armpits and then pulling with a soft bandage tied around the top of the thigh; another servant holds the other end of the bandage, and they pull either from the front towards the shoulder joint or from behind towards the spine.
He should pull them all at once until the patient's body is lifted from the ground and remains suspended, for this type of traction is common to the four types; if the jaw returns as we have said, otherwise each type must necessarily undergo the specific treatment I mention.
As for its specific treatment if the jaw is pulled inward, one should lay the patient on their right side and then place a bandage on the origin of the thigh between the head of the femur and the spot just under the groin.
Then the ligament is pulled upward from the groin area towards the upper body in the direction of the clavicle, then another strong servant takes hold with both arms and embraces the thick part of the patient's thigh, pulling it outward forcefully until it returns to its proper place.
This type is easier than the other methods used to treat this condition, but if it proves impossible and the patient refuses to undergo this treatment in any way, then one should tie both of their legs together with a strong yet soft bandage around the heels and at the knees.
The distance between each leg and its companion should be about four finger-widths, with the affected leg extended further than the other by two fingers; then the patient is suspended from a wooden beam in the house at a height of about two arms' length above the ground.
Then you instruct a strong boy to embrace the head of the thigh, and another boy hangs onto the patient while pushing against the one embracing the thigh with force so that the joint returns quickly to its place; as for treating it specifically when the jaw is pulled outward...
The patient should lie on the operating table as we described for the hunchback, and a bandage should be tied around their ailing leg in particular, and also across their chest. Then place one stick at their feet and another at their head. Finally, place an additional stick firmly in the middle of the table.
Wet cloths should be wrapped around [the sticks] so that they do not hurt the patient, and the stick is placed between their thighs to prevent them from being pulled down when stretched. Then each servant pulls in their direction while the physician aligns the hip with their hands; if it resists returning,
Otherwise, place a board on top and apply pressure as mentioned in the hunchback treatment. However, the patient should lie on their right side instead. If dislocation is forward, you must extend their leg at once while they are in this same position on the table.
The physician places the palm of their right hand on the patient's groin and pushes it with their other hand, applying pressure which stretches downward toward the knee. If dislocation is rearward, the patient should not be extended downwards.
They should be elevated off the ground, resting on a solid object instead; their dislocated hip must protrude outwards as described when lying face-down on the table with bindings tightened.
As mentioned earlier, pressure with a board should also be applied to the location where the joint has popped out of place; once the hip joint returns as it ought and its restoration is evident—you know this when extending the patient's legs...
If you see them aligned and the patient can flex his leg and extend it without difficulty, then know that the limb has returned to its proper state. At this point, bring together the thighs and apply the dressing, securing it firmly with a bandage so that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient must remain at rest for three or four days, after which you should loosen the bandages and compare the leg to the other one; if they are of equal length then know that the fracture has healed properly and allow the patient to walk. If there is still some relaxation in it.
If not, return and re-dress it, tightening the bandage as before. Leave it for three more days then loosen it again; let him walk on it slowly for several days until it gains strength, God willing.
Chapter 32 (or Chapter XXXIII)
On the treatment of knee dislocation.
The knee dislocates in three ways: inward, outward, and downward (or backward). It never dislocates forward. To detect a dislocation, ask the patient to bring his leg up against the thigh.
If the leg does not attach to the thigh, then know that the knee is dislocated. To set all types of dislocation: have the patient sit with their legs extended (if they can manage it) and have a servant sit behind them, holding their waist and slightly bending them backward; you should then sit on their thighs.
Rest your back against their face and place their leg between yours; secure their knee with both hands, interlinking your fingers over it, then press the sides of the knee firmly with your palms; meanwhile another servant should extend his leg to help return the knee to its proper position.
A sign of the reduction is that the leg adheres to the thigh in a relaxed manner without force, then bandage it and attach the leg to the thigh, securing them both with a bandage for three or four days. Afterward, release [the patient], but do not allow them to walk much for several days until they are strengthened.
If you find it difficult to reduce the dislocation as we have described, then use strong traction with bandages which I will describe later in my treatment of hip dislocations until reduction is achieved.
Chapter 33.
Treatment of dislocation of the calcaneus.
The calcaneus may become slightly dislocated or completely dislocated, and this can occur either inwardly or outwardly. The sign of a complete dislocation is the swelling of the calcaneus which becomes prominent in the direction it has dislocated to.
Treatment of its slight dislocation is simple and it can easily be reduced by gentle traction with the hands until it is realigned, but if completely dislocated then: the patient should sit upright and a strong servant [or assistant] should stand behind them, supporting their back at waist level.
You then grasp their foot with your right hand at the top and with your left hand underneath, near the Achilles tendon [calcaneal tendon]. Pull twice on the foot towards you using both hands, then pull again with your left hand while pushing the sole of the foot upwards with your right hand. This should be done without force.
Repeat this procedure two times as described, then in the third [time] push the sole of their foot towards their leg while pulling on the Achilles tendon; if the calcaneus is reduced after one or two repetitions and you see that the foot is aligned, but if not repeat the above process until reduction occurs.
If it is impossible for you to reverse it as described, and otherwise, place the patient on their back on the ground, firmly embed a stake in the ground between their thighs, with cloth wrapped around it so as not to hurt the patient, then have an assistant hold their thigh.
Then another assistant should stretch the leg either with their hands or by tying it to the man's neck, and then each assistant stretches in the opposite direction of his partner while the stake remains upright between the patient's thighs for support so that the body is not pulled downwards during stretching, finally the physician realigns the jaw using both hands.
Another assistant holds the healthy leg downwards, and when the jaw is quickly restored, if it returns to a correct position then take the bandage and splint and secure them with tight bindings, tie the foot below with another binding, you must be mindful of the nerve that lies above the heel from behind.
So as the binding is not too tight and does not harm it, then leave him for two or three days, if the bandage loosens then tighten it again before releasing it on the third or fourth day, and the patient should avoid walking for forty days.
If the patient tries to walk before this time period has passed, you cannot guarantee that the jaw will not dislocate again and become ruined beyond repair; if swelling or inflammation occurs then you must use what we have previously described elsewhere in the treatment of swellings until it subsides.
Chapter 34.
Treatment of the fingers of the foot.
The remaining portion should be gently and smoothly leveled without any force, which is not difficult but quite easy. If it is the jaw that is affected then the patient should place their foot on a flat surface on the ground.
Or on a plank while standing as if walking, then you should stand and place your foot on the protruding joint and press it firmly with your foot until it returns to its proper position and you can see that it has flattened without any remaining bump.
Then place under the sole of their foot a board that fits the entire foot and has two ends, then you should tie it tightly with a strong binding for three days before releasing it. You must prevent them from walking for many more days until it is strengthened and you are certain there will be no relapse, if God wills.
Chapter thirty-five.
On the types of dislocation that occur with an injury, or with a fracture, or both together.
Whenever any of these happen, it is best to treat the injury and set the fracture immediately because they often result in death.
Therefore, only the most skilled and experienced practitioner should attempt to treat such a case with great care and caution without any haste or recklessness. At first, they must apply remedies that ease hot swellings alone before proceeding further.
It is up to God's will, unless you believe the patient has a chance at recovery and relief from further injury then it should be your utmost priority to cure them swiftly before any hot swelling occurs.
If the joint returns to its proper position, then treat any hot swellings and care for the injury with suitable drying ointments. If there is a fracture of the jaw where fragments of bone have become detached then they must be removed. Follow that which we have mentioned about simple diseases in the preceding chapters on their respective locations.
Strive to avoid venturing into uncertain paths, as I have advised you before; that is better for your reputation and safer for your well-being, God willing.
